# Philosophy Textbook Atlas

## Table 1 · Overview, Reference Works & Philosophical Method

*Scope:* Serious entry points to the discipline (distinct from popular philosophy), the major reference works, methodological texts on philosophical writing and argument, philosophy's relation to neighbouring disciplines, and the meta-question of how to read the history of philosophy. Level tags: `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Anthology]`.

### 1.1 What Is Philosophy? — Serious Introductions

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` General introduction `[Analytic]` | *The Problems of Philosophy*, 1912 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Bertrand Russell** | Russell's hundred-page tour of the classical problems — the existence of matter, knowledge by acquaintance vs. description, universals, induction, a priori knowledge. The most successful brief introduction to analytic philosophy ever written, in the sense that it states real problems with real arguments rather than narrating them. Its phenomenalist commitments (sense-data, the inference to a physical world) are dated and were largely abandoned by mid-century analytic philosophy, but the chapters on universals and induction remain live. The prose is a model of philosophical writing. Limitation: gives no sense of the linguistic, modal, and naturalistic turns that define philosophy since 1950. | Any reader with a serious appetite, no prior philosophy required. Best read as a first contact with what doing philosophy actually looks like, then supplemented by something contemporary (Blackburn, Nagel). See also SEP: "Bertrand Russell" for orientation to his broader corpus. |
| `[Systematic]` General introduction `[Analytic]` | *What Does It All Mean? A Very Short Introduction to Philosophy*, 1987 `[Introductory]` | **Thomas Nagel** | Nine compressed chapters on the central problems: external world, other minds, mind–body, free will, right and wrong, justice, death, meaning of life. Nagel writes with rare clarity and intellectual honesty — he raises problems without pretending to resolve them, which is what philosophical introduction should do. The chapter on the meaning of life is itself a small classic. Limitation: deliberately brief; the reader gets the shape of problems but not the architecture of positions. | Complete beginners, particularly those skeptical that philosophy is more than opinion. Read in an afternoon; then either continue with Blackburn's *Think* for depth or move to a specific subfield. |
| `[Systematic]` General introduction `[Analytic]` | *Think: A Compelling Introduction to Philosophy*, 1999 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Simon Blackburn** | A substantial introduction (~300 pages) covering knowledge, mind, free will, the self, God, reasoning, the world, what to do. More argumentative and demanding than Nagel; Blackburn reconstructs the dialectic of each problem rather than gesturing at it. The free will and self chapters are particularly strong. Blackburn's own metaethical quasi-realism colours the ethics treatment — readers should know this. The best single-volume self-study introduction currently available. | Motivated readers prepared to think along with arguments rather than skim summaries. Ideal as preparation for subfield study; pairs well with a logic primer (Restall or Smith — see Table 5). |
| `[Reference]` Historical orientation `[Bridge]` | *A New History of Western Philosophy*, 4 vols., 2004–2007 (one-vol. ed. 2010) `[Introductory]` `[Reference]` | **Anthony Kenny** | The current best single-author history of Western philosophy: Volume I (Ancient), II (Medieval), III (Early Modern to Hegel), IV (Modern to the late 20th century). Kenny is a distinguished analytic philosopher and historian of medieval philosophy; the medieval volume is exceptional, the modern volume serviceable. Each volume divides chronological narrative from thematic chapters on individual problems (knowledge, mind, ethics) — this dual structure is its main innovation. Honestly limited: Kenny's analytic training shows in his comparative impatience with Hegel, Heidegger, and the post-Kantian Continental tradition, which he treats more briefly and less sympathetically than he treats the medievals or analytic philosophy. Replaces Copleston's nine-volume *History* for most purposes, while Copleston remains useful for the Scholastic period. | General readers, students orienting before deep study, scholars wanting a reliable single source on a period outside their specialty. Use as scaffolding to which primary texts (Tables 2–4, 9) are added; do not substitute it for them. |
| `[Systematic]` General introduction `[Continental]` `[Bridge]` | *What Is Ancient Philosophy?*, 1995 `[Introductory]` `[Bridge]` | **Pierre Hadot**; trans. **Michael Chase** (Harvard, 2002) | Hadot's argument that ancient philosophy was primarily a *way of life* — a set of spiritual exercises aimed at transforming the practitioner — and only secondarily a body of theoretical doctrines. The book has reshaped how the Hellenistic schools (Stoicism, Epicureanism, Scepticism) are read and influenced Foucault's late work on ethics of the self. As a definition of "what philosophy is," it is a counterweight to the analytic conception of philosophy as argument and analysis. Limitation: programmatic, with the historical evidence sometimes pressed to fit the thesis; readers should consult Cooper's *Pursuits of Wisdom* (Table 2) for a more cautious treatment of the same terrain. **Recommended translation:** Chase (Harvard, 2002) — the only English translation and competent. | Readers who suspect that "philosophy" means more than analytic problem-solving; students of ancient philosophy as a bridge from the analytic textbook approach to the historical-existential approach. See also SEP: "Ancient Ethical Theory" and Hadot's *Philosophy as a Way of Life* in §1.5 below. |

### 1.2 Major Reference Works — Encyclopedias, Companions, Dictionaries

**Meta-resource note.** The single most important orientation tool in academic philosophy is the **Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy** (`plato.stanford.edu`) — a dynamically updated, peer-reviewed, free, professional reference work edited at Stanford CSLI. Entries are written by leading specialists, signed, dated, and revised on a published schedule. Citation in academic philosophy is universal. Where an SEP article exists on a topic, it is almost always the right starting point for orientation before consulting books. Quality is variable across thousands of entries — some are definitive (e.g. "Kant's Transcendental Arguments," "Modal Logic," "Phenomenology") while others are merely competent — but the worst SEP article is generally better than the best Wikipedia article on the same topic. The SEP is not given an atlas row of its own because it is a journal-style reference rather than a book, but every table that follows assumes the reader has it open.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Reference]` Encyclopedic reference | *Routledge Encyclopedia of Philosophy*, 10 vols., 1998 (online updates ongoing) `[Reference]` | **Edward Craig** (general editor) | The most comprehensive single reference work in philosophy, with roughly 2,000 signed articles by specialist contributors. Stronger than SEP for coverage of non-Western philosophy (Indian, Chinese, Islamic, African traditions all have substantial entries), historical figures of secondary importance, and topics outside the core analytic canon. Less consistently maintained: many print-era articles have not been substantively revised since 1998, and the online edition's update schedule is slower and less transparent than SEP's. The single-volume *Concise Routledge Encyclopedia* (2000) is a useful shorter version, derived from the same content. | Reference shelf or institutional library access. The first place to look for serious non-Western or minor-figure entries; supplement with SEP for analytic and continental topics that have been actively updated. |
| `[Reference]` Single-volume reference | *The Cambridge Dictionary of Philosophy*, 3rd ed., 2015 `[Reference]` | **Robert Audi** (general editor) | The best one-volume philosophy dictionary in English: roughly 5,000 entries from short term-definitions to substantial essays on major figures and topics. Especially strong on analytic philosophy of language, mind, and epistemology — Audi's own background. Used by professional philosophers as a desk reference for terminology outside their specialty. The Oxford alternative (*Oxford Companion to Philosophy*, Honderich, 2nd ed. 2005) is broader and more cosmopolitan in tone but less analytically precise; the choice between them is largely a matter of which terminological idiom one prefers. | Anyone reading philosophy regularly. Worth owning physically for the looking-up of unfamiliar terms while reading primary texts. |
| `[Reference]` Subject-specific companions `[Anthology]` | *Cambridge Companions to Philosophy* series, 1990–present `[Reference]` `[Advanced]` | Various editors | The Cambridge Companion volumes are commissioned essay collections (12–20 chapters) on individual philosophers, schools, or major topics. Quality varies more than the series uniformity suggests, and learners should select rather than collect. The genuinely outstanding volumes — those that have become standard secondary reference for their topic — include: **Kant** (ed. Paul Guyer, 1992; 2nd ed. 2006), **Hume** (ed. David Norton, 1993; 2nd ed. 2008), **Plato** (ed. Richard Kraut, 1992), **Aristotle** (ed. Jonathan Barnes, 1995), **Hegel** (ed. Frederick Beiser, 1993), **Heidegger** (ed. Charles Guignon, 1993; 2nd ed. 2006), **Wittgenstein** (eds. Hans Sluga and David Stern, 1996; 2nd ed. 2017), **Frege** (ed. Tom Ricketts and Michael Potter, 2010), and **Husserl** (eds. Barry Smith and David Woodruff Smith, 1995). Weaker volumes do exist — the early companions on topics rather than figures are uneven — and inclusion in the series is no guarantee of quality. | Graduate students and researchers approaching a figure or topic. Read the named outstanding volumes alongside the primary texts in the relevant atlas table; consult others selectively after checking the editor and the table of contents. The companions are not a substitute for the primary texts they accompany. |
| `[Reference]` Subject-specific handbooks `[Anthology]` | *Oxford Handbooks in Philosophy* series, 2003–present `[Reference]` `[Research]` | Various editors | Larger, more research-oriented than the Cambridge Companions: typical Oxford Handbooks run 30–50 chapters and 700–1,200 pages, aimed at advanced graduate students and researchers rather than first-year graduate readers. The handbooks on systematic topics are often more useful than those on individual figures (where the Cambridge Companions are better organised). The standout volumes include **Metaethics** (eds. Copp, 2006; superseded in part by Hubin et al.), **Free Will** (ed. Kane, 2nd ed. 2011), **Philosophy of Mind** (eds. McLaughlin, Beckermann, Walter, 2009), **Philosophical Methodology** (eds. Cappelen, Gendler, Hawthorne, 2016), and **Causation** (eds. Beebee, Hitchcock, Menzies, 2009). Weak handbooks exist in the series and inclusion is no quality guarantee. | Researchers entering a subfield. Read the relevant handbook's introduction and 5–10 most relevant chapters before tackling primary monographs; the chapters function as state-of-the-field reviews with bibliographies. |
| `[Reference]` Encyclopedic survey `[Analytic]` | *The Oxford Handbook of Analytical Philosophy*, 2005, and *A Companion to Analytic Philosophy* (Blackwell), 2001 `[Reference]` | **Frank Jackson & Michael Smith** (Oxford); **A.P. Martinich & David Sosa** (Blackwell) | Two reasonable single-volume overviews of analytic philosophy as a whole, one organised by topic (Jackson–Smith), the other by figures (Martinich–Sosa). Both are useful as scaffolding before reading primary texts in the analytic tradition; neither is essential if one has Cambridge Companions and SEP. The Blackwell volume's figure-by-figure organisation makes it more useful as a study guide for the history of analytic philosophy than as a current-state reference. Hans-Johann Glock's *What Is Analytic Philosophy?* (Cambridge, 2008) is a sharper meta-treatment of the same question — what is the analytic tradition, and is it still a coherent label? — and is preferable to either of these as a single book to read on the topic. | Graduate students orienting in the analytic tradition; researchers from continental or non-Western backgrounds wanting a map. See also SEP: "Analytic Philosophy." |

### 1.3 Philosophical Writing, Argument Reconstruction & Method

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Writing & argument craft | *Philosophical Writing: An Introduction*, 4th ed., 2015 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **A. P. Martinich** | The standard manual on how to write a philosophy paper in the analytic tradition: thesis formulation, argument reconstruction, charitable interpretation, structure, citation, common errors. Brief (~200 pages) and practical, with worked examples. Limitation: addresses the conventions of analytic philosophy specifically; continental writing has different and largely unwritten norms (one learns them by reading Heidegger, Derrida, Adorno, and their commentators). Nothing comparable exists for continental writing, which is itself a methodological symptom. | Undergraduates and beginning graduate students writing analytic philosophy papers. Worth owning before the first term paper; revisit annually. |
| `[Systematic]` Argument fundamentals | *A Rulebook for Arguments*, 5th ed., 2018 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Anthony Weston** | A 100-page guide to the construction and evaluation of arguments — generalisations, analogies, causal arguments, deductive forms, extended arguments, fallacies. Not a logic textbook; rather, the practical complement to one. Useful precisely because it stays general; it is not committed to particular formal systems and so applies to philosophical, scientific, and ordinary reasoning equally. Limitation: nothing here will be new to anyone who has worked through an introductory logic text; its value is for readers who have not. | High school upward, anyone improving their reasoning. Read before or alongside Martinich; afterwards move to formal logic (Smith, Restall — Table 5). |
| `[Reference]` Methodology of philosophy `[Anthology]` `[Analytic]` | *The Oxford Handbook of Philosophical Methodology*, 2016 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Herman Cappelen, Tamar Szabó Gendler & John Hawthorne** (eds.) | The current research-level reference on philosophical method: 40 chapters covering intuitions and thought experiments, conceptual analysis, experimental philosophy, formal methods, naturalism, the history of philosophy as method, ordinary language philosophy, and metaphilosophy. Maps the post-2000 explosion of metaphilosophical work — particularly the experimental-philosophy critique of intuition-based methodology and its rebuttals. Limitation: heavily analytic in orientation; continental methodology (hermeneutic, dialectical, deconstructive) appears chiefly through chapters by analytically-trained commentators. | Graduate students and researchers engaged with metaphilosophical questions. Not for first reading on what philosophy is; read after one has done philosophy and started wondering whether it has a method. |
| `[Research]` Philosophical method `[Analytic]` | *The Philosophy of Philosophy*, 2007 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Timothy Williamson** | A sustained defence of armchair philosophical methodology against the naturalist and experimentalist critiques: Williamson argues that philosophical questions are substantive questions about reality (not merely conceptual questions about our concepts), that thought experiments deliver real evidence, and that the analytic/synthetic distinction does not bear the weight the critics place on it. The book is also a major statement of Williamson's broader epistemology (knowledge-first, against the JTB tradition). Limitation: technically demanding and assumes substantial background; the polemical edge is sharp and the engagement with experimental philosophy was already being overtaken by developments in that field at publication. | Graduate students and researchers in epistemology, metaphysics, or philosophy of language with prior background. Read after working through some Quine ("Two Dogmas of Empiricism," "Epistemology Naturalized") and one introduction to experimental philosophy. See also SEP: "Experimental Philosophy" and "Philosophical Methodology." |
| `[Primary]` Foundational essay `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` | "Philosophy and the Scientific Image of Man" (in *Science, Perception and Reality*, 1963) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Wilfrid Sellars** | The locus classicus of one influential view of what philosophy is: the discipline whose task is to bring the *manifest image* of human beings (the world as it appears to ordinary self-understanding — persons, intentions, colours, values) into stereoscopic relation with the *scientific image* (the world as described by physical science — particles, fields, neural processes). Few essays of comparable length have done more to shape analytic philosophy's self-understanding; the manifest/scientific image distinction is now common currency. Limitation: programmatic rather than complete; the harder work of actually carrying out the synthesis Sellars proposes has been the work of analytic philosophy since. The companion essay "Empiricism and the Philosophy of Mind" (in the same volume) is more difficult but equally important — see Table 7. | Graduate students in any analytic subfield. Read in tandem with Quine's "Two Dogmas" and Sellars's "Empiricism and the Philosophy of Mind" — the three essays together define mid-century analytic philosophy's methodological self-image. See also SEP: "Wilfrid Sellars." |

### 1.4 Philosophy and Its Relation to Science, Mathematics & Literature

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Philosophy as humanism `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` | *Philosophy as a Humanistic Discipline*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Bernard Williams**; ed. **A. W. Moore** | A posthumous collection of Williams's late essays defending philosophy's character as a humanistic — historically-informed, literarily attuned, ethically engaged — discipline against scientistic conceptions. The title essay is among the most acute statements available of why philosophy is not and should not become continuous with cognitive science. Williams's prose is itself a demonstration of the thesis. Limitation: a collection rather than a treatise; the cumulative argument must be assembled by the reader from essays written for different occasions. Williams's *Descartes: The Project of Pure Enquiry* (Table 3) is the worked example of the methodology defended here. | Graduate students and researchers, especially those uncertain whether to defect to neighbouring fields. Pair with Sellars's "Scientific Image" essay above — they articulate opposing conceptions of what philosophy is for, and the comparison is more illuminating than either taken alone. |
| `[Systematic]` Philosophy & literature `[Bridge]` | *Love's Knowledge: Essays on Philosophy and Literature*, 1990 `[Advanced]` | **Martha C. Nussbaum** | The most sustained recent argument that certain ethical understandings can be enacted and communicated by literary form in ways that no philosophical treatise can match, with particular attention to Henry James, Beckett, and the Greek tragedians. Nussbaum draws on her work in ancient ethics (especially Aristotle and the Stoics) and on her engagement with Iris Murdoch. The essay "Form and Content, Philosophy and Literature" is the methodological core. Limitation: Nussbaum's specific literary judgments and her cognitivist account of emotion are contestable; the strength of the case for philosophy–literature continuity does not depend on accepting them. | Readers in literature, ethics, or aesthetics; serious readers who suspect that ethics-as-axiology misses something. Pairs with Murdoch's *Sovereignty of Good* (below) as the two essential texts on the topic; see also Cora Diamond's *The Realistic Spirit* for the Wittgensteinian counterpart. |
| `[Primary]` Ethics & literature `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` | *The Sovereignty of Good*, 1970 `[Introductory]` `[Evergreen]` | **Iris Murdoch** | Three short essays — "The Idea of Perfection," "On 'God' and 'Good'," and the title piece — arguing for a Platonic, attention-based ethics against the volitional ethics dominant in Oxford in the 1960s. Murdoch contends that moral progress consists in *just attention* to particulars rather than in choice between options, and that art and morality share the same essential discipline of unselfing. Brief (~100 pages) and intensely written; readable in one sitting but rewards multiple returns. Limitation: programmatic rather than systematic — Murdoch's view of the Good is sketched, not argued through; her novels and her late *Metaphysics as a Guide to Morals* (1992) carry the larger picture. The book's influence on contemporary virtue ethics (Foot, McDowell, Hursthouse) and on moral particularism (Dancy) is substantial. | Anyone serious about ethics who is willing to be unsettled. Read alongside Anscombe's "Modern Moral Philosophy" (Table 8) — both are landmark critiques of mid-century Oxford ethics from different directions. See also SEP: "Iris Murdoch." |
| `[Systematic]` Philosophy, mathematics & literature `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` | *The Realistic Spirit: Wittgenstein, Philosophy, and the Mind*, 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Anthology]` | **Cora Diamond** | Essays connecting Wittgenstein's later philosophy to ethics, mathematics, and literature in a single methodological vision. Diamond is co-architect (with James Conant) of the "resolute reading" of the *Tractatus*, which holds that the book's apparently metaphysical claims are themselves to be thrown away as nonsense — a controversial reading sharply contested by Hacker, Pears, and others. The essays "The Importance of Being Human," "Anything but Argument?," and "Wittgenstein and Metaphysics" demonstrate what philosophy looks like when it takes seriously Wittgenstein's claim that philosophical problems arise from misunderstandings of our forms of expression. Bridges philosophy of mathematics, ethics, and literary criticism more successfully than any comparable single volume. Limitation: Diamond's prose is patient and demanding; the resolute reading commitments are methodologically heavy and not always argued in detail within these essays. | Advanced readers with some Wittgenstein background and interest in any of the three areas Diamond brings together. Read after at least one careful pass through the *Tractatus* and *Philosophical Investigations*. See also SEP: "Ludwig Wittgenstein." |

### 1.5 Historiography of Philosophy — How to Read the History of Philosophy

The methodological choice between *rational reconstruction* (engaging past arguments as if they were contemporary contributions) and *historical reconstruction* (situating thought in its historical context and tradition) is itself a substantive philosophical decision. Each approach captures something the other misses: rational reconstruction tests whether the arguments still work; historical reconstruction shows what the arguments were arguments *for* and *against*. Most actual work mixes the two, but the mixture is not neutral. This subsection presents the methodological landscape and exemplars of each approach.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Anthology]` Methodology of history of philosophy `[Bridge]` | *Philosophy in History: Essays on the Historiography of Philosophy*, 1984 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Richard Rorty, J. B. Schneewind & Quentin Skinner** (eds.) | The methodological landmark for the historiography of philosophy. Rorty's opening essay distinguishes four genres of writing on past philosophy — *rational reconstruction* (engaging past philosophers as contemporaries), *historical reconstruction* (recovering what they meant in their own context), *Geistesgeschichte* (canonical narrative of the field's self-understanding), and *doxography* (catalogue) — each with different aims, criteria of success, and characteristic pathologies. Contributors include Skinner (on intellectual history method, drawing on his Cambridge School work on Hobbes and Machiavelli), Charles Taylor, Alasdair MacIntyre, Lorraine Daston, and others. Limitation: the four-fold taxonomy is contested — much actual history of philosophy mixes genres, and the rational/historical opposition is sharper in theory than in practice. The Bennett and Hadot entries below represent the two poles whose tension this volume maps. | Anyone writing history of philosophy or wondering what kind of object the history of philosophy is. Read Rorty's essay before any of Tables 2–4 or 9; the other essays consult as needed. See also SEP: "Methodology in the History of Philosophy." |
| `[Primary]` Historiography & traditions of inquiry `[Bridge]` | *Three Rival Versions of Moral Enquiry: Encyclopaedia, Genealogy, and Tradition*, 1990 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alasdair MacIntyre** | MacIntyre's Gifford Lectures, presenting three rival traditions of moral inquiry: the *encyclopaedic* (Enlightenment, exemplified by the 9th edition of *Encyclopaedia Britannica*, with its confidence in tradition-neutral rationality), the *genealogical* (Nietzsche, Foucault — every claim to universal reason is unmasked as the contingent expression of power), and the *tradition-constituted* (Thomistic Aristotelianism, which MacIntyre defends). Each tradition has its own internal standards of rationality, and inter-traditional adjudication can only proceed by translating between them and observing which can solve more of the other's problems. The argument completes the trilogy begun in *After Virtue* (1981) and *Whose Justice? Which Rationality?* (1988). Limitation: MacIntyre's commitment to the Thomistic tradition is explicit, and his portrait of the "encyclopaedic" tradition is selectively constructed; readers should engage critically with both the constructive case and the historical characterisations. | Advanced readers in ethics, history of philosophy, or theology. Read *After Virtue* first; the full force of the historiographical argument only registers against the background of the earlier critique of contemporary moral discourse. See also Table 8 §8.4 (virtue ethics). |
| `[Secondary]` Rational reconstruction in practice `[Analytic]` | *Learning from Six Philosophers: Descartes, Spinoza, Leibniz, Locke, Berkeley, Hume*, 2 vols., 2001 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Jonathan Bennett** | The most ambitious modern defence-in-practice of the rational reconstruction approach to history of philosophy, applied across the early modern canon. Bennett openly states his interest in the arguments these philosophers made *insofar as the arguments still work or fail by current standards*, and his commentary reconstructs and evaluates rather than contextualises. The result is among the most genuinely useful analytic commentaries on the early modern period — and also a methodological provocation. Limitation: critics (notably Hampshire, Catherine Wilson, and many continental readers) have argued that Bennett's approach distorts the philosophers it treats, especially Spinoza, where Bennett's reconstructed argument substitutes substantially for Spinoza's own. This complaint is sometimes fair and sometimes not; pairing Bennett with more contextually-sensitive commentaries (Curley on Spinoza, Garber on Descartes — Table 3) is the right strategy. | Graduate students in early modern philosophy. Cross-referenced extensively in Table 3 entries; read after the primary texts rather than as a substitute for them. The two-volume set is also available as freely downloadable text from Bennett's *Early Modern Texts* website (where Bennett provides his own modernized translations of the original works — useful but lossy; the entry-specific translations recommended in Table 3 should be preferred for serious study). |
| `[Primary]` Historical-contextual reading `[Continental]` `[Anthology]` | *Philosophy as a Way of Life: Spiritual Exercises from Socrates to Foucault*, 1995 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Pierre Hadot**; ed. **Arnold I. Davidson**; trans. **Michael Chase** (Blackwell, 1995) | Essays by Hadot arguing that ancient philosophy must be read as a set of *spiritual exercises* and *ways of life*, not as a system of theoretical doctrines abstractable from their lived context. The essays "Spiritual Exercises" and "Forms of Life and Forms of Discourse in Ancient Philosophy" are the methodological core; the closing essay traces the influence of Hadot's reading on Foucault's late ethics of the self. The collection is the indispensable counterweight to the Bennett-style rational reconstruction approach above: where Bennett asks whether ancient arguments still work, Hadot asks what ancient arguments *were* — what they were doing in the lives of those who made them. Limitation: the application of the "ways of life" framework to post-ancient philosophy is uncertain, and Hadot himself doubted it transposed straightforwardly to the modern period; readers should treat the methodology as a corrective rather than a universal key. **Recommended translation:** Chase (Blackwell, 1995) — competent and the only complete English version of Davidson's selection. | Readers in ancient philosophy and anyone interested in the historicity of philosophical practice. Read against Bennett (above) for the methodological contrast; see Hadot's *What Is Ancient Philosophy?* in §1.1 for the systematic development. Foundational for Foucault's later work — see Table 9 §9.8. |

### Connections to other tables

The translation issues flagged in this table's framing are concretised throughout the historical tables: Plato and Aristotle in Table 2, Descartes through Hume in Table 3, Kant and Hegel in Table 4, Husserl and Heidegger in Table 9. Sellars's manifest/scientific image essay (§1.3) is foundational for the philosophy of mind discussions in Table 7 and connects to the Physics Atlas's treatments of explanation, reduction, and the relation between physical theory and ordinary experience. Murdoch and Nussbaum (§1.4) prepare the ground for virtue ethics in §8.4 and the moral psychology themes in §7.5 and §8.6. The historiographical alternatives presented in §1.5 — Bennett's rational reconstruction against Hadot's historical-contextual reading — are choices the reader will face concretely in every subsequent historical table; the methodological self-awareness this subsection encourages should travel forward. The Cambridge Companions and Oxford Handbooks identified as outstanding in §1.2 will be cross-referenced as secondary literature throughout Tables 2–9 where their specific volumes are particularly strong.

# Table 2 · Ancient & Medieval Philosophy

*Scope:* Pre-Socratic cosmology through Aristotle, the Hellenistic schools (Stoic, Epicurean, Sceptic), Neoplatonism, Latin Christian philosophy from Augustine through Aquinas, late medieval Scholasticism through Ockham, and the philosophy of the medieval Islamic world. The translation choice is a substantive philosophical commitment throughout this table.

### 2.1 Pre-Socratics — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Pre-Socratic fragments anthology `[Reference]` | *The Presocratic Philosophers: A Critical History with a Selection of Texts*, 2nd ed., 1983 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. S. Kirk, J. E. Raven & Malcolm Schofield** ("KRS") | The standard English-language presentation of the pre-Socratic fragments: Greek text with facing English translation, embedded in a chapter-by-chapter critical narrative of pre-Socratic thought from Thales to the early atomists. KRS established the scholarly format — fragment + testimonium + commentary — that anyone working on early Greek philosophy in English uses. The 2nd edition (Schofield's revision) updates the Heraclitus and Parmenides chapters substantially. Limitation: the selection reflects mid-century interpretive choices and underweights some figures (notably the medical writers and Hippocratic texts as philosophical sources); for fuller coverage consult Graham (below). **Recommended translation:** the KRS translations themselves are conservative and reliable; Greek text included for those who read it. | Anyone working on pre-Socratic philosophy. Read the narrative chapters for orientation, then return to specific fragments. Pair with Barnes (below) for the analytic-reconstructive reading. See also SEP: "Presocratic Philosophy." |
| `[Primary]` Pre-Socratic fragments anthology `[Reference]` | *The Texts of Early Greek Philosophy: The Complete Fragments and Selected Testimonies of the Major Presocratics*, 2 vols., 2010 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Daniel W. Graham** (ed. & trans.) | The fullest English-language anthology: Greek text with facing translation, organised by philosopher, with substantial introductions. Graham presents more material than KRS and updates the textual scholarship; his presentation of the Milesians and of Heraclitus is particularly thorough. Limitation: Graham's interpretations are interventionist (notably his reading of Anaxagoras as the originator of the elements doctrine), and the commentary is less critical than KRS's; the two works complement rather than replace each other. The Loeb Classical Library's nine-volume *Early Greek Philosophy* edition by **Laks & Most** (2016) is now the most complete scholarly resource — Greek and English facing, with the most current text — and is the new reference standard, though its size and price make KRS or Graham more practical for most readers. | Researchers in pre-Socratic philosophy. KRS remains the better learning text; Graham and Laks–Most are consulted for completeness on specific figures or fragments. |
| `[Secondary]` Pre-Socratic philosophy — analytic reconstruction `[Analytic]` | *The Presocratic Philosophers*, 2nd ed., 1982 (one-vol. ed.) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jonathan Barnes** | The most ambitious analytic reconstruction of pre-Socratic arguments in English: Barnes reads Parmenides, Zeno, Anaxagoras, and the atomists as serious philosophical arguers and reconstructs their reasoning in something close to contemporary form. The treatment of Parmenides' deductive argument and of Zeno's paradoxes is exemplary of the rational-reconstruction approach (see §1.5). Limitation: Barnes is unapologetic about reading anachronistically — he wants the arguments to work or fail by current standards — and historians of philosophy in the contextual mode (McKirahan, Most) have argued that this method distorts the pre-Socratic project. The complaint is partly fair, but Barnes's reconstructions remain the sharpest available test of which pre-Socratic arguments actually succeed. | Graduate students with some prior pre-Socratic exposure; analytically-trained philosophers approaching ancient material for the first time. Read KRS first for the textual and historical ground; then Barnes for the argumentative reconstruction. |
| `[Secondary]` Historiography of pre-Socratic studies `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *The Concept of Presocratic Philosophy: Its Origin, Development, and Significance*, 2018 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **André Laks**; trans. **Glenn W. Most** | The meta-historical question: how did "Presocratic philosophy" become a category, and what does its construction tell us about the modern reception of ancient thought? Laks traces the concept from its 19th-century origins (Eduard Zeller and the German tradition) to its current usage, and argues that the category is more retrospective and constructed than its standard use suggests. Brief (~150 pages), important for situating the pre-Socratic material critically rather than naively. The book is a companion to the Laks–Most Loeb edition cited above. **Recommended translation:** Most (Princeton, 2018) — translated by the same scholar who co-edited the primary texts, so terminology is consistent. | Graduate students who have already read the pre-Socratics and want to think about how they are being read. Not a first book on the topic. |

### 2.2 Plato — Primary Texts

**Translation orientation.** John M. Cooper's *Plato: Complete Works* (Hackett, 1997) is now the scholarly standard in English: it collects (mostly) the best individual Hackett translations under one cover, with Cooper and Hutchinson's editorial framework. Jowett's Victorian translation (1871) is fluent and historically influential but interpretively dated — Jowett's Plato is a 19th-century English idealist, not a 4th-century BC Athenian. The Loeb editions (Greek + English facing) by Fowler, Lamb, Bury and others are old but useful where Greek is needed. For individual dialogues, the best translation often differs from the one included in Cooper, and serious work on a single dialogue should consult the alternatives. The Hackett individual volumes (small paperbacks with substantial introductions) are the workhorses of undergraduate teaching.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Plato — complete works `[Reference]` | *Plato: Complete Works*, 1997 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Plato**; ed. **John M. Cooper** & **D. S. Hutchinson** (Hackett) | The current standard English edition of Plato's complete corpus in one volume (~1,800 pp.): every dialogue plus the *Letters* and the dubious works, with brief editorial introductions and an authoritative essay by Cooper on Plato's chronology and corpus. Most translations are taken from the Hackett individual editions and are uniformly competent; a few (notably *Sophist*/*Statesman* by White, and Cooper's own *Theaetetus*) are revisions or new translations made for this edition. **Recommended translation:** Cooper (Hackett, 1997) as default for any dialogue not separately studied. For individual dialogues with stronger alternatives, see entries below. Limitation: in a one-volume format the translator notes and apparatus are compressed; for any dialogue studied in depth, the standalone Hackett or Cambridge edition is preferable. | Anyone working seriously on Plato; the version to own. Functions both as desk reference for the corpus and as a single source for the dialogues not addressed in dedicated entries below. See also SEP: "Plato." |
| `[Primary]` Plato — politics, ethics, metaphysics, epistemology | *Republic*, c. 375 BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Plato**; trans. **G. M. A. Grube**, rev. **C. D. C. Reeve** (Hackett, 1992) | The longest and most synoptic Platonic dialogue: ten books moving from the question "what is justice?" through the construction of an ideal *polis*, the tripartite soul, the theory of the Forms (including the Sun, Line, and Cave images), the philosopher-ruler, the critique of mimetic poetry, and the myth of Er. The *Republic* alone has set the agenda for Western political philosophy, philosophy of education, philosophy of art, and a substantial part of metaphysics. Its central claims are sufficiently contestable that it has remained productive across 2,400 years of disagreement. Limitation: the unity of the dialogue's argument is genuinely disputed — Book I has the look of an early Socratic *elenchos* whose conclusions Books II–X effectively replace. **Recommended translation:** Grube–Reeve (Hackett, 1992) for accessibility with scholarly accuracy; **Tom Griffith** (Cambridge, 2000) for greater literary care; **Allan Bloom** (Basic Books, 1968) for the most literal translation, but with a Straussian interpretive frame that the reader must keep in view. Avoid Jowett. | Read in full by every philosophy student. Annas's *Introduction to Plato's Republic* (§2.3) is the indispensable secondary companion. See also SEP: "Plato's Ethics and Politics in The Republic" and the *Cambridge Companion to Plato's Republic* (Ferrari, ed., 2007). |
| `[Primary]` Plato — soul, immortality, metaphysics | *Phaedo*, c. 380 BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Plato**; trans. **G. M. A. Grube** (Hackett, 1977) or **David Sedley & Alex Long** (Cambridge, 2010) | The dialogue dramatising Socrates' last day and presenting four arguments for the soul's immortality, alongside the most developed presentation in the middle dialogues of the theory of Forms as causes (the "second sailing" passage, 95e–102a). The "second sailing" is among the most analysed passages in Plato; the arguments for immortality are mostly considered failed but the metaphysical context they supply is essential. The dialogue is also a literary masterpiece — its closing scene is the founding image of philosophical death. Limitation: the relation between the Phaedonian Forms and the Forms of the *Republic* and the *Parmenides* is debated; the *Phaedo* is not self-sufficient as a metaphysics. **Recommended translation:** Sedley–Long (Cambridge, 2010) for the most accurate and philosophically scrupulous version, with an authoritative introduction; Grube (Hackett, 1977) for accessibility and inclusion in Cooper. | Read after the *Apology* and *Crito* for the dramatic arc; read alongside *Republic* V–VII for the metaphysics. Burnyeat and Frede have written extensively on the second sailing. |
| `[Primary]` Plato — epistemology | *Theaetetus*, c. 369 BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Plato**; trans. **M. J. Levett**, rev. **Myles Burnyeat** (Hackett, 1990) | Plato's sustained examination of *what is knowledge?* — and the dialogue that has come to define epistemology as a discipline. Three definitions are proposed and refuted: knowledge as perception (with a long examination of Protagoras' relativism and the Heraclitean flux doctrine), knowledge as true belief, and knowledge as true belief with an account (*logos*). The dialogue ends in *aporia*, and reading the negative result is itself a philosophical exercise — does the *Theaetetus* refute the JTB analysis it is sometimes thought to propose, or does it merely show that *logos* must be specified differently? Limitation: the relation of the *Theaetetus* to the *Sophist* and *Statesman* (which follow dramatically) is debated and bears on whether Plato has a positive theory of knowledge in the late period. **Recommended translation:** Levett rev. Burnyeat (Hackett, 1990) — Burnyeat's revision is meticulous and his accompanying introduction (~250 pp., longer than the dialogue) is itself among the major works on Plato's epistemology. | Graduate students in epistemology and ancient philosophy. Read the Burnyeat introduction as a secondary text in its own right — see also §2.3. The *Sophist* (also late Plato) addresses the related metaphysical problems of being and non-being. |
| `[Primary]` Plato — cosmology, philosophy of nature `[Bridge]` | *Timaeus*, c. 360 BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Plato**; trans. **Donald J. Zeyl** (Hackett, 2000) | Plato's cosmological dialogue: the construction of the cosmos by the *Demiurge*, the doctrine of the four elements as regular solids (the "Platonic solids"), the receptacle of becoming, the world-soul, and the doctrine of likely accounts (*eikos logos*) appropriate to a changing physical world. Through Cicero's partial Latin translation, the *Timaeus* was the only Platonic dialogue continuously available in the Latin Middle Ages and so shaped Western cosmology and natural philosophy for over a millennium — until displaced by Aristotelian and then mechanistic natural philosophies. Limitation: the *Timaeus*'s status within Plato's corpus is uncertain (the late chronology is contested), and the relation between its mythical mode and the dialectical argumentation of other dialogues is interpretively delicate. **Recommended translation:** Zeyl (Hackett, 2000) — clear, scrupulous, with a substantial introduction; **F. M. Cornford**'s *Plato's Cosmology* (1937) remains valuable for its translation-with-commentary format, though the translation itself is now dated. | Readers in ancient philosophy, philosophy of science, or history of cosmology. Connect to Physics Atlas treatments of early cosmology; see also SEP: "Plato's Timaeus." |

### 2.3 Plato — Secondary Literature & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Secondary]` Plato — Socratic philosophy `[Analytic]` | *Socrates: Ironist and Moral Philosopher*, 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Gregory Vlastos** | The landmark mid-20th-century reconstruction of Socrates' philosophical method and ethical doctrines, separating "Socrates" (the figure of the early dialogues) from "Plato" (the metaphysician of the middle dialogues) on developmental grounds. Vlastos's analysis of the *elenchos*, of Socratic eudaimonism, of complex irony, and of the priority of definition over instance have set the terms of subsequent Plato scholarship — even where his developmentalist framework is rejected (notably by unitarians like Kahn). Limitation: the sharp Socrates/Plato distinction is now contested by a substantial scholarly faction, and Vlastos's reading is shaped by his preference for moral over metaphysical Plato. | Graduate students in ancient philosophy; the starting point for serious engagement with the early dialogues. Pair with Cornford's older works for a contrasting interpretive style. See also SEP: "Socrates." |
| `[Secondary]` Plato — *Republic* commentary `[Analytic]` | *An Introduction to Plato's Republic*, 1981 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Julia Annas** | Still the best single-volume philosophical commentary on the *Republic* in English. Annas works through the dialogue book by book, reconstructing the argument and identifying where it succeeds, where it equivocates, and where its conclusions outrun its premises. She is unsparing on the weaknesses (the analogy between soul and city; the argument from injustice's self-defeating character) without losing sight of the dialogue's philosophical seriousness. Limitation: written before the surge of literary-political readings (Ferrari, Frede) and somewhat austere in its analytic focus; the dialogue's dramatic structure and political-rhetorical dimensions receive less attention than its arguments. | Read alongside the *Republic* itself, ideally book by book. Required for any serious undergraduate or graduate engagement. |
| `[Secondary]` Plato — *Theaetetus* commentary `[Research]` `[Analytic]` | *The Theaetetus of Plato*, 1990 (introduction to the Levett translation) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Myles Burnyeat** | A 250-page introduction-and-commentary that is, in effect, a monograph on the *Theaetetus*. Burnyeat reconstructs the dialogue's logical structure, defends what he calls Reading A against Reading B (the two main interpretive options on the dialogue's negative conclusion), and connects the *Theaetetus* to ancient and modern epistemology. The treatment of the Protagorean section, of the Heraclitean flux, and of the *logos*-condition for knowledge are model exercises in close reading combined with philosophical reconstruction. Limitation: Burnyeat's confidence that there is a determinate answer to interpretive questions the dialogue itself leaves open will not satisfy every reader; some scholars (notably Sedley) read the dialogue as pursuing knowledge as recognition rather than as articulation. | Graduate students in epistemology or Plato. A model of what philosophical commentary can be when it operates at full scholarly stretch. |
| `[Reference]` Plato — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Plato*, 1992 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Richard Kraut** (ed.) | One of the outstanding Cambridge Companion volumes: 14 essays by leading Plato scholars covering the corpus, individual dialogues, and major themes (Forms, ethics, politics, knowledge, philosophy of language, the soul, religion). Kraut's introduction is itself a substantial orientation to Platonic methodology and chronology. Limitation: now thirty years old, with the more recent literature on developmentalism, on Plato's late metaphysics, and on the *Parmenides* underrepresented. The *Oxford Handbook of Plato* (Fine, ed., 2nd ed. 2019) is more current and more comprehensive but less consistently outstanding chapter-by-chapter. | Graduate students orienting in Plato studies. Read Kraut's introduction first; then individual chapters paired with the corresponding dialogues. |

### 2.4 Aristotle — Primary Texts

**Translation orientation.** The Revised Oxford Translation edited by **Jonathan Barnes** (Princeton, 2 vols., 1984) — known as the *Complete Works* — collects revised versions of the Oxford Translation chiefly produced by W. D. Ross and his collaborators in the early 20th century. It is the scholarly default in English. For individual works of central importance, however, more recent specialised translations are preferable: the Clarendon Aristotle series (each volume a different translator + commentator) is the gold standard for serious study of single works, and several Hackett volumes (Irwin on the *Nicomachean Ethics*; Reeve on the *Metaphysics* and *Politics*) are now scholarly references in their own right. The Loeb editions (Greek + English) remain useful for those reading the Greek.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Aristotle — complete works `[Reference]` | *The Complete Works of Aristotle: The Revised Oxford Translation*, 2 vols., 1984 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Aristotle**; ed. **Jonathan Barnes** (Princeton) | The standard English edition of the Aristotelian corpus: nearly all the surviving works (treatises, dialogues fragments, and the *Constitution of Athens*), in revisions of the early-20th-century Oxford Translation. The revision is conservative; the Rossian and Ackrillian style is consistent enough that the corpus reads as a unity. Includes the *Posterior Analytics* (in Barnes's own translation, the basis of his Clarendon edition), the *Physics* (Hardie–Gaye, dated but serviceable), the *Metaphysics* (Ross, classic), the *De Anima* (J. A. Smith, now superseded by Shields), and the *Politics* (Jowett rev. Barnes). **Recommended translation:** the Barnes edition is the default; for any work studied in depth, use the alternatives listed in entries below. Limitation: heavily revised in some places (the *Posterior Analytics*), barely revised in others (the *Eudemian Ethics*); the apparatus is minimal compared with the Clarendon series. | Anyone working seriously on Aristotle. The library reference for any work not separately studied. See also SEP: "Aristotle." |
| `[Primary]` Aristotle — ethics, virtue, eudaimonia | *Nicomachean Ethics*, 4th c. BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Aristotle**; trans. **Terence Irwin** (Hackett, 2nd ed. 1999) or **Christopher Rowe** with commentary by **Sarah Broadie** (Oxford, 2002) | The most influential work in the history of ethics: ten books on the human good (*eudaimonia*), virtue of character, voluntary action and choice, particular virtues (courage, temperance, justice), virtue of intellect (*phronesis*, *sophia*), incontinence (*akrasia*), friendship, and pleasure. The *NE* originates a tradition (virtue ethics) that has had two great periods — its ancient and medieval reception, and its contemporary revival since Anscombe (1958). The treatment of *phronesis* and of practical reasoning is the dialogue's most enduring philosophical contribution. Limitation: the relation between Book VI's intellectual virtues and Book X's contemplative ideal is famously difficult to reconcile (the "dominant vs. inclusive" debate over *eudaimonia*); the relation to the *Eudemian Ethics* (which shares three books with the *NE*) is similarly unresolved. **Recommended translation:** Irwin (Hackett, 2nd ed. 1999) for self-study, with substantial glossary and notes; Rowe–Broadie (Oxford, 2002) for the best combined translation-with-commentary; Crisp (Cambridge) for a careful contemporary translation in the Cambridge Texts series. | Read in full by every philosophy student. The central primary text for §8.4 (virtue ethics). Pair with Broadie's *Ethics with Aristotle* (§2.5) for the best secondary companion. |
| `[Primary]` Aristotle — first philosophy, ontology | *Metaphysics*, 4th c. BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Aristotle**; trans. **C. D. C. Reeve** (Hackett, 2016); alternative: **W. D. Ross** (in Barnes Complete Works) | Aristotle's *prōtē philosophia* — fourteen books (probably assembled posthumously from separate treatises) treating being qua being, substance, form and matter, potentiality and actuality, the unmoved mover, and the critique of Platonic Forms. Books Z, H, Θ (the "central books") on substance and the categories of being are the metaphysical heart and have generated more secondary literature than almost any other text in Western philosophy. The *Metaphysics* is not a unified treatise — its books were composed at different times and the editorial assembly is ancient — and reading it requires attention to which book one is in. Limitation: the textual condition of the *Metaphysics* is the worst of any major Aristotelian work; difficult passages are sometimes corrupt, and the *Posterior Analytics*'s methodological promises are imperfectly carried out in the *Metaphysics*'s practice. **Recommended translation:** Reeve (Hackett, 2016) — the most recent careful translation with substantial commentary; Ross (in Barnes) remains the historical scholarly default. Burnyeat's *A Map of Metaphysics Zeta* (§2.5) is the indispensable companion to Book Z. | Graduate students in metaphysics, ancient philosophy, or scholasticism. Begin with Book Γ (Gamma) on the principle of non-contradiction and Book Δ (Delta) on philosophical terminology; then Books Z–Θ. See also SEP: "Aristotle's Metaphysics." |
| `[Primary]` Aristotle — philosophy of mind, biology | *De Anima*, 4th c. BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Aristotle**; trans. **Christopher Shields** (Clarendon, 2016); alternative: **D. W. Hamlyn** (Clarendon, 1968; rev. 1993) | The founding work of philosophy of mind in the Western tradition: three books on the soul as the *form* of a living body, the faculties of nutrition, perception, imagination (*phantasia*), and intellect (*nous*), and the puzzles of the active intellect (Book III, chapter 5 — one of the most disputed passages in the history of philosophy). Aristotle's hylomorphic account of soul, on which mental properties are formal properties of suitably organised bodies, has been revived in contemporary philosophy of mind under names like "Aristotelian functionalism" (Nussbaum, Wedin) and used as a model for non-reductive physicalism. Limitation: Book III is fragmentary in argument and the active intellect doctrine became a battlefield in medieval philosophy (Averroes, Aquinas) precisely because Aristotle's text is unclear. **Recommended translation:** Shields (Clarendon, 2016) — newest and most philosophically scrupulous, with a 400-page commentary that is itself a monograph; Hamlyn (Clarendon) is the classic alternative and remains useful. | Graduate students in philosophy of mind and ancient philosophy. Connect to Table 7 §7.7 (embodied cognition) for contemporary descendants of the hylomorphic approach. See also SEP: "Aristotle's Psychology." |
| `[Primary]` Aristotle — political philosophy | *Politics*, 4th c. BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Aristotle**; trans. **C. D. C. Reeve** (Hackett, 2nd ed. 2017); alternative: **Carnes Lord** (Chicago, 2nd ed. 2013) | Eight books on the *polis* and the political conditions of human flourishing: the nature of the political community, the household (including the controversial defence of natural slavery in Book I), the classification of constitutions, the analysis of existing regimes including the critique of Plato's *Republic*, and the ideal state. The *Politics* originates the tradition of empirical constitutional comparison alongside normative political philosophy. The argument that the polis exists for the sake of the good life and that humans are by nature political animals has been the touchstone for communitarian, civic-republican, and virtue-political thought ever since. Limitation: the defence of slavery in Book I is morally and philosophically defective, and Aristotle's casual misogyny pervades the discussion of the household; commentary that does not honestly address these failures should not be trusted. The book order is also disputed (some scholars place Books VII–VIII before IV–VI). **Recommended translation:** Reeve (Hackett, 2nd ed. 2017) for accuracy with accessibility; Lord (Chicago) for the most literal version, with Straussian framing visible in the introduction. | Read after the *Nicomachean Ethics* — Aristotle himself directs the reader from *NE* X to the *Politics*. Central for §8.4 (virtue ethics) and §8.10 (political philosophy). |

### 2.5 Aristotle — Secondary Literature & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Secondary]` Aristotle — single-author overview `[Analytic]` | *Aristotle*, 2nd ed., 2014 (Routledge Philosophers) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Christopher Shields** | The best single-volume philosophical introduction to Aristotle in English: chapter-length treatments of the central topics (categories and logic, philosophy of nature, soul, ethics, politics, metaphysics) with Shields's own analytic reconstructions of the arguments. Shields is uncommonly clear about where Aristotle's arguments work and where they don't, and the chapter on hylomorphism is among the best short treatments available. Limitation: the analytic-reconstructive approach inherits the methodological tradeoffs flagged in §1.5 — readers seeking richer historical or rhetorical context should pair with Lear (below) or with the relevant Cambridge Companion. | The starting point for serious engagement with Aristotle by anyone with general philosophical background. Read alongside the primary texts; consult the matching Clarendon volumes for individual works. |
| `[Secondary]` Aristotle — introduction with philosophical vision `[Evergreen]` | *Aristotle: The Desire to Understand*, 1988 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jonathan Lear** | A more interpretive and synoptic introduction than Shields: Lear organises the corpus around the Aristotelian project of understanding the cosmos as ordered toward intelligibility, working from the *Metaphysics*'s opening claim that "all men by nature desire to know." Less concerned than Shields to evaluate Aristotle's arguments by contemporary standards; more concerned to exhibit the systematic unity of the corpus. The chapter on substance and the chapter on man's nature are particularly strong. Limitation: the unifying vision is itself an interpretive thesis, and Lear papers over some genuine tensions in the corpus to maintain it. | Read after a first pass through *NE* and selections of the *Metaphysics*. Particularly valuable for students who find analytic commentary too argumentative-fragmentary. |
| `[Secondary]` Aristotle — *Nicomachean Ethics* commentary `[Evergreen]` | *Ethics with Aristotle*, 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Sarah Broadie** | The outstanding philosophical study of the *Nicomachean Ethics* in English: a book-by-book engagement with the argument, with extended treatment of *eudaimonia*, voluntariness, *phronesis*, the unity of the virtues, *akrasia*, and the contemplative life. Broadie's reading of *phronesis* as a kind of perception rather than a kind of deliberation is among the most influential interpretations of the past forty years. Less a commentary than an extended argument *with* Aristotle, treating the *NE* as a still-living philosophical project. Limitation: dense; presupposes that the reader has the Greek text or a careful translation open throughout. | Graduate students working on Aristotelian ethics. Pair with the Rowe–Broadie translation (above) — the two are designed to be used together, and the combined volume (Oxford World's Classics, 2002) is the best Aristotle-with-commentary package available. |
| `[Secondary]` Aristotle — *Metaphysics* Z commentary `[Research]` | *A Map of Metaphysics Zeta*, 2001 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Myles Burnyeat** | A short (~150 pp.), surgical reconstruction of the argument of *Metaphysics* Book Z (Zeta) — the central book of the central books, on substance and form. Burnyeat traces the dialectical structure across Z's seventeen chapters, identifying the apparent dead ends, the genuine theses, and the relationships between competing characterisations of substance. The result is the indispensable companion to the most difficult and important book of the *Metaphysics*. Limitation: presupposes a substantial prior engagement with Z; not a first reading of the book but a guide for those who have already tried. The German tradition (Frede–Patzig's *Aristoteles "Metaphysik Z"*, 1988) remains untranslated and is the major scholarly alternative. | Graduate students in ancient metaphysics or scholastic studies. Read after at least one direct encounter with *Metaphysics* Z; revisit annually. |
| `[Reference]` Aristotle — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Aristotle*, 1995 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Jonathan Barnes** (ed.) | The standard orientation volume: 13 essays by leading Aristotle scholars covering the life and works, logic, natural philosophy, biology, philosophy of mind, ethics, politics, rhetoric, poetics, and reception. Barnes's own opening essay on the corpus is a model of compact scholarship. Limitation: now three decades old; substantial newer literature on Aristotle's biology (Lennox), on the *Posterior Analytics* (McKirahan, Bronstein), and on hylomorphism is not represented. The *Oxford Handbook of Aristotle* (Shields, ed., 2012) is more recent and broader but less consistently outstanding chapter-by-chapter. | Graduate students orienting in Aristotle studies. Read Barnes's introduction first; consult other chapters paired with the corresponding primary works. |

### 2.6 Hellenistic Philosophy — Stoics, Epicureans, Sceptics

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Hellenistic fragments anthology `[Reference]` | *The Hellenistic Philosophers*, 2 vols., 1987 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **A. A. Long & D. N. Sedley** | The standard English-language presentation of the Hellenistic philosophical traditions: Volume 1 contains topically-organised translations with substantial philosophical commentary; Volume 2 provides the Greek and Latin texts with apparatus. Covers Stoicism, Epicureanism, Academic Scepticism, and Pyrrhonian Scepticism through the major themes (logic, physics, ethics, epistemology). Long and Sedley reconstructed the philosophical positions from the scattered surviving fragments and testimonia with such philosophical care that the volumes function both as primary anthology and as a major work of secondary scholarship. Limitation: the topical organisation makes it harder to read any single school's view as it would have been presented; Inwood–Gerson (below) supplements with school-by-school presentations. **Recommended translation:** Long–Sedley (Cambridge, 1987) is now the scholarly reference; their translations are uniformly competent. | Anyone working on Hellenistic philosophy. Volume 1 alone is sufficient for most non-textual purposes; both volumes for serious scholarly work. See also SEP: "Stoicism," "Epicureanism," "Ancient Skepticism." |
| `[Primary]` Stoic practical philosophy | *Meditations*, c. 170–180 AD `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Marcus Aurelius**; trans. **Robin Hard** (Oxford, 2011); alternative: **Gregory Hays** (Modern Library, 2002) | The personal notebooks of the Roman emperor and Stoic, twelve books of self-addressed reflection on Stoic ethics, cosmology, and the practice of philosophy as a way of life. Not a systematic treatise — the *Meditations* repeats themes obsessively (the indifference of fortune, the cosmic perspective, the practice of attention) precisely because Stoicism is a discipline that must be re-enacted. The work has had two distinct lives: as a curiosity for ancient historians, and (more recently) as the founding document of "popular Stoicism" — a usage that distorts the text but reveals its enduring practical appeal. Limitation: the lack of system is structural and intentional; readers seeking a systematic Stoicism should turn to Long–Sedley, Epictetus' *Discourses*, or the secondary literature. **Recommended translation:** Hard (Oxford World's Classics, 2011) for scholarly accuracy with literary care; Hays (Modern Library, 2002) for a more contemporary English prose, but with some interpretive liberties. | Self-study readers; students of Stoic ethics; readers of the popular Stoicism literature seeking the primary text. Pair with Long's *Epictetus: A Stoic and Socratic Guide to Life* and Hadot's *The Inner Citadel* (a study of the *Meditations*) for substantial commentary. |
| `[Primary]` Epicurean philosophy of nature & ethics `[Bridge]` | *De Rerum Natura* (*On the Nature of Things*), c. 60–55 BC `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Lucretius**; trans. **Martin Ferguson Smith** (Hackett, 2001) or **A. E. Stallings** (Penguin, 2007, verse) | The fullest surviving exposition of Epicurean philosophy, presented as a Latin philosophical poem in six books: atoms and the void, the soul's mortality, the gods' indifference to human affairs, the development of language and civilisation, and the natural origin of mind and culture. *De Rerum Natura* is the only Epicurean primary text that survives intact, and its recovery in 1417 (Bracciolini in Germany) had measurable effects on the early modern scientific revolution (see Greenblatt's *The Swerve*, popular but disputed). The poem operates simultaneously as physics, ethics, and literary masterpiece — and the unity of the three is itself part of the philosophical argument. Limitation: Lucretius is a follower of Epicurus, not an originator, and the relation between Lucretian and earlier Epicurean doctrine is sometimes hard to fix. **Recommended translation:** Smith (Hackett, 2001) for a faithful prose translation with substantial introduction and notes; Stallings (Penguin, 2007) for an English verse translation that preserves the poetic dimension at some cost to philosophical precision. The two-volume Loeb (Rouse rev. Smith, 1992) is the standard scholarly edition. | Readers in Epicurean philosophy, philosophy of mind (the materialist soul), or history of science. Pair with Long & Sedley's *Hellenistic Philosophers* sections on Epicureanism. See also SEP: "Lucretius." |
| `[Secondary]` Hellenistic philosophy — survey `[Evergreen]` | *Hellenistic Philosophy: Stoics, Epicureans, Sceptics*, 2nd ed., 1986 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **A. A. Long** | The standard single-volume introduction to the three major Hellenistic schools: their philosophical doctrines, their internal debates, and their relations to one another. Long writes for the educated non-specialist while maintaining scholarly accuracy; the chapters on Stoic epistemology and on Pyrrhonian scepticism are particularly strong. The 2nd edition incorporates substantial revisions reflecting the explosion of Hellenistic scholarship after 1970 (driven significantly by Long's own work and by the parallel work of Burnyeat, Frede, and Sedley). Limitation: organised by school rather than by problem; readers wanting cross-school comparison on a specific topic should turn to Long & Sedley. | The first secondary text to read on Hellenistic philosophy. Pair with Long & Sedley for the primary material. |
| `[Secondary]` Ancient philosophy as way of life `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *Pursuits of Wisdom: Six Ways of Life in Ancient Philosophy from Socrates to Plotinus*, 2012 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **John M. Cooper** | A research monograph by the editor of the *Plato: Complete Works* (§2.2), defending against Pierre Hadot the view that ancient philosophical "ways of life" were grounded in and inseparable from systematic philosophical argument — *not* in spiritual exercises that could be detached from theoretical philosophy. Cooper treats Socratic, Aristotelian, Stoic, Epicurean, Sceptic, and Plotinian conceptions of the philosophical life in turn, in each case showing how the practical orientation depends on substantive theoretical commitments. The book is the major recent corrective to the Hadot-Foucault reading of ancient philosophy (see §1.5 Hadot entry) without dismissing the underlying observation that ancient philosophy *was* a way of life. Limitation: Cooper's case against Hadot is sometimes presented as starker than it needs to be; the two positions are partially complementary. | Advanced readers in ancient philosophy; anyone engaging with the Hadot–Foucault tradition. Read after Long; pair with Hadot's *Philosophy as a Way of Life* (§1.5) for the methodological debate. |

### 2.7 Neoplatonism & Late Antique Philosophy

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Neoplatonic metaphysics | *Enneads*, c. 254–270 AD `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Plotinus**; trans. **Lloyd P. Gerson** et al. (Cambridge, 2018); alternative: **Stephen MacKenna** (Penguin, abridged) | The founding work of Neoplatonism: 54 treatises (organised posthumously by Porphyry into six sets of nine — *Enneads*) on the One, the Intellect (*Nous*), the Soul (*Psychē*), beauty, providence, the descent of the soul, and the contemplative ascent. Plotinus develops Plato's metaphysics into a hierarchical ontology that became the framework for Christian (Augustine, Pseudo-Dionysius, Aquinas), Jewish, and Islamic philosophical theology for over a millennium. The *Enneads* are also extraordinarily difficult: the prose is compressed, the order non-systematic, and the doctrines develop across treatises in ways that resist linear reading. Limitation: the absence of a canonical reading order combined with Plotinus's own opacity makes the text very hard to enter without secondary guidance. **Recommended translation:** Gerson (Cambridge, 2018) — the new scholarly standard, replacing Armstrong's seven-volume Loeb (1966–1988) for English-only readers; MacKenna's older translation is literary and beautiful but interpretively dated. Treatises I.6 (On Beauty), V.1 (On the Three Primary Hypostases), and VI.9 (On the Good or the One) are conventional starting points. | Graduate students in ancient philosophy, philosophical theology, or medieval philosophy. Always read with substantial secondary guidance; O'Meara (below) is the standard first companion. See also SEP: "Plotinus." |
| `[Secondary]` Plotinus — introduction `[Self-Study]` | *Plotinus: An Introduction to the Enneads*, 1993 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Dominic J. O'Meara** | The best short introduction to the *Enneads*: O'Meara organises the material by topic (the One, Intellect, Soul, the sensible world, the human person, return to the One) and provides a clear map of which treatises develop which themes. Concise (~150 pp.) and accurate. Limitation: introductory rather than research-level; for deeper engagement with specific treatises, consult Gerson's *Plotinus* (Routledge Philosophers, 1994) or the individual Plotinus-volume commentaries (Atkinson, Bréhier, Hadot's *Plotin: Traité 38*). | Read before opening the *Enneads*; then re-read alongside specific treatises. |
| `[Secondary]` Neoplatonic tradition — historical survey `[Evergreen]` | *Neoplatonism*, 2nd ed., 1995 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **R. T. Wallis** | The standard history of the Neoplatonic tradition from Plotinus through Iamblichus, Proclus, Damascius, and the Athenian and Alexandrian schools to the closure of the Academy in 529. Wallis treats both the philosophy and its institutional and religious dimensions (theurgy, the relations to Christianity and to pagan religion). The 2nd edition adds a substantial supplementary bibliography by Lloyd Gerson. Limitation: dated in some interpretive judgments (especially on Iamblichean theurgy, which more recent scholarship views less dismissively than Wallis); for current state of the field consult the *Cambridge Companion to Plotinus* (Gerson, ed.) and Lloyd's two-volume *Anatomy of Neoplatonism*. | Graduate students in late ancient philosophy, philosophy of religion, or the reception of Plato. Useful for orientation; the field has moved on but the framework remains useful. |

### 2.8 Medieval Philosophy — Augustine through Aquinas

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Augustine — autobiography, memory, time | *Confessions*, 397–400 AD `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Augustine**; trans. **Henry Chadwick** (Oxford, 1991); alternative: **Maria Boulding** (New City, 1997) | The first sustained autobiography in Western literature and one of the most philosophically substantial: thirteen books moving from Augustine's childhood through his conversion to a meditation on memory (Book X), time (Book XI), and the creation narrative of Genesis (Books XII–XIII). The discussion of time in Book XI — does the past exist? does the future? if not, how can either be measured? — has remained productive for philosophy of time from Husserl through contemporary analytic philosophy. The treatment of memory in Book X anticipates phenomenological questions about the structure of consciousness. Limitation: the autobiographical and theological dimensions are inseparable from the philosophical ones, and readers wanting pure philosophy will find the dialogue with God uncongenial; this is itself part of what makes the *Confessions* a distinctive philosophical achievement. **Recommended translation:** Chadwick (Oxford, 1991) for scholarly accuracy with literary grace; Boulding (New City, 1997) for slightly more accessible English with the full Latin apparatus in the parallel scholarly edition. | Any serious philosophy student. The chapters on time and memory are central; the rest is philosophy embedded in autobiography. See also SEP: "Saint Augustine." |
| `[Primary]` Augustine — political theology, philosophy of history | *City of God*, 413–426 AD `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Augustine**; trans. **R. W. Dyson** (Cambridge, 1998); alternative: **Henry Bettenson** (Penguin, 1972) | A vast (22 books, ~1,100 pp.) treatise responding to the pagan claim that the 410 sack of Rome resulted from the empire's adoption of Christianity. Augustine constructs the framework of the two cities (the *civitas Dei* and the *civitas terrena*) — communities defined by their objects of love — that has shaped Western political theology and political philosophy. Books XI–XIV on creation, the fall, and the nature of evil; Books XIX–XXII on the highest good, peace, and the last things are the philosophically densest. The treatment of just war (Book XIX) and of slavery as consequence of sin (Book XIX, ch. 15) are foundational for medieval and early modern political thought. Limitation: long and uneven; the books on pagan religion (I–X) are of greater historical than philosophical interest, and most readers will work selectively. **Recommended translation:** Dyson (Cambridge, 1998) for the scholarly Cambridge Texts edition with the most reliable apparatus; Bettenson (Penguin, 1972) for accessibility. | Graduate students in political philosophy, political theology, or medieval philosophy. Books XI, XIV, XIX are the conventional core. |
| `[Primary]` Aquinas — systematic theology and philosophy | *Summa Theologiae*, 1265–1273 (unfinished) `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Thomas Aquinas**; trans. **Fathers of the English Dominican Province** (Benziger, 1911; available free online) or **Blackfriars** edition (60 vols., 1964–1981); accessible selection: **Timothy McDermott**, *Summa Theologiae: A Concise Translation* (Christian Classics, 1989) | The most comprehensive and architecturally complete medieval Christian philosophical-theological work: three parts (the existence and nature of God; humans and their actions; Christ and the sacraments), divided into questions, each question into articles structured as objections, *sed contra*, response (*respondeo*), and replies. The treatment of the existence of God (the Five Ways, ST Ia q. 2), the divine nature and attributes, the doctrine of the soul and the human person (treatise on man, Ia qq. 75–89), the philosophy of action and ethics (the Treatise on Law in IaIIae, the Treatise on Virtue and the Treatise on Happiness), and the moral and theological virtues fill most of the systematic philosophical content. The *Summa* is the architectural model of medieval Aristotelianism — Aristotle as transmitted through the Islamic philosophers (Avicenna, Averroes — §2.10) and synthesised with Augustine, Pseudo-Dionysius, and Christian revelation. Limitation: the *Summa* is a textbook for beginners in theology — its argumentative compression assumes the broader scholastic tradition; for the underlying philosophical positions one must also consult the *Summa Contra Gentiles*, the *Disputed Questions* (on truth, on the soul, on evil), and the commentaries on Aristotle. **Recommended translation:** The English Dominican translation (1911) is serviceable, free online (*New Advent*), and remains standard for citation; the Blackfriars 60-volume edition (Latin facing English, ed. Gilby et al.) is the scholarly version; McDermott's *Concise Translation* selects 20% of the text and is the best entry point for non-specialist readers. | Begin with McDermott's concise translation; then return to the full text for specific questions. The Five Ways (Ia q. 2 a. 3) and the Treatise on Happiness (IaIIae qq. 1–5) are essential; the Treatise on Law (IaIIae qq. 90–108) is central to natural-law theory. Pair with Stump (below). See also SEP: "Saint Thomas Aquinas." |
