| `[Secondary]` Medieval philosophy — survey `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Medieval Philosophy: An Historical and Philosophical Introduction*, 2007 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **John Marenbon** | The current standard single-volume survey of medieval philosophy in English: covers the Greek, Arabic, Jewish, and Latin traditions from c. 200 to c. 1400, with substantial integration of the Islamic philosophers (Marenbon is among the few Latin medievalists who treats the Arabic tradition seriously rather than as background). Organised chronologically with thematic excursions. Limitation: comprehensive coverage at the cost of depth on any single figure; for Aquinas use Stump (below), for Ockham use Adams, for Avicenna use Wisnovsky. Marenbon's own scholarly work focuses on the 12th century (Abelard, Gilbert of Poitiers) and that period receives correspondingly stronger treatment than (e.g.) early Franciscan thought. | The first secondary text to read on the medieval period as a whole. Use as scaffolding before reading individual figures. |
| `[Secondary]` Aquinas — philosophical study `[Research]` | *Aquinas* (Arguments of the Philosophers series), 2003 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Eleonore Stump** | The standard book-length philosophical study of Aquinas in English: ~600 pages covering metaphysics (essence, existence, simplicity), philosophy of mind (soul, intellect, will, freedom), ethics (action, virtue, the moral law), and philosophical theology (foreknowledge, evil, atonement). Stump's reconstruction of Aquinas's accounts of action, freedom, and the will is the major contemporary contribution to Aquinas studies in the analytic tradition. The treatment of double-effect reasoning and of the *praesens evum* (the eternal present) are particularly strong. Limitation: Stump's interpretations are sometimes more analytically charitable than the texts strictly warrant; the contrast with Kenny's *Aquinas on Mind* (which finds more Aquinas-style materialism) is instructive. | Graduate students in medieval philosophy, philosophy of mind, or philosophy of religion. Read after Marenbon and McDermott's selection from the *Summa*. |

### 2.9 Late Medieval — Scotus, Ockham & the Dissolution of Scholasticism

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Scotus — metaphysics, theology, ethics | *Philosophical Writings: A Selection*, 1962 (rev. 1987) `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **John Duns Scotus**; trans. **Allan B. Wolter** (Hackett) | The standard English selection from Scotus's enormous corpus (*Ordinatio*, *Lectura*, *Reportatio*, the *Quodlibetal Questions*): on the natural knowledge of God's existence, the univocity of being, individuation (the famous *haecceitas*), divine ideas, the will and contingency, and the moral law. Scotus is the second great medieval scholastic alongside Aquinas — and his metaphysics (univocity of being against analogy; formal distinction; haecceity as principle of individuation) departs sharply from Thomas's and reshaped Franciscan theology and post-medieval philosophy. Limitation: the Wolter selection is necessarily partial; Scotus's text is technically dense and the apparatus required for serious study is enormous. **Recommended translation:** Wolter (Hackett, 1987 rev.) — the standard English selection, with Wolter's introductions; the Vatican critical edition (in progress since 1950) is the Latin reference; Williams's *Cambridge Companion to Duns Scotus* (2003) is the indispensable secondary entry. See also Cross's *Duns Scotus* (Oxford, 1999) for the best single-volume philosophical study. | Graduate students in medieval philosophy or metaphysics. Not a first encounter with scholastic thought — read Marenbon, then Aquinas, then Scotus. See also SEP: "John Duns Scotus." |
| `[Primary]` Ockham — logic, metaphysics, theology | *Philosophical Writings: A Selection*, 1957 (rev. 1990) `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **William of Ockham**; trans. **Philotheus Boehner**, rev. **Stephen F. Brown** (Hackett) | The standard English selection from Ockham's logical and philosophical works: the *Summa Logicae*, the commentary on Porphyry, and selections from the *Quodlibetal Questions* and the *Reportatio*. Includes Ockham's nominalist critique of common natures, his account of mental language, his defence of divine omnipotence against the necessity of any particular created order, and his famous logical economy ("plurality is not to be posited without necessity" — *the* "Ockham's razor," though Ockham himself never used the formula). The Loux/Freddoso translation of the complete *Summa Logicae* (Notre Dame, 1974/1980) is the more substantial primary source for serious work on Ockham's logic. **Recommended translation:** Boehner rev. Brown (Hackett, 1990) for the selection; Loux–Freddoso (Notre Dame) for the *Summa Logicae* parts. | Graduate students in medieval philosophy, philosophy of language, or logic. Adams's *William Ockham* (below) is essential secondary; see also SEP: "William of Ockham." |
| `[Secondary]` Ockham — comprehensive study `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *William Ockham*, 2 vols., 1987 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Marilyn McCord Adams** | The definitive English-language study of Ockham's philosophy: nearly 1,400 pages covering Ockham's nominalism (the rejection of universals as extramental realities, ST treatment of categories), his physics and natural philosophy, his philosophical theology (divine omnipotence and the contingency of the moral order, divine ideas), and his political philosophy. Adams's reconstruction of Ockham's nominalism and of his theology of divine omnipotence reshaped late medieval studies in English; her engagement is uncommonly philosophically serious. Limitation: enormous and forbidding; not a first reading on Ockham but the destination for serious work. | Researchers and advanced graduate students in medieval philosophy. Read after the Hackett selection of Ockham and Marenbon's medieval survey. |

### 2.10 Islamic Philosophy — Al-Farabi, Avicenna, Averroes

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Avicenna — metaphysics | *The Metaphysics of The Healing* (*Al-Ilāhiyyāt min al-Shifāʾ*), c. 1020–1027 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Avicenna (Ibn Sīnā)**; trans. **Michael E. Marmura** (Brigham Young University Press, 2005, parallel Arabic–English) | The metaphysics section of Avicenna's vast *Shifāʾ* (The Cure/Healing) — the most systematically architectonic philosophical work of the Islamic world and the single most influential philosophical text on Latin scholasticism. Ten books moving from the nature of metaphysics as a science, through the famous *existence/essence* distinction (which entered Latin philosophy through the 12th-century translation and shaped Aquinas, Scotus, Henry of Ghent), the necessary and the contingent (the *necessary-of-itself* vs. *necessary-through-another*), the cosmological argument from contingency, the doctrine of the active intellect, and prophecy. Avicenna's existence/essence distinction reshaped both Islamic and Latin metaphysics for centuries. Limitation: long, terminologically dense, and presupposes Avicenna's logic and physics from earlier parts of the *Shifāʾ*. **Recommended translation:** Marmura (BYU, 2005) — the only complete English translation of the *Ilāhiyyāt*, with facing Arabic; the standard scholarly edition. | Graduate students in medieval philosophy, philosophy of religion, or Islamic studies. Wisnovsky's *Avicenna's Metaphysics in Context* (2003) is the major recent secondary study. See also SEP: "Ibn Sina [Avicenna]" and "Arabic and Islamic Metaphysics." |
| `[Primary]` Averroes — philosophy and religion | *The Book of the Decisive Treatise* (*Faṣl al-Maqāl*), 1179–1180 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Averroes (Ibn Rushd)**; trans. **Charles E. Butterworth** (Brigham Young University Press, 2001, parallel Arabic–English) | Averroes's short juridical defence of the legitimacy — indeed the obligation — of philosophical inquiry from the standpoint of Islamic law (*sharīʿa*). The argument: the Qur'an itself commands the study of beings; demonstrative philosophy is the highest form of such study; therefore philosophical inquiry is religiously required of those capable of it. The treatise is also Averroes's response to Al-Ghazali's *Incoherence of the Philosophers* (which had attacked Avicenna and the philosophical tradition) — Averroes's longer rebuttal is the *Incoherence of the Incoherence* (*Tahāfut al-Tahāfut*). The brief *Decisive Treatise* (~50 pp.) is the more accessible entry to Averroist thought. Limitation: a polemical and rhetorical work; the philosophical substance of Averroism is developed in the great commentaries on Aristotle, particularly the *Long Commentary on the De Anima* (Taylor's translation, Yale, 2009). **Recommended translation:** Butterworth (BYU, 2001) — accurate, with substantial introduction. | Graduate students in medieval philosophy, philosophy of religion, or Islamic studies. See also SEP: "Ibn Rushd [Averroes]." |
| `[Primary]` Al-Ghazali — critique of falsafa | *The Incoherence of the Philosophers* (*Tahāfut al-Falāsifa*), c. 1095 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Al-Ghazali**; trans. **Michael E. Marmura** (Brigham Young University Press, 2nd ed. 2000, parallel Arabic–English) | The most systematic and influential critique of the Islamic Aristotelian-Neoplatonic tradition (Al-Farabi, Avicenna) ever written: twenty problems on which Al-Ghazali argues the *falāsifa* (philosophers) either contradict themselves or contradict Islam, including the eternity of the world (Problem 1, the longest), divine knowledge of particulars (Problem 13), and bodily resurrection (Problem 20). The critique of necessary causation in Problem 17 — anticipating Hume's regularity theory by six centuries — is among the most striking. Al-Ghazali's targets are clear and the argumentation careful; this is not anti-philosophical polemic but philosophical critique conducted on philosophical grounds. Averroes's *Tahāfut al-Tahāfut* is the standard reply. Limitation: Al-Ghazali's own positive epistemology (Ash'arite occasionalism, Sufi mysticism) is not developed here — for that see his *Deliverance from Error* and *Niche of Lights*. **Recommended translation:** Marmura (BYU, 2nd ed. 2000) — the scholarly standard, with facing Arabic. | Graduate students in medieval philosophy or Islamic studies. Read alongside the relevant Avicenna sections; pair with Frank Griffel's *Al-Ghazali's Philosophical Theology* (Oxford, 2009) for secondary orientation. See also SEP: "Al-Ghazali." |
| `[Secondary]` Islamic philosophy — survey `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Philosophy in the Islamic World: A Very Short Introduction*, 2015, and *Philosophy in the Islamic World: A History of Philosophy Without Any Gaps, Volume 3*, 2016 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Peter Adamson** | Adamson's *Philosophy in the Islamic World* (volume 3 of his expanding *History of Philosophy Without Any Gaps* project, based on the podcast of the same name) is the most readable, accurate, and complete English-language survey of Islamic philosophy currently available — covering not only the classical *falsafa* tradition (Al-Kindi, Al-Farabi, Avicenna, Averroes, Al-Ghazali) but also Ash'arite and Mu'tazilite theology, Sufi philosophy, Jewish philosophy of the Islamic world (Maimonides, Saadia Gaon), and the post-classical traditions (Mulla Sadra, the Safavid school) often neglected in Western surveys. The shorter Very Short Introduction is a useful first entry. Limitation: written for the general reader; for technical detail consult the *Cambridge Companion to Arabic Philosophy* (Adamson & Taylor, eds., 2005). | The starting point for serious engagement with Islamic philosophy. The longer volume is essential; the VSI for orientation. See also SEP for individual figure entries. |
| `[Reference]` Islamic philosophy — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Arabic Philosophy*, 2005 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Peter Adamson & Richard C. Taylor** (eds.) | The standard scholarly orientation: 17 essays by leading specialists covering the major figures (Al-Kindi, Al-Razi, Al-Farabi, Avicenna, Al-Ghazali, Averroes, Suhrawardi, Mulla Sadra) and topics (logic, metaphysics, psychology, ethics, political philosophy, the influence on Latin philosophy). Limitation: covers the "Arabic" tradition specifically — Islamic philosophy in Persian (Mulla Sadra, the Illuminationists) and the later Ottoman tradition receive less attention; for the broader picture consult Adamson's history (above) and the more recent *Oxford Handbook of Islamic Philosophy* (El-Rouayheb & Schmidtke, 2017). | Graduate students orienting in Islamic philosophy. Read after Adamson's survey. |

### Connections to other tables

The translation framework applied throughout this table will continue in Table 3 (Descartes through Hume — most of whom wrote in Latin or French) and Table 4 (Kant and Hegel — the German Idealist translation choices are arguably as consequential as the Plato or Aristotle ones). Aristotle's *Nicomachean Ethics* and *Politics* are the foundation of §8.4 (virtue ethics) and §8.10 (political philosophy); the *De Anima* connects to §7.7 (embodied cognition) and Aquinas's hylomorphism is one of the live options in contemporary philosophy of mind. The *Timaeus* and Aristotle's *Physics* connect to the early-cosmology and natural-philosophy sections of the Physics Atlas; Lucretius's atomism does the same. The Islamic philosophers (§2.10) are not optional background to medieval Christian philosophy — Aquinas's metaphysics (§2.8) is in continuous dialogue with Avicenna's, and the existence/essence distinction was naturalised in Latin philosophy through Avicenna's translation. The methodological choice flagged in §1.5 — Bennett-style rational reconstruction (Barnes on the pre-Socratics) versus Hadot-style historical-contextual reading (Cooper on ancient ways of life) — operates throughout this table and the reader's choice of secondary literature partly determines which Aristotle, which Plotinus, which Aquinas one ends up with.

# Table 3 · Early Modern Philosophy (Descartes through Hume)

*Scope:* The rationalist tradition (Descartes, Spinoza, Leibniz), the empiricist tradition (Locke, Berkeley, Hume), the surveys and thematic studies of the period, and philosophy's entanglement with the scientific revolution. Treat the rationalism/empiricism categorisation as a Kantian retrospective construction whose historiographical limits the entries below make explicit.

### 3.1 Descartes — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Descartes — complete works | *The Philosophical Writings of Descartes*, 3 vols., 1985–1991 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **René Descartes**; trans. **John Cottingham, Robert Stoothoff, Dugald Murdoch** (with **Anthony Kenny** for the correspondence, vol. 3) | The standard English edition (CSM): Vol. 1 covers the *Rules*, *Discourse on Method*, *Optics*, *Meteorology*, *Geometry*, *Principles of Philosophy*, *Comments on a Certain Broadsheet*, *Passions of the Soul*; Vol. 2 contains the *Meditations* with the seven sets of *Objections and Replies*; Vol. 3 selects from the correspondence. The translation aims for philosophical accuracy rather than literary elegance and is the citation standard for Anglophone Descartes scholarship; pagination references the Adam–Tannery (AT) critical edition. The *Meditations* with *Objections and Replies* — Descartes's exchanges with Hobbes, Arnauld, Mersenne, Gassendi, and others — must be read together; isolating the *Meditations* loses half the philosophical content. **Recommended translation:** CSM for citation; for a Latin–English facing edition of the *Meditations* alone, Cress (Hackett, 1993) is the standard student version, and the Cambridge Texts edition by **Cottingham** (1996) integrates with Cottingham's broader Descartes scholarship. | Owned by anyone working seriously on Descartes. Read *Meditations + Objections and Replies* (Vol. 2) first; consult Vols. 1 and 3 selectively. See also SEP: "René Descartes." |
| `[Primary]` Descartes — early methodology and natural philosophy | *Discourse on the Method*, 1637; *Rules for the Direction of the Mind*, c. 1628 (unpublished in his lifetime) `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **René Descartes**; trans. **CSM** Vol. 1 | The *Discourse* is Descartes's autobiographical-methodological essay introducing the three scientific essays it originally prefaced (*Optics*, *Meteorology*, *Geometry* — among the founding works of modern science). It contains an early presentation of the doubt and the *cogito* (Part IV) and the famous distinction between thinking substance and extended substance, but in a less philosophically developed form than the *Meditations*. The *Rules* is a fragmentary earlier work on philosophical and mathematical method, important for understanding Descartes's project before the metaphysical turn of the 1640s. Limitation: both works are preparatory rather than systematic — the *Meditations* is where Descartes works through his philosophy at full pressure, and the *Discourse* and *Rules* are best read as historical contexts for it. | Read the *Discourse* before the *Meditations* for orientation; the *Rules* after the *Meditations* for the methodological background. The *Geometry* (which introduced analytic geometry) is essential reading for the history of mathematics — see the Mathematics Atlas. |
| `[Secondary]` Descartes — philosophical study `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Descartes: The Project of Pure Enquiry*, 1978 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Bernard Williams** | The single most influential English-language philosophical study of Descartes: Williams reads the *Meditations* as the founding attempt to characterise *the absolute conception of reality* — a description of the world from no particular perspective — and traces Descartes's struggles with the gap between absolute and perspectival knowledge through his metaphysics, epistemology, and theology. The book is also the major exemplar of Williams's own approach to history of philosophy: rational reconstruction in the service of a philosophical thesis, conducted with full historical care (cf. §1.4). The chapters on the *cogito*, on the Cartesian circle, and on knowledge and certainty are individual classics. Limitation: Williams's "absolute conception" framing is itself a substantive philosophical thesis that not all Descartes scholars share; the book is Descartes seen through one powerful philosophical lens rather than a neutral commentary. | Graduate students in early modern philosophy or epistemology. Read after the *Meditations* itself. Pair with Cottingham's *Descartes* (Blackwell, 1986) for a more conservative reading of the same material. |
| `[Secondary]` Descartes — philosophy of mind `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Descartes's Dualism*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Marleen Rozemond** | The major recent reconstruction of Descartes's mind-body dualism: Rozemond argues that Descartes's dualism is best understood not as inherited Platonism but as a transformation of scholastic Aristotelian categories of substance and mode. The book has reshaped scholarship by foregrounding Descartes's engagement with the scholastic tradition and dispelling the common picture of Cartesian dualism as a free-standing philosophical innovation. Limitation: focused specifically on the dualism question; readers wanting a broader treatment of Descartes's metaphysics should pair with Garber's *Descartes' Metaphysical Physics* (§3.8) or with Hoffman's collected papers. | Graduate students in philosophy of mind or early modern. Useful corrective to anachronistic readings of Cartesian dualism in contemporary philosophy of mind. |
| `[Reference]` Descartes — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Descartes*, 1992 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **John Cottingham** (ed.) | One of the outstanding Cambridge Companion volumes: 14 essays by leading Descartes scholars (Garber on the physics, Hatfield on the senses, Sorell on theology, Cottingham on the *cogito*, Wilson on the soul, Williams's own contribution on the *Meditations* themselves). Cottingham's introduction is a substantial orientation. Limitation: now over thirty years old; the substantial recent work on Descartes's natural philosophy (Garber, Schmaltz, Slowik) and on his place in the scholastic-mechanist transition (Des Chene) is later. The *Oxford Handbook of Descartes and Cartesianism* (Nadler, Schmaltz, Antoine-Mahut, eds., 2019) is current and broader. | Graduate students orienting in Descartes scholarship. Read alongside the *Meditations* and Williams. |

### 3.2 Spinoza — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Spinoza — complete works | *The Collected Works of Spinoza*, 2 vols., 1985 and 2016 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Baruch Spinoza**; trans. **Edwin Curley** (Princeton) | The standard scholarly English edition. Vol. 1 (1985) contains the *Short Treatise*, the *Treatise on the Emendation of the Intellect*, *Descartes's "Principles of Philosophy"*, the *Metaphysical Thoughts*, the *Ethics*, and early correspondence. Vol. 2 (2016) contains the *Theological-Political Treatise* (TTP), the unfinished *Political Treatise*, the *Hebrew Grammar*, and later correspondence. Curley's translation is uniformly the scholarly default for citation; his apparatus integrates with Curley's own *Behind the Geometrical Method* commentary. **Recommended translation:** Curley for citation and serious work; Shirley (Hackett, 2002, with introduction by Garrett) is a fluent and reliable alternative for the *Ethics* if one wants a less austere translation. Bennett's *Early Modern Texts* translations are useful for orientation but should not be cited. | Owned by anyone working on Spinoza. Vol. 1 contains the *Ethics* — Spinoza's central philosophical work and the only one he wrote for publication; Vol. 2 contains the political and religious works that have driven the major revival of Spinoza scholarship since 2000. See also SEP: "Baruch Spinoza." |
| `[Secondary]` Spinoza — *Ethics* commentary `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *A Study of Spinoza's Ethics*, 1984 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jonathan Bennett** | The landmark analytic reconstruction of the *Ethics*: Bennett works through the propositional structure proposition by proposition, reconstructing Spinoza's arguments and assessing where they succeed and where they don't. The treatment of Spinoza's substance monism, parallelism, the conatus doctrine, and the theory of the affects (Parts III–IV) is the most rigorous in English. The book is also a methodological provocation — Bennett openly substitutes his own arguments for Spinoza's where he finds Spinoza's reasoning defective, and the result is sometimes a different philosophy than Spinoza's own. Limitation: precisely the methodological substitution Bennett practices; readers will need supplementary readings (Della Rocca, Garrett, Curley) to recover what Spinoza was doing rather than what Bennett's Spinoza is. | Graduate students in early modern philosophy. The standard secondary text against which other readings of the *Ethics* define themselves. Pair with the Curley *Collected Works* (geometrical structure of the *Ethics*) and with Della Rocca below. |
| `[Secondary]` Spinoza — single-author overview `[Analytic]` | *Spinoza* (Routledge Philosophers series), 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Michael Della Rocca** | The best single-volume introduction to Spinoza's philosophy in English, organised around the Principle of Sufficient Reason (PSR) as the architectonic principle that Della Rocca argues unifies Spinoza's entire system. Della Rocca's reading is interpretively bold — making the PSR more central than most scholars accept — but exceptionally clear and philosophically engaging. Chapters cover substance and modes, the human mind, the affects, ethics and politics, and religion and the state. Limitation: the PSR-monism framework is contested; readers should compare with Curley's *Behind the Geometrical Method* (1988) for a less radical reading. | Read alongside the *Ethics* itself. The current best entry-level secondary text. See also Curley's *Behind the Geometrical Method* (Princeton, 1988) for the historical-contextualist alternative. |
| `[Reference]` Spinoza — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Spinoza*, 2nd ed., 2018 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Don Garrett** (ed.) | The standard orientation volume. The 2nd edition substantially updates the 1996 first edition and reflects the post-2000 boom in Spinoza scholarship (the TTP, the political philosophy, the affects, the metaphysics of attributes). Strong essays from Garrett himself on Spinoza's epistemology and ethics, from Della Rocca on parallelism, from Steinberg on political philosophy, and from Nadler on religion. Limitation: like all such volumes, uneven across chapters; some essays are major contributions and others are competent surveys. | Graduate students entering Spinoza scholarship. Pair with the *Oxford Handbook of Spinoza* (Della Rocca, ed., 2017) for the broadest current research overview. |

### 3.3 Leibniz — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Leibniz — philosophical essays selection | *Philosophical Essays*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. W. Leibniz**; trans. **Roger Ariew & Daniel Garber** (Hackett) | The standard English selection from Leibniz's vast and dispersed corpus: includes the *Discourse on Metaphysics* (1686), the correspondence with Arnauld, the *New System*, the *Monadology* (1714), the *Principles of Nature and Grace*, and selections from the *Theodicy*, with a chronological organisation that allows the reader to follow the development of Leibniz's mature metaphysics from the mid-1680s to his death. Leibniz never wrote a systematic treatise — his philosophy is reconstructed from essays, correspondence, and short pieces — so any single-volume "Leibniz" is necessarily a constructed object; Ariew and Garber's selection is the most useful one available in English. **Recommended translation:** Ariew–Garber (Hackett, 1989) for selection and citation; Loemker's earlier *Philosophical Papers and Letters* (Reidel, 1969) is more complete but harder to obtain and uses dated translations. The Academy edition (*Sämtliche Schriften und Briefe*) is the critical Latin/French/German edition, still in progress after a century. | Anyone working on Leibniz. Read the *Discourse on Metaphysics* and the Arnauld correspondence first — these contain the substance-and-accident metaphysics in its most accessible form. The *Monadology* and *Principles of Nature and Grace* are the late summaries. See also SEP: "Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz." |
| `[Primary]` Leibniz — engagement with Locke `[Bridge]` | *New Essays on Human Understanding*, written c. 1704, published 1765 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. W. Leibniz**; trans. **Peter Remnant & Jonathan Bennett** (Cambridge, 1996) | Leibniz's response to Locke's *Essay Concerning Human Understanding*, structured as a dialogue between Philalethes (Lockean) and Theophilus (Leibnizian) running through Locke's text book by book. Leibniz declined to publish it after Locke's death in 1704, and it appeared only posthumously in 1765 — by which time Kant was already at work. The text is the major bridge between the so-called rationalist and empiricist traditions: Leibniz responds to Locke as a serious interlocutor, defending innate ideas (rightly understood), the active soul, the principle of indiscernibles, and the doctrine of small perceptions. The dialogue form makes it more readable than most of Leibniz's other writings. Limitation: structured around Locke's text and therefore takes Lockean issues in Lockean order, which is not always Leibniz's natural order. **Recommended translation:** Remnant–Bennett (Cambridge, 1996) — clear, scholarly, with substantial introduction. | Excellent text for studying Locke and Leibniz together; the explicit response makes their differences visible in a way the standalone works do not. See also §3.4 on Locke. |
| `[Secondary]` Leibniz — comprehensive philosophical study `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Leibniz: Determinist, Theist, Idealist*, 1994 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Robert Merrihew Adams** | The single major work of Leibniz scholarship in English of the past half-century. Adams reconstructs Leibniz's metaphysics under three labels — determinism (about the actual world's contingency), theism (about God's relation to created substances), and idealism (about the ultimate constitution of reality from monads) — and demonstrates the philosophical coherence and depth of the resulting system. Particularly important on the theory of contingency (Leibniz's reconciliation of universal determinism with non-necessitarianism), on the principle of sufficient reason, on the doctrine of pre-established harmony, and on the relation between monads and bodies. Limitation: dense and demanding; not a first reading on Leibniz. Garber's *Leibniz: Body, Substance, Monad* (Oxford, 2009) is the major alternative, focused specifically on the body/substance relation and arguing against the standard "idealist" reading of Leibniz. | Researchers and advanced graduate students. Read after the Ariew–Garber selection. The Garber book is the essential complement — reading the two against each other is itself a graduate education in Leibniz interpretation. |
| `[Secondary]` Leibniz — orientation `[Anthology]` `[Reference]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Leibniz*, 1995 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Nicholas Jolley** (ed.) | The standard orientation volume: 12 essays covering metaphysics, philosophy of mind, theology, physics, logic, mathematics, and political thought. Adams's chapter on metaphysics summarises themes from his *Determinist, Theist, Idealist* (above). Limitation: now thirty years old; the post-1995 work on Leibniz's natural philosophy (Garber, Antognazza, Phemister) and on his political thought (Riley, Mulvaney) is not represented. For current state of the field consult Antognazza's *Leibniz: An Intellectual Biography* (Cambridge, 2009) and her *Oxford Handbook of Leibniz* (2018). | Graduate students entering Leibniz scholarship. Cross-reference with the corresponding entries in Garber & Ayers's *Cambridge History of Seventeenth-Century Philosophy* (§3.7). |

### 3.4 Locke — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Locke — epistemology, metaphysics, philosophy of mind | *An Essay Concerning Human Understanding*, 1689 (4th ed. 1700) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Locke**; ed. **Peter H. Nidditch** (Clarendon, 1975) | The founding work of British empiricism and one of the most influential philosophical books ever written: four books treating the origins of ideas (against innatism), the nature of ideas (simple and complex, primary and secondary qualities, substance, mode, relation), language, and knowledge. Book II contains the classical discussions of personal identity, free will, substance, and the inner sense; Book III is the founding work of modern philosophy of language; Book IV develops Locke's theory of knowledge as the perception of agreement and disagreement among ideas. Limitation: Locke's prose is digressive and the *Essay* is sometimes inconsistent — Locke revised the work substantially across four editions and the seams sometimes show; this is itself part of what makes the text interesting. **Recommended edition:** Nidditch's Clarendon edition (1975) is the scholarly standard, based on the 4th edition with the textual variants noted. For a less expensive edition for self-study, Winkler's abridgement (Hackett, 1996) is reliable. Bennett's *Early Modern Texts* version is useful for orientation only. | Read in full by any serious philosophy student. Book II is the metaphysics-and-mind core; Book IV the epistemology. Pair with Leibniz's *New Essays* (§3.3) for the systematic alternative response. See also SEP: "John Locke." |
| `[Primary]` Locke — political philosophy | *Two Treatises of Government*, 1689 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Locke**; ed. **Peter Laslett** (Cambridge, 1988; rev. ed. 1991) | The founding text of liberal political philosophy in the English-speaking world: the First Treatise refutes Filmer's patriarchal absolutism; the Second develops Locke's positive theory of the state of nature, natural rights to life, liberty, and property (the labour theory of acquisition), the social contract, the right of revolution, and the limits of legitimate political authority. The book's influence on the American constitutional tradition and on subsequent liberal theory (Nozick, Rawls in some respects, the libertarian tradition generally) is foundational. Laslett's introduction reshaped Locke scholarship by establishing the *Two Treatises* as a 1681–1683 composition (rather than a 1688 defence of the Glorious Revolution) and as part of Locke's engagement with the Exclusion Crisis. Limitation: Locke's treatment of property and labour (the famous "spoilage" and "enough and as good" provisos) is contested in its philosophical force and has been criticised for its colonial implications (notably by Tully — see below). **Recommended edition:** Laslett (Cambridge, 1988) is the scholarly standard. | Read in full by any student of political philosophy. The Second Treatise is the philosophically essential half. Pair with Dunn (below) for the contextual reading and with §8.10 (political philosophy). |
| `[Secondary]` Locke — *Essay* commentary `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Problems from Locke*, 1976 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. L. Mackie** | The classical analytic engagement with Locke's *Essay*: Mackie selects six problems (primary and secondary qualities, representative perception, abstract general ideas, substance, personal identity, real and nominal essences) and works each through carefully, showing both how Locke argued and how the problems look from a current vantage point. The chapter on personal identity remains foundational for that contemporary debate (cf. §6.8). Limitation: as the title suggests, the book takes problems *from* Locke rather than offering a full commentary on the *Essay*; readers wanting fuller coverage should consult Lowe's *Locke* (Routledge, 2005) or Jolley's *Locke* (1999). | Graduate students in early modern or in personal identity. Read after a first pass through Locke's *Essay*. |
| `[Secondary]` Locke — political philosophy in context `[Continental]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Political Thought of John Locke*, 1969 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Dunn** | The Cambridge School historical-contextualist reading of Locke: Dunn argues that Locke's political theory is unintelligible without its theological foundation (the doctrine of human beings as God's workmanship and the natural law as divine command), and that the modern liberal Locke is a 19th- and 20th-century construction. The book displaced the dominant Macpherson reading (Locke as proto-bourgeois ideologist) and reshaped Locke studies. Dunn's argument is at once philosophical and historiographical — about what Locke meant and about what reading him properly demands. Limitation: Dunn's polemical framing has sometimes obscured the philosophical question of what survives of Locke once the theological grounding is rejected; later work by Waldron, Tully, and Simmons explores this. | Graduate students in political philosophy and the history of political thought. The major counterpart to Mackie above: Mackie reads Locke as a still-engageable analytic philosopher, Dunn reads him as a 17th-century theologian-political theorist. The contrast is exactly the §1.5 methodological choice in concrete form. |
| `[Reference]` Locke — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Locke*, 1994 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Vere Chappell** (ed.) | The standard orientation volume: 11 essays covering the life, the *Essay* (chapters on ideas, knowledge, primary/secondary qualities, substance, personal identity), the *Two Treatises* (Tully on property, Ashcraft on the political context), and Locke's epistemology of religion. Limitation: now thirty years old; the substantial newer work on Locke's natural philosophy (Anstey, 2011) and on the relation between his epistemology and his political theory (Forde, Stuart-Buttle) is not included. The *Cambridge Companion to Locke's Essay Concerning Human Understanding* (Newman, ed., 2007) is a focused alternative for the *Essay* alone. | Graduate students orienting in Locke scholarship. Read alongside both the *Essay* and the *Two Treatises*. |

### 3.5 Berkeley — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Berkeley — immaterialism | *A Treatise Concerning the Principles of Human Knowledge*, 1710, and *Three Dialogues between Hylas and Philonous*, 1713 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **George Berkeley**; ed. **Roger Woolhouse** (Penguin, 1988) or **Howard Robinson** (Oxford, 1996) | Berkeley's two presentations of the same philosophical position: immaterialism — the doctrine that there are no mind-independent material substances, only minds and their ideas, with the regularity of nature explained by God's continuous causation of sensible ideas in finite minds. The *Principles* is the systematic presentation; the *Three Dialogues* the more accessible Socratic exposition. Berkeley's arguments — especially the master argument against the conceivability of unperceived objects (*Principles* §22–23 and *Three Dialogues* I) and the regress argument against material substance — are exemplary pieces of analytic-style argument from a philosopher 250 years before analytic philosophy. Limitation: the master argument is widely regarded as containing a fallacy (or at best as requiring controversial assumptions Berkeley does not make explicit), and the positive theology that takes up the slack from rejecting matter is doing heavy work. **Recommended edition:** the Woolhouse Penguin combines both works with a useful introduction; for scholarly work consult the Luce–Jessop *Works of George Berkeley* (Nelson, 9 vols., 1948–1957). | Excellent self-study primary text — Berkeley's prose is the clearest and most accessible of the major early moderns, and the *Three Dialogues* can be read in an afternoon. Read with Winkler (below) as commentary. See also SEP: "George Berkeley." |
| `[Primary]` Berkeley — theory of vision | *An Essay towards a New Theory of Vision*, 1709 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **George Berkeley** (in Luce–Jessop *Works* Vol. 1, or standalone Hackett edition) | Berkeley's earliest major work, treating the philosophy of perception specifically. The argument that distance is not immediately perceived by vision but inferred from cues correlating visual and tactile sensations — the *heterogeneity thesis* (visible and tangible objects are numerically distinct) — anticipates much later work in psychology of perception and is independent of the immaterialism of the *Principles*. The text is the bridge between Berkeley's optics and his metaphysics, and it remains influential in the philosophy and psychology of vision. Limitation: largely supplanted in its empirical claims by 19th- and 20th-century vision science, but the philosophical arguments about cross-modal perception retain force. | Useful for readers in philosophy of perception or psychology of vision (cf. Table 7 and the Psychology Atlas if applicable). Read after the *Principles*. |
| `[Secondary]` Berkeley — comprehensive interpretation `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Berkeley: An Interpretation*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Kenneth P. Winkler** | The major late-20th-century philosophical study of Berkeley's metaphysics: Winkler reconstructs Berkeley's idealism with full attention to its theological grounding, defends a particular reading of the master argument, and treats both the *Principles* and the *Three Dialogues* in detail. The book reshaped the field by taking Berkeley seriously as a metaphysician with arguments rather than as a curiosity in the empiricist tradition. Limitation: focused on the immaterialist core; readers wanting fuller treatment of Berkeley's later religious and philosophical writings (*Alciphron*, *Siris*) should consult Berman or Stoneham. | Graduate students. The starting point for serious work on Berkeley. Pair with Stoneham's *Berkeley's World* (Oxford, 2002) for an alternative metaphysical interpretation. |

### 3.6 Hume — Primary Texts & Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Hume — early systematic philosophy | *A Treatise of Human Nature*, 1739–1740 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Hume**; ed. **David Fate Norton & Mary J. Norton** (Clarendon Critical Edition, 2007; student edition Oxford Philosophical Texts, 2000) | Hume's first and longest philosophical work, published when he was 28 and largely ignored at the time. Book I (Of the Understanding) contains the most ambitious analytic-skeptical project in 18th-century philosophy: the theory of impressions and ideas, causation as constant conjunction and projected necessary connection, scepticism about induction, the externalist account of belief, and the famous "destruction" of the self at the end of Book I. Book II treats the passions; Book III lays out the sentimentalist moral theory (against ethical rationalism) with the founding statement of the is/ought problem (III.i.1). The first *Enquiry* (below) is the more polished restatement of Book I, but the *Treatise* contains material — particularly the discussions of personal identity, of the self, and of the passions — not found in the *Enquiries*. Limitation: the *Treatise* itself is in some places more obscure than the *Enquiries*, and Hume in late life formally repudiated it as "juvenile" — a repudiation scholars no longer accept at face value. **Recommended edition:** Norton and Norton's *Clarendon Critical Edition* (2007) for scholarly work; their student edition (Oxford, 2000) for course use. The older Selby-Bigge edition is superseded. | Read in full by any serious philosophy student. Book I is the philosophically densest; Book III essential for ethics; Book II often neglected but containing important material on emotion and motivation. See also SEP: "David Hume." |
| `[Primary]` Hume — mature epistemology and ethics | *An Enquiry concerning Human Understanding*, 1748, and *An Enquiry concerning the Principles of Morals*, 1751 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Hume**; ed. **Tom L. Beauchamp** (Clarendon Critical Editions, 2000 and 1998; student editions Oxford Philosophical Texts) | Hume's mature restatement of the core epistemological and moral arguments from the *Treatise*, in a more polished prose for a general educated readership. The first *Enquiry* contains the canonical statements of Hume's empiricism (Section II), the problem of induction (IV–V), the analysis of causation (VII), the famous Section X on miracles, the discussion of liberty and necessity (VIII), and Hume's mitigated scepticism (XII). The second *Enquiry* (EPM) is Hume's preferred presentation of his moral theory and considered by Hume himself his best work; it is structurally simpler than Book III of the *Treatise* but loses some of the systematic articulation. The first *Enquiry* should be read in addition to — not instead of — *Treatise* Book I; the second *Enquiry* can serve as the entry point to Hume's ethics if one is not reading the full *Treatise*. **Recommended edition:** Beauchamp's *Clarendon Critical Editions* (2000, 1998) are now the scholarly standard; the Oxford Philosophical Texts paperbacks are the affordable alternative. | The natural starting point for self-study of Hume — more accessible than the *Treatise*. Pair the first *Enquiry* with Stroud (below). |
| `[Primary]` Hume — philosophy of religion | *Dialogues Concerning Natural Religion*, written by 1751, published posthumously 1779 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Hume**; ed. **Dorothy Coleman** (Cambridge Texts, 2007) or **Norman Kemp Smith** (1947) | Hume's final philosophical work and the most artful philosophical dialogue in English: three speakers — Demea (orthodox rationalist theist), Cleanthes (natural theologian, design argument), and Philo (sceptic) — debate the existence and nature of God. The text demolishes the design argument (anticipating much that Darwin would make decisive a century later), undermines the cosmological argument, and leaves the reader genuinely uncertain about Hume's positive position (the famous Section XII ambiguity over Philo's apparent concessions). Hume withheld the work from publication during his lifetime out of concern for his reputation and his nieces' inheritance, arranging for posthumous publication by his nephew. Limitation: the dialogue form complicates attribution of Hume's own views — the canonical scholarly debate (Kemp Smith vs. Capaldi vs. Gaskin) on whether Philo's positive concessions are sincere or ironic is unresolved. **Recommended edition:** Coleman (Cambridge Texts, 2007) for current scholarship with substantial introduction. | Required reading for philosophy of religion. Short (~100 pp.) and the most readable of Hume's major works after the second *Enquiry*. See also Gaskin's *Hume's Philosophy of Religion* (1988). |
| `[Secondary]` Hume — philosophical study `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Hume* (Arguments of the Philosophers series), 1977 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Barry Stroud** | The standard analytic study of Hume in English: book-length treatment of causation, induction, the external world, the self, the passions, ethics, and religion. Stroud reads Hume as a naturalist whose scepticism is the *result* rather than the project of his philosophical inquiry — an interpretation now broadly accepted. The chapters on causation and on the external world are particular successes. Stroud's prose is uncommonly clear and the book remains the best entry-level secondary text on Hume. Limitation: now nearly fifty years old; the substantial recent work on Hume's positive epistemology (Garrett, Owen), on his political philosophy, and on his relation to the Scottish Enlightenment is later. | Read alongside the *Enquiries* and *Treatise*. The Routledge/Routledge Philosophers replacement (Don Garrett, 2014) is also reliable but Stroud remains preferable. |
| `[Secondary]` Hume — research-level interpretation `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Cognition and Commitment in Hume's Philosophy*, 1997 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Don Garrett** | The major contemporary research monograph on Hume's epistemology: Garrett argues that Hume's apparent scepticism and his apparent naturalism are reconciled through Hume's account of the inevitable cognitive commitments imposed by the mind's natural operations. The treatment of induction, of the senses' belief in body, and of the self has reshaped Anglophone Hume scholarship. Garrett's reading is the major contemporary alternative to the older Kemp Smith naturalist reading and to the older Hendel-Stroud sceptical reading. Limitation: technically demanding; not a first reading on Hume. | Graduate students and researchers in early modern epistemology. Read after Stroud and a first pass through the *Treatise*. |

### 3.7 The Early Modern Period — Surveys & Thematic Studies

The category of "early modern philosophy" itself, and the canonical six-philosopher narrative (Descartes, Spinoza, Leibniz, Locke, Berkeley, Hume) are 19th-century inheritances — refined by Kant's retrospective opposition of dogmatic rationalism and inconclusive empiricism, and then by the German textbook tradition. Neither Leibniz nor Hume thought of themselves as members of opposed "rationalist" and "empiricist" camps; the entire framing systematically marginalises figures (Hobbes, Malebranche, Cudworth, Bayle, Cavendish, du Châtelet) whose work the period actually included. The texts below both employ and contest this framework.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Reference]` Early modern philosophy — comprehensive reference `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge History of Seventeenth-Century Philosophy*, 2 vols., 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Daniel Garber & Michael Ayers** (eds.) | The standard scholarly reference for 17th-century philosophy: roughly 1,500 pages of thematic chapters by leading specialists, organised by topic (knowledge, body, soul, ethics, politics, religion, natural philosophy) rather than by figure. The thematic organisation is itself the methodological innovation — it makes visible the cross-figure debates (e.g. on the nature of body, on the soul, on the foundations of morality) that the figure-by-figure narrative obscures. Garber's chapters on natural philosophy and Ayers's on knowledge are particular contributions. Limitation: a reference rather than a continuous read; expensive; the 18th century is covered by Haakonssen's separate *Cambridge History of Eighteenth-Century Philosophy* (2 vols., 2006), which is similarly thematic and similarly authoritative. | The library reference for serious work on the 17th century. Use as scaffolding before reading primary texts on cross-figure topics. |
| `[Secondary]` Historiography of early modern `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *From Descartes to Hume: Continental Metaphysics and the Development of Modern Philosophy*, 1981 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Louis E. Loeb** | The classical critique of the rationalism/empiricism narrative: Loeb argues that the philosophically substantive division of the early modern period is between *Cartesian* and *anti-Cartesian* metaphysics — with Leibniz on the Cartesian side and Hobbes, Locke, and Hume on the anti-Cartesian side. The book reorganises the period around the question of substance and shows that the standard "rationalism" / "empiricism" frame both groups and separates philosophers wrongly. Limitation: Loeb's own positive framework (Cartesian vs. anti-Cartesian) is also a thesis and has not won universal acceptance — but the negative argument against the standard frame has been broadly accepted. | Graduate students in early modern. Essential reading for anyone wondering whether the rationalism/empiricism textbook narrative actually captures what happened. |
| `[Systematic]` Continuity with the medieval `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *Metaphysical Themes 1274–1671*, 2011 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Robert Pasnau** | The major recent study of the *continuity* between medieval scholasticism and early modern metaphysics: Pasnau covers substance, accidents, modes, qualities, and forms across four centuries, showing that early modern positions (Descartes's, Locke's, Leibniz's) are best read as responses to and transformations of late medieval debates rather than as ground-up innovations. The book has reshaped how early modern philosophy is taught and historiographically situated. Limitation: massive (~800 pp.) and demanding; presupposes substantial prior background in both periods. | Researchers in metaphysics or the history of philosophy who want the long view. Pair with Marenbon (§2.8) for the medieval framework. |
| `[Secondary]` The rationalist tradition — taxonomic introduction `[Self-Study]` | *The Rationalists: Descartes, Spinoza, Leibniz*, 1988 (Past Masters series) `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **John Cottingham** | A short, accessible introduction to the three so-called rationalists, treating each in turn and the rationalist tradition as a whole. Cottingham is a leading Descartes scholar and the book is reliable on each figure. Importantly, Cottingham is open about the constructed character of "rationalism" as a category. Limitation: introductory; for any depth, move directly to the primary texts and the figure-specific secondary literature. Its companion volume in the same Oxford series, **R. S. Woolhouse**'s *The Empiricists* (1988), provides a complementary brief introduction to Locke, Berkeley, and Hume. | Beginning students wanting orientation; readers comparing the two traditional groupings. Cottingham and Woolhouse together provide the conventional textbook framework — useful as orientation, then to be critically tested against Loeb and Pasnau above. |

### 3.8 Philosophy & the Scientific Revolution

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Metaphysics of modern science `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Metaphysical Foundations of Modern Physical Science*, 1924 (rev. 1932) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **E. A. Burtt** | The classical study of the metaphysical commitments built into modern physical science from Copernicus through Newton: the mathematisation of nature, the primary/secondary qualities distinction, the elimination of final causes, the relegation of mind to a residual epiphenomenal position. Burtt's central thesis — that the scientific revolution involved not the abandonment of metaphysics but its substitution by a new (Platonic-Pythagorean) metaphysics — has been influential ever since and was foundational for the historiographical schools of Koyré and Kuhn. Limitation: written in 1924, before the major archival recoveries on Newton (especially the alchemical and theological manuscripts) reshaped Newton studies; some of Burtt's specific claims are now superseded, but the framework remains. | Anyone working at the intersection of philosophy and the history of science. Read before Koyré's *From the Closed World to the Infinite Universe* and before more recent work on the scientific revolution. Connects to the Physics Atlas treatments of classical mechanics. |
| `[Research]` Theology and natural philosophy `[Research]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Theology and the Scientific Imagination from the Middle Ages to the Seventeenth Century*, 1986 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Amos Funkenstein** | A landmark work tracing how theological categories (divine omnipotence, divine ideas, the infinite, the contingent created order) shaped the development of natural philosophy from late scholasticism through Newton. Funkenstein argues that early modern science is unintelligible without its theological context — and that the secular self-image of modern science is itself a product of specifically theological developments. The treatment of divine omnipotence and the contingency of natural laws is particularly important for understanding the philosophical background to laws-of-nature debates (cf. §6.9). Limitation: the book ranges across an enormous span; some of the source-base is more thoroughly worked than other parts. | Researchers in philosophy of science, history of science, or philosophical theology. Pair with Pasnau (§3.7) for the metaphysical thread and with Janiak below for the Newtonian terminus. |
| `[Research]` Newton as philosopher `[Bridge]` `[Research]` | *Newton as Philosopher*, 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Andrew Janiak** | The major contemporary study of Newton's philosophical thought — distinct from but inseparable from his physics. Janiak treats Newton's methodology, his metaphysics of space and time, the absolute/relative distinction in *Principia* Scholium, the famous "I feign no hypotheses" passage and the methodology behind it, the doctrine of universal gravitation as occult quality vs. mathematical description, and Newton's correspondence with Leibniz (through Clarke). The book corrects the picture of Newton as a mere natural philosopher uninterested in metaphysics. Limitation: focused on the canonical philosophical aspects of Newton; the alchemical and theological manuscripts (extensively published since the 1990s) receive less attention than they could. **Companion reading:** Janiak's edition of *Newton: Philosophical Writings* (Cambridge, 2nd ed. 2014). | Researchers at the philosophy–physics border. Connects directly to Table 6 (laws of nature, modality) and to the Physics Atlas's treatments of Newtonian mechanics and the Newton–Leibniz space debate. |
| `[Secondary]` Historiography of the scientific revolution `[Bridge]` | *The Scientific Revolution*, 1996 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Steven Shapin** | The provocative short overview (~250 pp.) of the historiographical state of the scientific revolution as of the mid-1990s. Shapin opens with the deliberately polemical claim "There was no such thing as the Scientific Revolution, and this is a book about it," and uses the paradox to walk the reader through the social, institutional, religious, and political contexts in which what we *call* the scientific revolution took place. The book is the most accessible introduction to the sociology-of-science approaches (the Edinburgh school, Latour, Shapin's own collaborative work with Schaffer on Hobbes and Boyle). Limitation: deliberately provocative and one-sided; readers should pair with a more traditional historiographical treatment (Cohen's *The Scientific Revolution: A Historiographical Inquiry*, 1994). | Readers in philosophy of science wanting the post-Koyré, post-Kuhn historiographical landscape. Helpful as a corrective to the philosopher's tendency to read the scientific revolution as a purely intellectual event. |

### Connections to other tables

The translation conventions established in this table become contested in different ways in Table 4 (the Kant translation problem is among the most consequential in the philosophical canon). The Locke/Leibniz exchange in the *Essay* / *New Essays* is the most directly readable example of cross-tradition philosophical engagement in the period, and forms the natural prelude to Kant's project of accommodating both sides (Table 4). Hume's *Treatise* Book I and *Enquiry* are the texts Kant credited with awakening him from his dogmatic slumber, and are the indispensable background to the *Critique of Pure Reason*. The *Treatise* discussions of personal identity and the self connect to §6.8 (personal identity), and the is/ought passage to §8.7 (metaethics). Locke's *Two Treatises* is the founding document of the liberal political tradition addressed in §8.9 and §8.10. The scientific revolution section (§3.8) connects to the Physics Atlas treatments of Newtonian mechanics, absolute vs. relative space, and the philosophical commitments of classical mechanics; Burtt and Funkenstein in particular are background reading for any historically aware philosophy of physics. The historiographical critique in §3.7 (Loeb, Pasnau) connects to the broader methodological discussion of §1.5.

# Table 4 · Kant & Post-Kantian Philosophy (German Idealism to Nietzsche)

*Scope:* Kant's three Critiques and practical philosophy, the German Idealist response (Fichte, Schelling, Hegel), the anti-idealist reactions (Schopenhauer, Kierkegaard, Marx), and Nietzsche. Translation choice is consequential throughout — for Kant, for Hegel, and for the Heidegger-influenced reception of Nietzsche.

### 4.1 Kant — *Critique of Pure Reason*: Primary Text & Translation

**The Kant translation problem.** The *Critique of Pure Reason* exists in two substantially different editions: A (1781) and B (1787). The Transcendental Deduction was largely rewritten between editions, and other passages (the Paralogisms, the Refutation of Idealism added in B) differ significantly. **Norman Kemp Smith**'s translation (Macmillan, 1929) was the English standard for seventy years and shaped much of the early 20th-century reception — its tendency to interpret as it translates (notably in the handling of *Anschauung*, *Vorstellung*, and *Verstand*) has been documented by later scholars. **Paul Guyer and Allen Wood**'s translation (Cambridge, 1998) is now the scholarly standard: it prints A and B passages in parallel, is more literal, and integrates with the broader Cambridge Edition of the Works of Immanuel Kant. **Werner Pluhar**'s translation (Hackett, 1996) is a reasonable alternative for student use. Citations in scholarly literature use the A/B pagination of the German Akademie edition, not page numbers in any translation.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Kant — theoretical philosophy | *Critique of Pure Reason*, A 1781 / B 1787 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Immanuel Kant**; trans. **Paul Guyer & Allen W. Wood** (Cambridge, 1998) | The book that closes the early modern period and opens the next two centuries of Western philosophy: a sustained inquiry into the conditions of the possibility of synthetic a priori knowledge, comprising the Transcendental Aesthetic (space and time as forms of intuition), the Transcendental Analytic (the categories, the schematism, the principles of pure understanding, the deduction of the categories, the refutation of idealism), and the Transcendental Dialectic (the critique of rational psychology, rational cosmology, and rational theology — the Paralogisms, Antinomies, and the critique of the proofs of God's existence). The argumentative complexity is genuinely extreme; the architectonic (with its preferred fourfold tables of categories, judgments, etc.) is partly artificial and obstructs the underlying argument; and the doctrine of transcendental idealism — that we know appearances rather than things in themselves — is interpretively contested at the deepest level (the *two-aspect* vs. *two-world* reading is the central scholarly division). **Recommended translation:** Guyer–Wood (Cambridge, 1998) — current standard; Kemp Smith (1929) is the historical reference still cited in older literature. Use the A/B pagination of the German Akademie edition for all references. | The single most difficult text in the modern philosophical canon along with Hegel's *Logic*. Never read in isolation: always with at least Gardner or Allison (§4.2) open alongside. See also SEP: "Kant's Transcendental Idealism" and "Kant's Critique of Pure Reason." |
| `[Reference]` Kant — complete works | *The Cambridge Edition of the Works of Immanuel Kant*, 16 vols., 1992–present `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Immanuel Kant**; ed. **Paul Guyer & Allen W. Wood** (general editors), with multiple translators | The scholarly English edition of Kant's complete works, replacing piecemeal translations of varying quality with a coordinated and consistent corpus. Particularly important volumes: *Practical Philosophy* (Gregor, 1996) — *Groundwork*, *CPrR*, *Metaphysics of Morals*, and the political essays in one volume; *Critique of the Power of Judgment* (Guyer & Matthews, 2000); *Religion and Rational Theology* (Wood & di Giovanni, 1996); *Theoretical Philosophy 1755–1770* and *Theoretical Philosophy after 1781*; the *Lectures* on logic, ethics, metaphysics, anthropology, and religion; and the *Correspondence*. Translation terminology is harmonised across the series. Limitation: expensive in print; many volumes are now available individually as Cambridge Texts paperbacks. | Any researcher in Kant. Replace older standalone translations (Beck's *Critique of Practical Reason*, the Greene–Hudson *Religion*, etc.) with the Cambridge versions for serious work. |

### 4.2 Kant — *Critique of Pure Reason*: Commentary

Reading the *CPR* requires substantial secondary support, and the literature is large and uneven. A workable hierarchy: **Gardner** (introductory but accurate) for first reading; **Allison** as the standard scholarly companion; **Strawson** for the analytic reconstruction (with the awareness that its "double neglect" — of the systematic structure and of transcendental idealism — is real); **Longuenesse** for the most demanding recent reading; **Bird** for the major alternative interpretation rejecting Strawson's two-world reading.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Secondary]` *CPR* — introductory commentary `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Kant and the Critique of Pure Reason*, 1999 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Sebastian Gardner** | The best entry-level commentary on the first *Critique*: Gardner works through the text in order, providing both exposition and philosophical assessment. The treatment of the Transcendental Aesthetic and the Deduction is particularly clear; the discussion of transcendental idealism distinguishes the major interpretive options without forcing a choice. Limitation: introductory in depth — Gardner takes the reader to the threshold of the major scholarly debates but does not enter them. | The first secondary text to read alongside the *CPR* itself. Pair with Allison for depth. |
| `[Secondary]` *CPR* — major commentary `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Kant's Transcendental Idealism: An Interpretation and Defense*, rev. ed. 2004 (1st ed. 1983) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Henry E. Allison** | The standard English-language commentary on the *CPR* of the past forty years. Allison defends the *epistemic two-aspect* reading of transcendental idealism — appearances and things-in-themselves as two ways of considering the same objects, not two ontologically separate domains — against the Strawsonian two-world reading. The revised edition (2004) substantially rewrites the original to address objections; the chapters on the Transcendental Deduction, the Schematism, and the Antinomies are model exercises in close reading combined with philosophical reconstruction. Limitation: the two-aspect reading is interpretively contested (notably by Guyer, Van Cleve, and Langton on different grounds), and Allison sometimes makes Kant's position seem more coherent than the text strictly warrants. | Graduate students and researchers in Kant. The book to engage with after a first pass with Gardner. See also SEP: "Kant's Transcendental Idealism." |
| `[Secondary]` *CPR* — analytic reconstruction `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Bounds of Sense: An Essay on Kant's Critique of Pure Reason*, 1966 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **P. F. Strawson** | The founding work of post-war analytic Kant scholarship and the model of how to read a historical text in the rational-reconstruction mode. Strawson extracts what he takes to be Kant's central transcendental arguments (the objectivity argument of the Deduction, the temporal arguments of the Analogies, the Refutation of Idealism) and assesses them in their own right, while explicitly setting aside what he calls Kant's "metaphysics of transcendental idealism." The result has been enormously influential — and the explicit setting-aside is also the book's central limitation: Strawson's "Kant" lacks transcendental idealism, which is to say lacks the framework that makes the transcendental arguments do the work Kant intended. The "double neglect" charge — that Strawson neglects both the systematic structure and transcendental idealism — is now standard, but the book remains essential for the arguments it does reconstruct. | Graduate students in epistemology or analytic philosophy approaching Kant. Read with awareness of what Strawson has bracketed. |
| `[Secondary]` *CPR* — Transcendental Deduction `[Analytic]` `[Continental]` `[Research]` | *Kant and the Capacity to Judge: Sensibility and Discursivity in the Transcendental Analytic of the Critique of Pure Reason*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Béatrice Longuenesse**; trans. **Charles T. Wolfe** | The major recent reconstruction of the Transcendental Analytic, focused on the relation between Kant's logical doctrine of judgment and his transcendental philosophy. Longuenesse argues that the unity of the faculty of judgment governs both the table of logical forms and the table of categories, and that this dual function is the key to the Deduction. The book reshaped Kant scholarship by reopening the systematic relation between logic and transcendental philosophy in Kant. Limitation: demanding and assumes substantial prior Kant background; the engagement with German Kant scholarship is dense. | Researchers in Kant's theoretical philosophy. Read after Allison; not a first commentary. |
| `[Secondary]` *CPR* — alternative interpretation `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *The Revolutionary Kant: A Commentary on the Critique of Pure Reason*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Graham Bird** | The major alternative to Allison's two-aspect reading and Strawson's two-world reading: Bird defends what he calls the *methodological* interpretation, on which transcendental idealism is a doctrine about the conditions of inquiry rather than about a metaphysical distinction between appearances and things-in-themselves. The book is a 900-page line-by-line commentary on the *CPR*, the most thorough such commentary in English. Limitation: long and demanding; the methodological reading is contested and not all scholars accept that it differs from Allison's two-aspect reading as much as Bird claims. | Researchers wanting comprehensive coverage. Read against Allison; the contrast is itself illuminating. |

### 4.3 Kant — Practical Philosophy

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Kant — foundations of deontological ethics | *Groundwork of the Metaphysics of Morals*, 1785 `[Introductory]` `[Evergreen]` | **Immanuel Kant**; trans. **Mary Gregor** (Cambridge, 1997), or with substantial introduction **Christine M. Korsgaard** (Cambridge, 2012) | Kant's most concentrated statement of his moral philosophy: the derivation of the categorical imperative from the concept of a rational will, the three formulations of the moral law (universal law, humanity as an end-in-itself, the kingdom of ends), the distinction between hypothetical and categorical imperatives, and the doctrine of moral worth as acting from duty. Approximately 80 pages that have determined the shape of moral philosophy for 240 years — the universalisability formula alone has generated a library of commentary, and the humanity-as-end-in-itself formulation has remained central to contemporary Kantian and human-rights theory. Limitation: the argument is deceptively compressed; the transitions from §I to §II (popular morality to philosophical morality) and from §II to §III (the deduction of the categorical imperative from autonomy) are difficult and the §III deduction is generally considered to fail. **Recommended translation:** Gregor (Cambridge, 1997) for research; the Korsgaard edition (2012) reprints Gregor's translation with substantial philosophical scaffolding for first reading. | Read before all other Kant. Pair with Korsgaard's *Creating the Kingdom of Ends* (below) for the major contemporary defence; read after with Wood's *Kant's Ethical Thought*. See also SEP: "Kant's Moral Philosophy." |
| `[Primary]` Kant — systematic practical philosophy | *Critique of Practical Reason*, 1788, and *The Metaphysics of Morals*, 1797 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Immanuel Kant**; trans. **Mary Gregor** in *Practical Philosophy* (Cambridge, 1996) | The *Critique of Practical Reason* (CPrR) extends the *Groundwork*'s argument into a systematic critical doctrine of the will: the doctrine of the fact of reason, the postulates of pure practical reason (freedom, immortality, God), and the doctrine of the highest good. The *Metaphysics of Morals* (1797) is Kant's applied ethics and political philosophy — divided into the *Doctrine of Right* (juridical philosophy, including property, contract, the state, punishment) and the *Doctrine of Virtue* (a system of duties to oneself and to others, including casuistical questions on lying, suicide, sexual ethics). Limitation: the CPrR's "fact of reason" doctrine effectively retreats from the *Groundwork*'s attempt to derive the moral law from rational agency, and many scholars regard it as a weakening rather than a strengthening of Kant's position; the *Doctrine of Virtue* contains some philosophical embarrassments (Kant's views on women, on sexual ethics, on race) that must be honestly confronted, not airbrushed. | Read after the *Groundwork*. The *Doctrine of Right* is central for Kant's political philosophy and connects to §8.10. |
| `[Secondary]` Kant's ethics — major contemporary reconstruction `[Analytic]` `[Anthology]` `[Evergreen]` | *Creating the Kingdom of Ends*, 1996 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Christine M. Korsgaard** | The major work of Kantian ethics in late-20th-century English-language philosophy: a collection of essays defending and developing a constructivist reading of Kant in which the moral law is constituted by the activity of rational willing rather than discovered as an antecedent metaphysical fact. Korsgaard's essays on the value of humanity, on the right to lie, on personal identity, and on the relations between the formulas of the categorical imperative are individual classics. The constructivist framework she develops here is the basis of her later *Sources of Normativity* (1996) and *Self-Constitution* (2009). Limitation: Korsgaard's Kant is a particular reading — more constructivist and less metaphysically committed than the texts strictly support; readers should pair with Wood (below) for a more textually conservative interpretation. | Graduate students in ethics. The starting point for serious engagement with contemporary Kantianism. |
| `[Secondary]` Kant's ethics — comprehensive scholarly study `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Kant's Ethical Thought*, 1999 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Allen W. Wood** | The major systematic treatment of Kant's ethical thought as a whole. Wood emphasises (against Korsgaard) the metaphysical and anthropological foundations of Kant's ethics — the doctrine of humanity as an end in itself, the place of moral feeling, the role of empirical psychology in the *Metaphysics of Morals*. Wood is unusually careful about the unattractive parts of Kant's ethics (on race, on women, on sexual morality) and about which parts of the system survive their excision. The treatment of the formulas of the categorical imperative and of the role of inclination is exemplary. Limitation: Wood's interpretation has been criticised for downplaying the role of the *Groundwork*'s formal arguments; the metaphysics-first reading has its own controversies. | Graduate students in ethics. Read alongside Korsgaard for the major interpretive contrast in current Kant scholarship. |
| `[Secondary]` Kant's ethics — Kantian constructivism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Constructions of Reason: Explorations of Kant's Practical Philosophy*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Onora O'Neill** | The other major strand of contemporary Kantian ethics: essays developing a constructive procedural reading of the categorical imperative as a test of universalisability of maxims. O'Neill's account of the universalisability test (in essays like "Consistency in Action") is the most rigorous available; her broader engagement with the political and applied dimensions of Kantian ethics is also influential. Limitation: focused essays rather than a continuous treatise; the relation to Rawls's broader Kantian constructivism (which O'Neill engages explicitly) requires the reader to track threads across multiple essays. | Graduate students in ethics. Important counterweight to Korsgaard's more metaphysically ambitious constructivism. |

### 4.4 Kant — Aesthetics & Teleology (*Critique of Judgment*)

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Kant — aesthetics, teleology | *Critique of the Power of Judgment*, 1790 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Immanuel Kant**; trans. **Paul Guyer & Eric Matthews** (Cambridge, 2000) | The third *Critique*, divided into the Critique of Aesthetic Judgment (taste — the beautiful and the sublime — and fine art) and the Critique of Teleological Judgment (organic purposiveness, the unity of natural science). The *CPJ* is the founding text of modern aesthetics (the disinterested character of aesthetic pleasure, the universal validity claim, the antinomy of taste) and the major early-modern philosophical text on biology and natural purpose. The book also contains Kant's most accessible discussion of the relation between the theoretical and practical philosophy — the "bridge" function of judgment between nature and freedom. Limitation: the architectonic is forced (Kant's drive to find a third faculty paralleling understanding and reason); the aesthetic and teleological halves do not fit together as comfortably as Kant claims. The Pluhar (Hackett, 1987) and Bernard (older) translations are alternatives, but Guyer–Matthews is the current scholarly standard. | Read after the first two Critiques. Required for any serious work in aesthetics or philosophy of biology in the Kantian tradition. See also SEP: "Kant's Aesthetics and Teleology." |
| `[Secondary]` Kant's aesthetics — definitive study `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Kant and the Claims of Taste*, 2nd ed. 1997 (1st ed. 1979) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Paul Guyer** | The major English-language study of the aesthetic half of the third *Critique*. Guyer reconstructs Kant's account of aesthetic judgment, the deduction of the universal validity claim, the analyses of the beautiful and the sublime, and the doctrine of fine art. The 2nd edition substantially revises the 1979 original. Limitation: tightly focused on the aesthetic theory; readers wanting the full third Critique should also consult Allison's *Kant's Theory of Taste* (Cambridge, 2001) for a competing interpretation, or Henry Allison and Hannah Ginsborg for the teleology. | Graduate students in aesthetics or in Kant. The book against which other readings of Kant's aesthetics define themselves. |
