| `[Secondary]` Kant — aesthetics and biology together `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *Kant on Beauty and Biology: An Interpretation of the Critique of Judgment*, 2007 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Rachel Zuckert** | A major recent study arguing for the unity of the third *Critique*: Zuckert reconstructs the underlying account of judgment that, she argues, makes the aesthetic and teleological halves of the *CPJ* parts of a single coherent project. Particularly important on the relation between reflective judgment and the rest of Kant's system, and on the philosophical relevance of Kant's biology. Limitation: the unity thesis is contested and not all scholars accept Zuckert's reconstruction. | Researchers in Kant's third *Critique*. Read after Guyer for the alternative unifying interpretation. |

### 4.5 Kant — Surveys & the Critical Philosophy as a Whole

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Reference]` Kant — orientation `[Anthology]` | *The Cambridge Companion to Kant*, 1992, and *The Cambridge Companion to Kant and Modern Philosophy*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Paul Guyer** (ed.) | The two outstanding orientation volumes for Kant. The 1992 *Companion to Kant* covers the three *Critiques* and Kant's practical, aesthetic, and religious philosophy with essays from leading specialists (Guyer himself, Allison, Wood, Korsgaard, O'Neill). The 2006 *Companion to Kant and Modern Philosophy* extends the coverage forward to Kant's reception and engagement with contemporary philosophy; particularly valuable for situating Kant in the broader trajectory of modern philosophy. Limitation: the 1992 volume's essays on the first *Critique* have been substantially superseded by the post-2000 commentary literature; use as orientation rather than as current research reference. | Graduate students entering Kant scholarship. Read selectively alongside whichever Critique one is working through. |
| `[Research]` German philosophy after Kant — historical `[Continental]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Fate of Reason: German Philosophy from Kant to Fichte*, 1987 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Frederick C. Beiser** | The major English-language study of the immediate post-Kantian period — Reinhold, Jacobi, Schulze, Maimon, the first reception of Kant by his contemporaries — that produced the conditions for Fichte, Schelling, and Hegel. The book displaced earlier neglect of this period in Anglophone scholarship and is now the standard reference on the philosophical transition from Kant to German Idealism. Limitation: Beiser writes with strong interpretive commitments (his Hegelian sympathies are visible) and his presentations of figures like Maimon and Reinhold are partly polemical defences. | Graduate students in Kant or German Idealism. The indispensable bridge between Kant and §4.6 below. |

### 4.6 German Idealism — Fichte, Schelling, Hegel as a Movement

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Research]` German Idealism — historical survey `[Continental]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *German Philosophy 1760–1860: The Legacy of Idealism*, 2002 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Terry Pinkard** | The most accessible and historically rich account of German philosophy from the late Enlightenment through the dissolution of idealism in English. Pinkard reads German Idealism as a sustained engagement with the philosophical and political consequences of the French Revolution and the loss of traditional metaphysical and political authority. Particularly strong on Hegel (Pinkard's specialty) and on the social and institutional context of post-Kantian philosophy. Limitation: more historical-narrative than systematic-philosophical; for the systematic reconstruction of post-Kantian arguments consult Beiser or Pippin. | The first secondary text to read on the post-Kantian movement. Pair with Beiser's *German Idealism: The Struggle Against Subjectivism* (Harvard, 2002) for the major systematic alternative. |
| `[Research]` German Idealism — systematic interpretation `[Continental]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *German Idealism: The Struggle Against Subjectivism, 1781–1801*, 2002 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Frederick C. Beiser** | A systematic and polemical reading of German Idealism as essentially anti-subjectivist — committed to overcoming the residual Cartesian subjectivism that Kant himself had not fully escaped. Beiser argues, against the standard reading on which German Idealism radicalises Kantian subjectivism, that the major figures (Fichte, the early Schelling, Hegel) were all moving toward an objective absolute. Limitation: the interpretive thesis is contested by Pippin and the Pittsburgh school (who read German Idealism as a development of the Kantian critical project, not a reversal of it); read against Pippin's *Hegel's Idealism* (§4.7). | Graduate students in German Idealism. Read alongside or after Pinkard for the systematic-interpretive complement. |
| `[Primary]` Fichte — early *Wissenschaftslehre* | *Foundations of the Entire Wissenschaftslehre*, 1794–1795 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **J. G. Fichte**; trans. **Peter Heath & John Lachs** (Cambridge, 1982) | Fichte's first systematic presentation of his post-Kantian philosophy: the derivation of self-consciousness from the act of self-positing of the I, the I/not-I dialectic, and the construction of all consciousness from the activity of the absolute I. Fichte's project is to give Kantian transcendental philosophy a unified foundation in the self-positing subject — eliminating Kant's residual commitments to the thing in itself. Limitation: notoriously difficult prose; Fichte revised the *Wissenschaftslehre* many times across his career and the 1794 version is the most influential historically but not Fichte's mature view. The Cambridge collection of Fichte's *Introductions to the Wissenschaftslehre* (Breazeale, ed., Hackett 1994) is the better entry point. **Recommended translation:** Heath–Lachs (Cambridge, 1982) is the standard. | Graduate students in German Idealism. Not the place to start with Fichte; begin with the Breazeale *Introductions* and the *System of Ethics*. See also SEP: "Johann Gottlieb Fichte." |

### 4.7 Hegel — Primary Texts & Commentary

**The Hegel translation problem.** For the *Phenomenology of Spirit* (1807): A. V. **Miller**'s translation (Oxford, 1977) was the standard for forty years and remains widely cited; **Terry Pinkard**'s translation (Cambridge, 2018) is now preferred by most scholars for its closer fidelity to the German and its handling of technical vocabulary; **Michael Inwood**'s translation (Oxford, 2018) is a third option with extensive commentary. For the *Science of Logic*: **George di Giovanni**'s translation (Cambridge, 2010) is the new scholarly standard and has displaced **Miller**'s older Allen & Unwin translation (1969).

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Hegel — dialectic of consciousness | *Phenomenology of Spirit*, 1807 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. W. F. Hegel**; trans. **Terry Pinkard** (Cambridge, 2018) | The first major work of Hegel's mature system and one of the most influential and most difficult texts in modern philosophy: a phenomenological-dialectical narrative of the development of consciousness from sense-certainty through perception, understanding, self-consciousness (the famous master-slave dialectic), reason, spirit (including the analysis of the French Revolution), religion, and absolute knowing. The *Phenomenology* is simultaneously an introduction to Hegel's system and a substantive philosophical work in its own right; its structure has supported readings as a developmental epistemology (Pippin), a social theory of recognition (Pinkard), an existential narrative (Kojève), and a religious-historical philosophy of spirit (Hyppolite). Limitation: Hegel's prose is wilfully obscure, the dialectical method is contested in its form and substance, and the relation between the *Phenomenology* and the *Logic* is itself a major scholarly question. **Recommended translation:** Pinkard (Cambridge, 2018) for current scholarship; Miller (Oxford, 1977) for historical reference; Inwood (Oxford, 2018) for first reading with substantial running commentary. The Preface and Introduction together repay separate sustained study. | Always read with substantial secondary support (Stern as introductory; Pinkard or Pippin for depth). The master-slave dialectic (Sections A and B of "Self-Consciousness") is the single most influential passage. See also SEP: "Hegel" and "Hegel's Dialectics." |
| `[Primary]` Hegel — systematic metaphysics and logic | *Science of Logic*, 1812–1816 (Encyclopedia *Logic*, 1817; rev. 1827, 1830) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. W. F. Hegel**; trans. **George di Giovanni** (Cambridge, 2010) for the *Science of Logic*; **T. F. Geraets, W. A. Suchting & H. S. Harris** (Hackett, 1991) for the Encyclopedia *Logic* | The systematic core of Hegel's philosophy: the development of the categories of thought from being and nothing through becoming, determinate being, quantity, measure, essence, the doctrine of the notion (or concept), and the idea. The *Science of Logic* is Hegel's most ambitious and most difficult work — the development of the structure of reality from the most abstract categories of pure thought, with no presuppositions taken from experience. The Encyclopedia *Logic* (the "Lesser Logic" of the *Encyclopedia of the Philosophical Sciences*) is the more accessible later restatement, with extensive remarks for student readers. Limitation: the *Logic* is a sustained ontological and metaphysical argument whose validity is contested at the deepest level — Hegel's claims that thought develops categories from itself, that the dialectic produces real philosophical results, and that being and thought are identical are accepted by his defenders and dismissed by his analytic critics. **Recommended translation:** di Giovanni (Cambridge, 2010) for the *Science of Logic*; Geraets et al. (Hackett, 1991) for the Encyclopedia *Logic*. The Houlgate study (below) is the indispensable English-language commentary. | Begin with the Encyclopedia *Logic* (briefer, with the supplementary remarks) before the *Science of Logic*. Houlgate's *Opening of Hegel's Logic* (Purdue, 2006) is the best companion. |
| `[Primary]` Hegel — political philosophy | *Elements of the Philosophy of Right*, 1820 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. W. F. Hegel**; trans. **H. B. Nisbet**; ed. **Allen W. Wood** (Cambridge, 1991) | Hegel's mature political philosophy: the dialectical development of free will through abstract right (property, contract, wrong), morality (the limits of Kantian moral subjectivity), and ethical life (Sittlichkeit — the family, civil society, the state). The book is the founding text of modern social philosophy and the major early critique of liberal individualism; it has been read in opposite directions by Marxists, conservative communitarians, and contemporary recognition theorists (Honneth). Limitation: the famous Preface (with "the rational is actual and the actual is rational") has been the subject of bitter dispute over whether Hegel endorses the Prussian state of his time or proposes a normative standard against which it could be measured; the answer is genuinely interpretively open. **Recommended translation:** Nisbet (Cambridge, 1991) with Wood's editorial apparatus is the scholarly standard. | Read after at least selections from the *Phenomenology*. Central for §8.10 (political philosophy). |
| `[Secondary]` Hegel — single-author overview `[Continental]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Hegel*, 1975 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Charles Taylor** | The standard English-language single-volume study of Hegel's philosophy. Taylor reconstructs Hegel's system as an expressivist response to the modern problem of meaning and self-understanding, with substantial attention to the historical and intellectual context (the engagement with Kant, the influence of Herder, the relation to Schelling). The book is long (~600 pp.) but uniformly clear, and its account of Hegel's project — to overcome the divisions of modernity through a philosophy that exhibits the rational structure of self-determining spirit — has shaped subsequent Anglophone Hegel scholarship even where it has been disputed. Limitation: Taylor's expressivist reading is one interpretation; the analytic-Kantian reading (Pippin) and the recognition-theoretic reading (Pinkard, Honneth) are major alternatives. | The first secondary text to read on Hegel as a whole. Pair with Pippin below for the systematic-analytic alternative. |
| `[Secondary]` Hegel — analytic-Kantian reading `[Analytic]` `[Continental]` `[Bridge]` `[Research]` | *Hegel's Idealism: The Satisfactions of Self-Consciousness*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Robert B. Pippin** | The major work in the analytic-Kantian reading of Hegel that has reshaped Anglophone Hegel scholarship since 1990. Pippin reads Hegel as the radicalisation rather than the rejection of Kant's critical project — Hegel completes the Kantian programme by overcoming the lingering distinction between concepts and the world that Kant could not eliminate. The book bridges analytic philosophy of mind and continental Hegel scholarship in a way that has made Hegel newly accessible to analytic philosophers (alongside the related work of McDowell, Brandom, and Pinkard — see §4.10 and Table 9). Pippin's later *Hegel's Realm of Shadows* (Chicago, 2019) extends the project to the *Logic*. Limitation: the analytic-Kantian reading is contested by the more metaphysically robust readings (Stern, Beiser, Houlgate); Pippin's Hegel is in some ways a Kantian-and-then-some, which some scholars regard as evading Hegel's more ambitious metaphysical claims. | Graduate students in Hegel or in the analytic-continental bridge tradition. Read with Taylor for the alternative; both required for current Hegel scholarship. |

### 4.8 Schopenhauer, Kierkegaard & Anti-Idealist Reactions

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Schopenhauer — metaphysics, ethics, aesthetics | *The World as Will and Representation*, Vol. 1, 1818 (Vol. 2, 1844) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Arthur Schopenhauer**; trans. **Judith Norman, Alistair Welchman & Christopher Janaway** (Cambridge, 2010 and 2018) | The systematic statement of Schopenhauer's philosophy: a development of the Kantian distinction between appearance and thing-in-itself, in which the thing-in-itself is identified as *will* — a blind, striving, suffering metaphysical principle of which our willing is the inner experience. The system covers metaphysics (Book I — the world as representation; Book II — the world as will), aesthetics (Book III — art as temporary escape from the will), and ethics (Book IV — denial of the will, compassion, asceticism). Schopenhauer was the first major Western philosopher to engage seriously with Indian thought (the *Upanishads*, Buddhism). Limitation: the metaphysical identification of the thing-in-itself with will is the system's most original move and its most philosophically vulnerable — Schopenhauer's argument for it, in §18, is among the most contested passages in the corpus. **Recommended translation:** Norman–Welchman–Janaway (Cambridge, 2010 for Vol. 1, 2018 for Vol. 2) is now the scholarly standard, replacing E. F. J. Payne's older Dover translation. | Self-study readers; students of post-Kantian philosophy, aesthetics, or philosophical pessimism. Pair with Janaway's *Self and World in Schopenhauer's Philosophy* (Oxford, 1989) and the *Cambridge Companion to Schopenhauer* (Janaway, ed., 1999). See also SEP: "Arthur Schopenhauer." |
| `[Primary]` Kierkegaard — existential pseudonymous works | *Either/Or* (1843), *Fear and Trembling* (1843), and *Concluding Unscientific Postscript to Philosophical Fragments* (1846) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Søren Kierkegaard**; trans. **Howard V. Hong & Edna H. Hong** (Princeton, *Kierkegaard's Writings* series); accessible alternatives by **Alastair Hannay** (Penguin) | Kierkegaard's pseudonymous works deploy a strategy of *indirect communication*: the views are advanced not by Kierkegaard but by characters (Johannes de Silentio in *Fear and Trembling*, Johannes Climacus in the *Postscript*, A and B in *Either/Or*) whose positions Kierkegaard does not straightforwardly endorse. *Either/Or* contrasts the aesthetic and ethical lives; *Fear and Trembling* explores the suspension of the ethical by faith through the Abraham–Isaac story; the *Postscript* develops the doctrine of *subjective truth* and the impossibility of a systematic Christianity. The works founded the existential tradition and remain foundational for any reading of Heidegger, Sartre, and 20th-century theology (Bultmann, Tillich, Barth). Limitation: the indirect communication strategy means that any straightforward attribution of views to Kierkegaard must be argued for, not assumed. **Recommended translation:** Hong–Hong (Princeton) for scholarly work; Hannay (Penguin) for accessibility and literary readability. | Read alongside continental philosophy of the 20th century (Table 9). The *Postscript*'s critique of Hegelian system-building is essential context for understanding the anti-systematic strain in continental philosophy. See also SEP: "Søren Kierkegaard." |
| `[Secondary]` Schopenhauer — philosophical study `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Self and World in Schopenhauer's Philosophy*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Christopher Janaway** | The major English-language philosophical study of Schopenhauer: a careful reconstruction of the metaphysical and epistemological foundations of *The World as Will and Representation*, with particular attention to the Kantian background, the doctrine of the body as the immediate object of will, and the philosophy of mind. Janaway is unsparing about the difficulties in Schopenhauer's argument for the identification of the thing-in-itself with will. Limitation: focused on the metaphysical core; readers wanting Schopenhauer's aesthetics or ethics should consult the relevant essays in the *Cambridge Companion to Schopenhauer* (Janaway, ed., 1999) or Magee's older *The Philosophy of Schopenhauer* (Oxford, 1983). | Graduate students in post-Kantian philosophy. Read after *WWR* Book I. |
| `[Secondary]` Kierkegaard — introduction `[Continental]` `[Self-Study]` | *Kierkegaard: An Introduction*, 2009 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **C. Stephen Evans** | The best accessible philosophical introduction to Kierkegaard in English: organised by topic (the pseudonyms, the stages of existence, faith and reason, ethics, the religious writings) rather than by text. Evans is particularly good on the relation between the pseudonymous works and the explicitly Christian *Works of Love* and *Practice in Christianity*. Limitation: written from a sympathetic Christian-philosophical perspective; for a more secular interpretive frame consult Westphal's *Becoming a Self* or Lippitt's *Kierkegaard and the Problem of Self-Love*. | The first secondary text to read on Kierkegaard. Pair with the *Cambridge Companion to Kierkegaard* (Hannay & Marino, eds., 1998). |

### 4.9 Marx — Primary Texts & Philosophical Commentary

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Marx — major selection `[Anthology]` | *The Marx-Engels Reader*, 2nd ed., 1978 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Karl Marx & Friedrich Engels**; ed. **Robert C. Tucker** (Norton) | The standard one-volume selection of Marx's and Engels's writings in English: the *Economic and Philosophic Manuscripts of 1844*, *Theses on Feuerbach*, the *German Ideology* (Part I), the *Communist Manifesto*, the *18th Brumaire*, selections from *Capital* and the *Grundrisse*, and the major political and philosophical letters. Tucker's selections cover the philosophical Marx (the early manuscripts on alienation), the political Marx (the *Manifesto*, the political essays), and the economic Marx (the selections from *Capital* and *Grundrisse*). Limitation: as a selection, the Reader is necessarily incomplete; for *Capital* and the *Grundrisse* in full, separate editions are needed. **Recommended translations:** Tucker's selections use various established translations; for *Capital* Vol. 1 in full, use Ben Fowkes (Penguin, 1976) — see next entry. | The standard course text and reference for Marx as philosopher. See also SEP: "Karl Marx." |
| `[Primary]` Marx — magnum opus | *Capital: A Critique of Political Economy*, Vol. 1, 1867 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Karl Marx**; trans. **Ben Fowkes** (Penguin Classics, 1976) | Marx's mature systematic work: the analysis of the commodity (Part I, including the famous chapters on the value form and on commodity fetishism), the transformation of money into capital, the labour process and the theory of surplus value, the working day, machinery and large-scale industry, the laws of capitalist accumulation, and the "so-called primitive accumulation." Volume 1 is the only volume Marx completed for publication; Volumes 2 and 3 were assembled by Engels from manuscripts and are philosophically less polished. The opening chapters on the value form and commodity fetishism have remained productive for philosophy and social theory long after the labour theory of value as a precise economic doctrine has been largely abandoned by professional economists. Limitation: Marx's labour theory of value has been subjected to enduring economic critique (Bortkiewicz onwards) that the philosophical reception has often ignored; conversely, dismissals of *Capital* on labour-theory-of-value grounds often miss what the book is doing philosophically. **Recommended translation:** Fowkes (Penguin, 1976) is the scholarly standard, with Mandel's introduction. | Read with secondary support; David Harvey's *A Companion to Marx's Capital* (Verso, 2010) is the most useful contemporary guide for first reading. |
| `[Secondary]` Marx — analytic reconstruction `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Karl Marx's Theory of History: A Defence*, 1978 (exp. ed. 2000) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. A. Cohen** | The founding work of "analytical Marxism": a rigorous reconstruction of Marx's theory of history (the development of productive forces, the determination of relations of production, the dialectic of base and superstructure) as a functional-explanatory theory testable by ordinary standards of philosophical and social-scientific argument. Cohen's defence of historical materialism explicitly brackets the Hegelian-dialectical inheritance and asks whether the substantive theses are defensible on independent grounds; he argues that the strongest version of historical materialism is the productive-forces-primacy thesis. Limitation: Cohen's reconstruction has been criticised by other Marxists (Wood, Brenner) as too functionalist and as evading the role of class struggle in Marx's account; the broader analytical-Marxism project (Cohen, Elster, Roemer) has had a complicated reception. | Graduate students in political philosophy or the philosophy of social science. Read after a first encounter with Marx's own writings. |
| `[Secondary]` Marx — philosophical study `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Karl Marx* (Arguments of the Philosophers series), 1981 (2nd ed. 2004) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Allen W. Wood** | The most comprehensive single-volume philosophical study of Marx in English: covers historical materialism, the theory of alienation, the analysis of capitalism, Marx's view of human nature, his relation to morality and justice (Wood's famous thesis that Marx does not condemn capitalism as *unjust*), and his philosophy of history. The 2004 second edition adds substantial responses to critics. Wood is unusually careful about the difference between Marx's own positions and the various received Marxisms. Limitation: Wood's thesis that Marx rejects moral evaluation of capitalism is contested by Cohen and others; this is itself among the major debates in Marxian ethics. | Graduate students in political philosophy or in 19th-century continental philosophy. The best single book for understanding Marx as a philosopher rather than as an economist or revolutionary. |

### 4.10 Nietzsche — Primary Texts & Commentary

**Translation orientation.** **Walter Kaufmann**'s Viking/Penguin translations (1950s–1960s) rehabilitated Nietzsche from the Nazi-era distortions and were the English standard for half a century; they are fluent and accessible but are coloured by Kaufmann's specific (existentialist, anti-Nazi) interpretive framework. The Cambridge Texts in the History of Philosophy series (under various editors and translators — Judith Norman, Adrian Del Caro, Maudemarie Clark, Carol Diethe — published from 1996 onward) is now the scholarly standard, with more literal translations and apparatus integrated with current scholarship. The Stanford *Complete Works of Friedrich Nietzsche* (in progress) will eventually replace the Cambridge editions as the most comprehensive English edition. **Important:** *The Will to Power* (compiled posthumously by Elisabeth Förster-Nietzsche from notebook entries) is not a book Nietzsche wrote and should not be treated as such; the notebook material itself is now translated as *Writings from the Late Notebooks* (Cambridge, 2003).

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Nietzsche — mature philosophy in aphoristic form | *Beyond Good and Evil: Prelude to a Philosophy of the Future*, 1886 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Friedrich Nietzsche**; trans. **Judith Norman**; ed. **Rolf-Peter Horstmann & Judith Norman** (Cambridge, 2002) | Nietzsche's most systematic mature work in the aphoristic mode: nine parts treating the prejudices of philosophers, the free spirit, the religious nature, the natural history of morality, the new philosophers, the herd and the noble, and the will to power as psychological doctrine. The book is the closest Nietzsche came to a systematic statement of his mature philosophy, but the systematicity is achieved through accumulation of aphorisms rather than continuous argument. Particularly important: the critique of the Kantian "synthetic a priori" (§11), the will-to-power psychology (§§13, 36), the perspectivism (§§18, 22), the master/slave morality typology (Part Nine). Limitation: aphoristic form makes systematic reconstruction by readers necessary and contestable. **Recommended translation:** Norman (Cambridge, 2002) for scholarly work; Kaufmann (Vintage, 1966) for accessibility. | The best entry point to mature Nietzsche. Pair with *On the Genealogy of Morality* (below). See also SEP: "Friedrich Nietzsche." |
| `[Primary]` Nietzsche — major polemic | *On the Genealogy of Morality: A Polemic*, 1887 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Friedrich Nietzsche**; trans. **Carol Diethe**; ed. **Keith Ansell-Pearson** (Cambridge, 2nd ed. 2006) | Nietzsche's most sustained argumentative work, in three essays: (I) the origin of the moral concepts good/evil from the priestly inversion of the warrior good/bad; (II) the origin of guilt and bad conscience from the internalisation of cruelty; (III) the meaning of ascetic ideals. The *Genealogy* is at once philosophy of history, moral psychology, philosophical anthropology, and polemic; it is also the most cited Nietzsche text in contemporary moral philosophy, where Williams, Geuss, and others have used it as a model for genealogical critique of moral concepts. Limitation: the historical-genealogical claims are partly speculative, and the relation between genealogy and normative critique — does showing the contingent origin of a moral concept undermine its normative force? — is itself philosophically contested. **Recommended translation:** Diethe rev. ed. (Cambridge, 2006); Kaufmann–Hollingdale (Vintage, 1989) is the older accessible alternative. | Required reading in ethics and moral psychology. Pair with Leiter and Reginster below. |
| `[Primary]` Nietzsche — literary-philosophical work | *Thus Spoke Zarathustra: A Book for All and None*, 1883–1885 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Friedrich Nietzsche**; trans. **Adrian Del Caro** (Cambridge, 2006) | Nietzsche's most literary and most influential work, written in a parodic biblical style and developing the doctrines of the death of God, the overhuman (*Übermensch*), the eternal recurrence, and the will to power. The four parts narrate Zarathustra's descent from his cave, his teachings to and rejection by the marketplace, and his solitary working-through of his doctrines. *Zarathustra* is also Nietzsche's most poetically ambitious work and the one he himself regarded most highly. Limitation: the literary mode makes interpretation contested at every level, and the popular reception (the Nazi and counter-cultural appropriations alike) has produced sustained distortions; reading without secondary support produces predictable misreadings. **Recommended translation:** Del Caro (Cambridge, 2006) for current scholarship; Kaufmann (Viking, 1954) for the older literary translation; avoid older Thomas Common translation. | Read after *Beyond Good and Evil* and the *Genealogy*, not before. Lampert's *Nietzsche's Teaching: An Interpretation of Thus Spoke Zarathustra* (Yale, 1986) is the major sustained interpretation. |
| `[Secondary]` Nietzsche — literary-philosophical interpretation `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Nietzsche: Life as Literature*, 1985 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alexander Nehamas** | The major analytic-philosophical study of Nietzsche from the 1980s and the most influential single book of Nietzsche scholarship in English in the past half-century. Nehamas argues that Nietzsche is best read as offering a philosophical project of *self-creation* — making one's own life into a coherent literary work — and that this aestheticist reading unifies the apparent contradictions in Nietzsche's positions. The treatment of perspectivism, the eternal recurrence, and the relation between Nietzsche the author and his texts is exemplary. Limitation: Nehamas's aestheticist reading is one major interpretation among several; the naturalist reading (Leiter, Clark) and the more existentially-political reading (Reginster) are major alternatives. | Graduate students in Nietzsche or in late 19th-century philosophy. Read alongside Leiter below. |
| `[Secondary]` Nietzsche — naturalist interpretation `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Nietzsche on Morality*, 2002 (2nd ed. 2015) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Brian Leiter** | The major contemporary naturalist reading of Nietzsche: Leiter argues that Nietzsche's project is best understood as continuous with naturalistic philosophy and psychology — Nietzsche as a naturalist moral psychologist whose work is empirically tractable by current standards. The book focuses on the *Genealogy* and on Nietzsche's moral psychology and metaethics; the chapter on the "fatalism" interpretation of Nietzsche on free will and moral responsibility has been especially influential. Limitation: the naturalist reading minimises the literary and aestheticist dimensions Nehamas emphasises, and underweights the role of self-creation as a positive Nietzschean project. | Graduate students in metaethics and moral psychology. The book against which contemporary Anglophone Nietzsche scholarship organises itself. |

### Connections to other tables

Kant's first *Critique* (§4.1–4.2) is foundational for almost everything in 20th-century epistemology and philosophy of mind (Tables 6 and 7); the Refutation of Idealism and the Transcendental Deduction remain live in contemporary epistemology. Kant's practical philosophy (§4.3) is the founding document of §8.3 (Kantian ethics) and indirectly of §8.5 (Rawlsian contractualism — Rawls's Kantian self-understanding is explicit). Hegel's *Philosophy of Right* (§4.7) is the founding document of communitarian political philosophy (Taylor, Sandel) addressed in §8.10. Schopenhauer's metaphysics of will and Kierkegaard's existential analysis (§4.8) are the immediate background to Heidegger, Sartre, and 20th-century existentialism in Table 9. Marx (§4.9) is the founding figure of critical theory (Frankfurt School, Table 9 §9.7) and of contemporary political philosophy from the left (cf. §8.11). Nietzsche's *Genealogy* (§4.10) reshaped 20th-century metaethics and moral psychology (Table 8) and is the explicit model for Foucault's genealogical method (Table 9 §9.8). The analytic-Kantian reading of Hegel (Pippin, §4.7) is one of the major bridges between the analytic and continental traditions and connects directly to McDowell's *Mind and World* (Table 9 §9.10) and to the Pittsburgh School's broader project.

# Table 5 · Logic, Philosophy of Language & Formal Epistemology

*Scope:* Mathematical logic in its philosophical applications, the philosophy of logical consequence, the classical analytic tradition in philosophy of language (Frege through early Wittgenstein), the post-Quinean tradition (Quine, Kripke, Putnam, Kaplan, Lewis), formal semantics, pragmatics and speech act theory, formal epistemology, and theories of truth. The discipline is predominantly analytic; continental engagements with language are addressed in Table 9.

### 5.1 Logic for Philosophers — Introductory & Intermediate

For the formal development of mathematical logic — propositional calculus, first-order logic, model theory, computability, incompleteness — consult the Mathematics Atlas (Smith's *Introduction to Formal Logic* and van Dalen's *Logic and Structure*; for Gödel's theorems, Smith's *Gödel's Theorems*). The entries below are the texts that develop logic for *philosophical* applications — modal logic, second-order logic, free logic, many-valued and intuitionistic logic — and that present the philosophical motivations for choices of formal system.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Logic for philosophers `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Logic: The Laws of Truth*, 2012 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Nicholas J. J. Smith** | The current best introductory logic text written for philosophy students rather than for mathematicians: propositional and first-order logic developed carefully, but with a focus on the philosophical motivations for the choices made (why classical bivalence, why first-order rather than higher-order, why the material conditional). The treatment of philosophical paradoxes and of the philosophical significance of metalogical results (completeness, compactness, Löwenheim–Skolem) is exemplary. Limitation: introductory and does not extend to modal logic or to most non-classical systems; pair with Sider (below) for the next level. | Philosophy students with no prior logic background. The single best self-study text for getting from zero to a philosopher's working knowledge of first-order logic. (Note: not the same Smith as the Math Atlas's *An Introduction to Formal Logic*.) |
| `[Systematic]` Modal and intensional logic for philosophers `[Analytic]` | *Logic for Philosophy*, 2010 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Theodore Sider** | The intermediate text covering what Smith does not: propositional modal logic with full possible-worlds semantics, quantified modal logic, two-dimensional modal logic, counterfactuals, intuitionistic logic, free logic, and second-order logic — with attention throughout to the philosophical issues raised by the choice of formal system. Sider's exposition is unusually clear on the philosophical commitments embedded in (e.g.) the choice between constant- and variable-domain semantics for quantified modal logic, or between S4 and S5 as the modal logic of metaphysical necessity. Limitation: presupposes a first course in logic (Smith, van Dalen, or equivalent). | Graduate students in metaphysics, philosophy of language, or epistemology — the formal toolkit assumed by most current analytic philosophy outside ethics. See also SEP: "Modal Logic." |
| `[Systematic]` Non-classical logics `[Analytic]` `[Reference]` | *An Introduction to Non-Classical Logic: From If to Is*, 2nd ed., 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Graham Priest** | The standard text on non-classical logics for philosophers: propositional and first-order intuitionistic logic, many-valued logics (Łukasiewicz, Kleene, Łukasiewicz with three values), relevant logics (R, E), conditional logics, paraconsistent logics (LP, dialetheism), free logic, fuzzy logic, and the related modal systems. Priest is a leading paraconsistent logician and the book reflects his sympathies, but the presentation of competing systems is even-handed. Limitation: dense; the second edition adds significant material on first-order versions of non-classical systems that not all readers will need. | Graduate students and researchers working with non-classical logics, or working in metaphysics or philosophy of language where the choice of underlying logic matters (truth, vagueness, fiction, modality). See also SEP: "Paraconsistent Logic" and "Many-Valued Logic." |

### 5.2 Philosophy of Logic — The Metaphysics of Logical Consequence

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Philosophy of logic — introduction `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Thinking About Logic: An Introduction to the Philosophy of Logic*, 1995 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Stephen Read** | The best introductory text on the philosophy of logic — distinct from logic itself: chapters on truth, logical consequence, paradoxes, the foundations of logic, possibility and necessity, vagueness, future contingents, and the conditional. Read presupposes only a first-course working knowledge of logic and is exemplarily clear on questions like "what makes a logical truth true?" and "is logic descriptive of reasoning or normative for it?" Limitation: introductory; for current research-level discussion consult the entries below. | Philosophy students with one prior logic course. The natural follow-up to Smith's logic text above. |
| `[Research]` Logical consequence — major monograph `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Concept of Logical Consequence*, 1990 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Etchemendy** | The major critical study of Tarski's model-theoretic definition of logical consequence. Etchemendy argues that Tarski's definition does not in fact capture the intuitive notion of logical consequence — and that the *coextensiveness* of the Tarskian definition with the intuitive notion in first-order logic depends on assumptions Tarski did not make explicit and that are themselves substantive. The book has reshaped the philosophy of logic; nearly every subsequent treatment of logical consequence responds to it. Limitation: technically demanding; presupposes Tarski's "On the Concept of Logical Consequence" and standard model theory. Etchemendy's critique has been disputed (notably by Sher, Ray, and others); the current state of the question is open. | Graduate students and researchers in logic and the philosophy of language. See also SEP: "Logical Consequence." |
| `[Research]` Logical pluralism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Logical Pluralism*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **JC Beall & Greg Restall** | The defence of the thesis that there is more than one correct logic — different logics for different *purposes* or *cases* (classical, intuitionistic, relevant), each capturing a different version of the intuitive notion of logical consequence. The book is the most influential contemporary statement of logical pluralism and has reshaped the dialectic in the philosophy of logic. Limitation: the version of pluralism Beall and Restall defend has been criticised by both monists (Priest in one direction, Read in another) and by other pluralists (Field) as either too modest or too radical; the book's exposition makes the specific commitments unusually clear. | Graduate students in philosophy of logic. Pair with Priest's *Doubt Truth to be a Liar* (Oxford, 2006) for the major monist alternative. |

### 5.3 Philosophy of Language — Classical Analytic Tradition

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Frege — selection `[Anthology]` `[Analytic]` | *The Frege Reader*, 1997 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Gottlob Frege**; ed. **Michael Beaney** (Blackwell) | The standard English-language selection from Frege's philosophical writings: selections from *Begriffsschrift* (1879, the first modern formal logic), the *Foundations of Arithmetic* (1884, in full), "Function and Concept" (1891), "On Sense and Reference" (1892), "On Concept and Object" (1892), "Thought" (1918), and selections from the *Basic Laws of Arithmetic*. Frege's logical and semantic innovations — quantification, function/argument analysis of predication, the sense/reference distinction, the context principle, the truth-value as the reference of a sentence — founded modern philosophy of language and the formal study of meaning. Limitation: a selection is necessarily partial; for *Foundations of Arithmetic* in full use Austin's translation (Blackwell), for *Basic Laws* use the Ebert–Rossberg translation (Oxford, 2013). **Recommended translation:** Beaney's edition for orientation; the Geach–Black *Translations from the Philosophical Writings of Gottlob Frege* (Blackwell, 3rd ed. 1980) is the older scholarly standard and remains useful. | Any philosophy student. The starting point for analytic philosophy of language and the foundations of mathematics. See also SEP: "Gottlob Frege" and Table 6 (philosophy of mathematics overlap). |
| `[Primary]` Russell — definite descriptions and analytic method `[Analytic]` | "On Denoting", 1905 (in *Logic and Knowledge*, 1956, or *The Collected Papers of Bertrand Russell* Vol. 4) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Bertrand Russell** | The single most influential paper in 20th-century analytic philosophy: Russell's analysis of definite descriptions ("the present King of France") as not denoting expressions but quantificational devices — *the F is G* analysed as ∃x(Fx ∧ ∀y(Fy → x=y) ∧ Gx). The theory dissolved the problem of empty descriptions, eliminated the apparent need for Meinongian non-existent objects, and demonstrated the technique of philosophical analysis that would define a tradition. Limitation: Strawson's "On Referring" (1950) raised the major counterargument — that empty definite descriptions presuppose rather than assert the existence of a referent — and the Russell–Strawson dispute has remained productive. The paper assumes Russell's broader logical framework as developed in *Principles of Mathematics* (1903) and *Principia Mathematica* (with Whitehead, 1910–1913). | Read alongside Frege's "On Sense and Reference" — the two papers founded analytic philosophy of language. See also Neale's *Descriptions* (MIT, 1990) for the major contemporary defence and elaboration. See also SEP: "Descriptions." |
| `[Primary]` Wittgenstein — early picture theory of meaning `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus*, 1921 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Ludwig Wittgenstein**; trans. **D. F. Pears & B. F. McGuinness** (Routledge, 1961) or **C. K. Ogden** (Routledge, 1922; the original English edition Wittgenstein himself reviewed) | The early Wittgenstein's compressed and oracular masterpiece: numbered propositions developing the picture theory of meaning (propositions picture states of affairs by sharing logical form), the truth-functional analysis of all complex propositions from elementary ones, the doctrine that logical propositions are tautologies and say nothing about the world, the famous "limits of language" doctrine, and the closing instruction to "throw away the ladder." The *Tractatus* shaped logical positivism in Vienna, was substantially repudiated by Wittgenstein himself in the *Philosophical Investigations*, and has been reread under the "resolute" interpretation (Diamond, Conant — cf. §1.4) that takes Wittgenstein's claim that his own propositions are nonsense seriously. Limitation: deliberately obscure; the recommended path is multiple passes with Anscombe's *Introduction to Wittgenstein's Tractatus* (Hutchinson, 1959) and Black's *A Companion to Wittgenstein's "Tractatus"* (Cornell, 1964) at hand. **Recommended translation:** Pears–McGuinness (Routledge, 1961) for accuracy; Ogden (1922) for the historical version, which Wittgenstein himself approved. | Graduate students in philosophy of language, logic, or 20th-century analytic philosophy. Read alongside the *Notebooks 1914–1916* (Blackwell) for the developmental record. See also SEP: "Ludwig Wittgenstein." |
| `[Secondary]` Frege — major commentary `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Frege: Philosophy of Language*, 1973 (2nd ed. 1981) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Michael Dummett** | The major book of 20th-century Frege scholarship and one of the founding documents of modern philosophy of language as a systematic discipline. Dummett reconstructs Frege's semantic theory — sense and reference, force and tone, the context principle, the analysis of generality — and uses it as the basis for an extended argument about the form a theory of meaning must take (the thesis that meaning is essentially connected to the conditions under which a sentence is verifiable rather than to truth conditions simpliciter — Dummett's anti-realism). Dummett's Frege is partly a constructed figure useful for Dummett's own project, but the reconstruction reshaped the field. Limitation: enormous (~700 pp.) and dense; the anti-realist programme Dummett pursues through Frege has been substantially superseded by other developments. The companion volume *The Interpretation of Frege's Philosophy* (1981) is a response to critics. | Graduate students in philosophy of language. Pair with the more textually conservative readings of Frege in Burge's *Truth, Thought, Reason* (Oxford, 2005). |

### 5.4 Philosophy of Language — Post-Quinean

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Quine — empiricism and meaning `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Two Dogmas of Empiricism", 1951, and *Word and Object*, 1960 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **W. V. O. Quine** | "Two Dogmas" is the founding paper of post-positivist analytic philosophy: an attack on the analytic/synthetic distinction and on the reductionist conception of empirical confirmation that, taken together, undermine the central commitments of logical positivism. The paper's holism — that beliefs face the tribunal of experience as a corporate body, with revision possible anywhere — has been productive far beyond its original target. *Word and Object* is Quine's systematic statement: the indeterminacy of translation, the inscrutability of reference, the doctrine of ontological commitment as quantification, and the privileged status of canonical first-order regimentation. The two together define one major axis of post-1950 analytic philosophy. Limitation: Quine's behaviourism (in *Word and Object*) is no longer the dominant philosophy of psychology, and his case for indeterminacy of translation depends partly on behaviourist assumptions; the case for indeterminacy from non-behaviourist premises is more contested. | Required reading in analytic philosophy. "Two Dogmas" is in *From a Logical Point of View* (Harvard, 1953) and in most anthologies. See also SEP: "Willard Van Orman Quine." |
| `[Primary]` Reference, necessity, identity `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Naming and Necessity*, 1980 (originally lectures, 1970) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Saul A. Kripke** | The single most influential work in analytic philosophy since 1970. Originally three lectures delivered at Princeton in January 1970, published with a preface and added material in 1980. Three central theses, each of which displaced an entrenched view: **(1) The modal argument against description theories of reference** — proper names ("Aristotle," "Gödel") are not synonymous with any cluster of descriptions associated with them; modal intuitions (Gödel could have failed to discover the incompleteness theorems while remaining Gödel) show that names are *rigid designators* that pick out the same individual in every possible world, while definite descriptions can be satisfied by different individuals in different worlds. **(2) The a posteriori necessary** — identity statements between rigid designators ("Hesperus is Phosphorus," "water is H₂O") are *necessarily true if true*, since rigid designators that co-refer in the actual world co-refer in every possible world; their *truth* however must be established empirically. This collapsed the long-assumed equation of the necessary with the a priori. **(3) The causal-historical theory of reference** — the reference of a name is fixed by an initial baptism and transmitted through a causal chain of name-using, not by the speaker's privately associated descriptions. The book also contains a sustained argument against the identity theory of mind (Kripke's modal argument for dualism). Limitation: the three theses are separable, and several have been substantially refined or contested in subsequent work (Evans's *Varieties of Reference* on names; Stalnaker's two-dimensional refinements; Block and Stalnaker on the modal argument against physicalism). | Required reading. The single most efficient text for understanding what happened in analytic metaphysics and philosophy of language between 1970 and 2000. See also SEP: "Names" and "Rigid Designators." |
| `[Primary]` Semantic externalism `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "The Meaning of 'Meaning'", 1975 (in *Mind, Language, and Reality: Philosophical Papers Vol. 2*, Cambridge, 1975) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hilary Putnam** | The founding paper of semantic externalism, presenting the Twin Earth thought experiment: an exact molecule-for-molecule physical duplicate of a 1750 English speaker on a planet where the colourless, odourless drinkable stuff that fills lakes is XYZ rather than H₂O, and using "water" to refer to it; the duplicate's word "water" refers to XYZ, not to H₂O, so the same internal psychological state can be associated with different referents — *meanings just ain't in the head*. The doctrine reshaped philosophy of mind (externalism about content), the theory of natural kind terms, and indirectly the metaphysics of mental content. Putnam combines this with a division-of-linguistic-labour thesis (experts are the bearers of stereotypical meanings for technical terms) and a sketch of a sophisticated externalist semantics. Limitation: Twin Earth thought experiments depend on assumptions about reference and modality that have been disputed; Putnam himself later partly retracted the realist semantics he had defended. | Read alongside Kripke. The two papers together set the agenda for post-1975 philosophy of language. See also SEP: "Hilary Putnam." |
| `[Primary]` Demonstratives, indexicals, and direct reference `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | "Demonstratives" and "Afterthoughts", 1989 (in *Themes from Kaplan*, eds. **Almog, Perry & Wettstein**, Oxford, 1989) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Kaplan** | The major systematic treatment of indexicals and demonstratives ("I," "you," "now," "here," "this," "that") in analytic philosophy of language. Kaplan distinguishes the *character* of an indexical (a function from context to content) from its *content* (the proposition expressed in a context), and argues that demonstratives are *directly referential* — their content in any context is just the referent, with no descriptive mediation. The framework has shaped formal semantics, philosophy of mind (Perry on indexical thought), and theories of belief and propositional attitudes. Limitation: long and technical; the 1989 publication is the polished version of papers circulating since 1977, and the relation to other directly-referential frameworks (Kripke, Donnellan) is a thread the reader must follow. | Graduate students in philosophy of language or formal semantics. Pair with Perry's "The Problem of the Essential Indexical" (1979). See also SEP: "Indexicals." |
| `[Primary]` Compositional semantics in possible-worlds framework `[Analytic]` | "General Semantics", 1970 (in *Philosophical Papers Vol. 1*, Oxford, 1983) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Lewis** | The compact paper that laid out the framework for compositional truth-conditional semantics in a possible-worlds setting: sentences as functions from indices (worlds, times, speakers, etc.) to truth values, the systematic recursive assignment of semantic values to syntactic categories, the treatment of generalised quantifiers as functions of higher type. The framework Lewis presents — refined by Montague, Kaplan, Stalnaker, and Heim and Kratzer (§5.5) — is the dominant framework in contemporary formal semantics. Limitation: a short paper; the full development is in the formal semantics literature that followed. | Read alongside Montague's "The Proper Treatment of Quantification in Ordinary English" (1973) and Heim and Kratzer (§5.5). |

### 5.5 Formal Semantics — Possible Worlds, Intensional Logic

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Formal semantics textbook `[Analytic]` `[Reference]` | *Semantics in Generative Grammar*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Irene Heim & Angelika Kratzer** (Blackwell) | The standard textbook of formal compositional semantics in the Montague–Lewis–Kaplan tradition: function-application semantics for English, treatment of quantification, binding and variables, intensionality, presupposition, conditionals, and modal expressions. The book is used as the introduction to formal semantics in most linguistics and philosophy of language graduate programmes. Limitation: assumes substantial logic background (Sider's *Logic for Philosophy* level); the lambda calculus is presupposed. The companion text **Chierchia & McConnell-Ginet**'s *Meaning and Grammar* (MIT, 2nd ed. 2000) is more elementary. | Graduate students in linguistics, philosophy of language, or cognitive science. The single text from which the contemporary formal semantics framework is best learned. |
| `[Primary]` Two-dimensional semantics and content `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Anthology]` | *Inquiry*, 1984, and *Context and Content*, 1999 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Robert Stalnaker** | The major statement of Stalnaker's possible-worlds approach to content: propositions as sets of possible worlds, belief as a set of worlds compatible with the agent's information state, the two-dimensional framework for indexical and contingent-a-priori cases, presupposition and assertion as essentially world-eliminating operations. *Inquiry* is the systematic monograph; *Context and Content* collects the major essays. Stalnaker's framework is the principal alternative to Russellian structured-proposition accounts (Soames, King) in contemporary philosophy of language. Limitation: the coarse-grained possible-worlds individuation of propositions has known problems (all logical truths express the same proposition; logically equivalent beliefs are identical), which Stalnaker addresses through his treatment of belief contexts but not all critics accept the responses. | Graduate students in philosophy of language or formal epistemology. See also Stalnaker's *Mere Possibilities* (Princeton, 2012) for the mature metaphysics. |
| `[Reference]` Formal semantics — comprehensive reference `[Anthology]` | *Logic, Language, and Meaning*, 2 vols., 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **L. T. F. Gamut** (collective pseudonym: van Benthem, Doets, van Eijck, Groenendijk, Stokhof) | The Dutch logico-semantic tradition's comprehensive textbook: Vol. 1 covers propositional and predicate logic with attention to philosophical applications; Vol. 2 covers intensional logic, possible-worlds semantics, generalised quantifiers, and dynamic semantics. The treatment is more philosophically oriented than Heim and Kratzer and includes substantial historical material. Limitation: extensive and demanding; for many readers Heim and Kratzer is the more practical entry point. | Graduate students wanting the philosophical-logical foundations of formal semantics. Less linguistics-oriented than Heim and Kratzer; more useful for the philosopher who wants to understand why the formalism takes the form it does. |

### 5.6 Pragmatics & Speech Act Theory

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Speech acts — founding text `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *How to Do Things with Words*, 1962 (2nd ed. 1975) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. L. Austin**; ed. **J. O. Urmson & Marina Sbisà** | The William James Lectures of 1955, posthumously edited: the founding work of speech act theory and the most influential text of Oxford ordinary-language philosophy. Austin begins with the constative/performative distinction, finds it untenable, and develops in its place the three-fold analysis of every utterance into locutionary (the saying), illocutionary (what is done in saying — promising, warning, asking), and perlocutionary (what is done by saying — persuading, frightening) acts. The framework reshaped philosophy of language and influenced linguistic pragmatics, jurisprudence (Hart's *Concept of Law*), and continental philosophy (Habermas, Searle vs. Derrida). Limitation: posthumously assembled from lecture notes; the systematic categorisation of illocutionary acts is Austin's *suggestive* taxonomy, not a worked-out theory. The 2nd edition incorporates Sbisà's editorial improvements. | Required reading. Short (~170 pp.) and exemplary of Austin's distinctive philosophical method. See also SEP: "Speech Acts." |
| `[Primary]` Speech act theory — systematic development `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Speech Acts: An Essay in the Philosophy of Language*, 1969 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **John R. Searle** | Searle's systematic development of speech act theory: the analysis of illocutionary acts in terms of constitutive rules, the distinction between propositional content and illocutionary force, the analysis of promising as a case study, and the famous derivation of "ought" from "is" through institutional facts. The book is Searle's most influential contribution to philosophy of language and has been the standard development of Austin's framework. Limitation: Searle's constitutive-rule analysis has been criticised as overly schematic, and the "ought from is" derivation through promising is contested both as an argument and as a reading of Hume. | Graduate students in philosophy of language. Pair with Searle's later *Expression and Meaning* (Cambridge, 1979) for the worked-out taxonomy of illocutionary acts. |
| `[Primary]` Implicature and conversational pragmatics `[Analytic]` `[Anthology]` `[Evergreen]` | *Studies in the Way of Words*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **H. P. Grice** | The collection of Grice's major papers, including "Logic and Conversation" (originally 1967 William James Lectures), "Utterer's Meaning, Sentence-Meaning, and Word-Meaning," and "Meaning." Grice's analysis of conversational implicature — the systematic ways speakers convey meanings beyond what is strictly said, governed by the cooperative principle and the maxims of quantity, quality, relation, and manner — is the foundation of modern pragmatics. The earlier "Meaning" paper develops the analysis of speaker meaning in terms of complex intentions. Limitation: Grice's maxims have been substantially refined or replaced in later pragmatics (Sperber and Wilson's *Relevance*; Levinson's neo-Gricean three-principle framework); the original framework remains the conceptual reference. | Required reading in philosophy of language and linguistics. See also SEP: "Implicature." |
| `[Research]` Inferentialist semantics and language `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Making It Explicit: Reasoning, Representing, and Discursive Commitment*, 1994 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Robert B. Brandom** | The major contemporary statement of inferentialist semantics: the meaning of an expression is constituted by its role in inferential practice — by the inferences in which it can correctly figure as premise or conclusion — rather than by its representational relation to a worldly correlate. The book systematically develops a normative pragmatics of assertion (in terms of commitments and entitlements), an inferentialist theory of propositional content, and an account of singular term reference. Brandom's project explicitly connects analytic philosophy of language to the Hegelian and idealist traditions (cf. §4.7 Pippin) and is a major bridge text. Limitation: enormous (~700 pp.) and forbiddingly difficult; Brandom's later *Articulating Reasons* (Harvard, 2000) is a much shorter introduction to the same project. | Graduate students in philosophy of language or the analytic/continental bridge tradition. Read *Articulating Reasons* first. |

### 5.7 Formal Epistemology — Bayesianism, Probability & Belief Revision

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Bayesian epistemology — argument for `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Scientific Reasoning: The Bayesian Approach*, 3rd ed., 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Colin Howson & Peter Urbach** | The most accessible sustained defence of Bayesian epistemology in English: the probability calculus as the logic of partial belief, Dutch book arguments for probabilism, conditionalisation as the rational response to evidence, Bayesian treatments of confirmation, simplicity, and theory choice in science. The book argues Bayesianism not as one option among several but as the correct framework for thinking about evidence and inference. Limitation: the case is presented partly polemically; for a more balanced treatment of objections (the problem of priors, the problem of old evidence, the conditionalisation/updating distinction) consult Joyce or the relevant SEP entries. | Graduate students in philosophy of science or formal epistemology. The natural starting point for understanding why Bayesianism is the default in much contemporary philosophy of science. See also SEP: "Bayesian Epistemology." |
| `[Systematic]` Formal epistemology — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *A Critical Introduction to Formal Epistemology*, 2015 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Darren Bradley** | The current best introductory textbook on formal epistemology: probability and Bayesian conditionalisation, accuracy-first epistemology, dempster-shafer and imprecise probability, decision theory under uncertainty, applications to confirmation theory and to the problem of induction. Bradley is critical where appropriate — he treats Bayesianism as one major framework among several rather than as the obvious truth. Limitation: introductory; for research-level coverage of each subtopic, the major monographs (Joyce, Pettigrew, Christensen, Easwaran) must be consulted. | Graduate students entering formal epistemology. Pair with Howson and Urbach for the partisan case for Bayesianism. |
| `[Research]` Accuracy-first epistemology `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Accuracy and the Laws of Credence*, 2016 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Richard Pettigrew** | The major recent monograph on accuracy-first epistemology: the project of grounding the laws of rational credence (probabilism, conditionalisation, the principal principle) in arguments that they minimise expected inaccuracy of belief, with inaccuracy measured by mathematically natural scoring rules. The book has reshaped formal epistemology by providing arguments for the Bayesian framework that do not depend on the contested Dutch book or representation-theorem approaches. Limitation: technically demanding; presupposes the formal epistemology literature it is responding to. | Researchers in formal epistemology. The current research frontier; not a first text on the subject. |

### 5.8 Truth — Correspondence, Deflationism, Coherence

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Truth — semantic conception `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "The Semantic Conception of Truth and the Foundations of Semantics", 1944 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alfred Tarski** | The accessible presentation of Tarski's formal definition of truth (originally developed in the 1933 "Concept of Truth in Formalized Languages"): truth as satisfaction-relative-to-language, the T-schema (*"snow is white" is true iff snow is white*), the indispensability of a metalanguage essentially richer than the object language for defining truth, and the proof of the indefinability of truth-in-L within L. Tarski's framework is the foundation of formal semantics (§5.5) and of most subsequent analytic discussion of truth. Limitation: Tarski's formal definition is sometimes claimed to be a *theory* of truth in a more substantive sense than Tarski intended (Davidson, Field, Etchemendy disagree about whether it is); the philosophical interpretation of the formal achievement is itself a substantial industry. | Required reading. Available in many anthologies; the *Philosophy and Phenomenological Research* original is the most-cited source. See also SEP: "Tarski's Truth Definitions." |
| `[Systematic]` Truth — deflationism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Truth*, 2nd ed., 1998 (1st ed. 1990) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Paul Horwich** | The major contemporary statement of minimalism (or deflationism) about truth: the thesis that all there is to truth is captured by the schema "the proposition that p is true if and only if p" — there is no substantive property of truth shared by all true propositions, and the philosophical puzzles about truth are dissolved rather than solved by recognising this. The book is the most rigorous development of the deflationist line, and the second edition substantially addresses critics. Limitation: minimalism faces well-known challenges from the liar paradox and from the apparent role of truth in inductive and theoretical reasoning (the success argument); Horwich's responses are not universally accepted. | Graduate students in philosophy of language or metaphysics. Read against Wright's *Truth and Objectivity* (Harvard, 1992) for the major alethic-pluralist alternative, and against Lynch's *Truth as One and Many* (Oxford, 2009) for functionalist pluralism. See also SEP: "The Deflationary Theory of Truth." |
| `[Reference]` Truth — comprehensive reference `[Anthology]` `[Reference]` | *The Oxford Handbook of Truth*, 2018 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Michael Glanzberg** (ed.) | The current research-level reference on theories of truth: 36 chapters covering correspondence, coherence, pragmatic, deflationary, primitivist, and pluralist theories; the relation to paradoxes; truth in formal semantics; truth-conditional vs. inferentialist meaning; truth in logic, ethics, and aesthetics. The chapters by Burgess and Burgess (on the paradoxes), Wright (on pluralism), Asay (on primitivism), and Field (on truth in mathematics) are particular contributions. Limitation: a reference rather than a continuous read; the price restricts it to library access for most readers. The earlier Lynch (ed.), *The Nature of Truth* (MIT, 2001), is a useful anthology of the seminal papers. | Researchers in philosophy of language, metaphysics, or epistemology. Consult chapter by chapter; not for linear reading. |

### Connections to other tables

This table is the formal-theoretical backbone for much of the rest of analytic philosophy: the logic and formal semantics tools developed here are presupposed in Table 6 (modality, ontological commitment, properties), Table 7 (mental content, externalism), and Table 8 (metaethics — Horwich's deflationism vs. Wright's pluralism reshape the metaethical landscape). Frege's foundations of arithmetic (§5.3) connect directly to the philosophy of mathematics in the Mathematics Atlas. Kripke's *Naming and Necessity* is the single most efficient bridge into Table 6 (modality, essence, the necessary a posteriori) and into Table 7 (the modal argument against the identity theory of mind). Putnam's externalism (§5.4) is the founding doctrine of content externalism in philosophy of mind (§7.4). Habermas's engagement with speech act theory (Table 9 §9.7) and Brandom's inferentialism (§5.6) connect this table to the analytic-continental bridge tradition. The formal-epistemology section (§5.7) connects to Bayesian approaches in philosophy of science and to decision theory in Table 8 (consequentialism). The Quine essays (§5.4) are also the methodological core of the §1.3 discussion of armchair philosophy and the analytic/synthetic distinction.

# Table 6 · Metaphysics, Ontology & Epistemology

*Scope:* Analytic epistemology (knowledge, justification, scepticism, virtue and social epistemology) and analytic metaphysics (existence, modality, time and persistence, causation and laws, properties and universals). David Lewis's systematic metaphysics is the centre of gravity for the metaphysical sections; the architecture of his programme is mapped explicitly.

### 6.1 Epistemology — Introductory Surveys

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Epistemology — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Epistemology: A Contemporary Introduction to the Theory of Knowledge*, 4th ed., 2020 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Robert Audi** | The standard graduate-level introductory textbook on epistemology in English: chapters on perception, memory, consciousness and introspection, reason and a priori knowledge, testimony, inductive and inferential knowledge, structures of justification (foundationalism, coherentism), virtue epistemology, scepticism, and the relation between knowledge and justification. Audi's exposition is even-handed across positions and the 4th edition incorporates the post-2000 developments in virtue and social epistemology. Limitation: a textbook in the classical sense — comprehensive but rarely advancing the dialectic; for research engagement consult the monographs in §§6.2–6.4. | The natural first reading for any serious engagement with analytic epistemology. See also SEP: "Epistemology." |
| `[Reference]` Epistemology — research orientation `[Anthology]` | *Contemporary Debates in Epistemology*, 2nd ed., 2014 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Matthias Steup, John Turri & Ernest Sosa** (eds.) | A debate-anthology in which leading epistemologists argue opposing positions on each of fourteen central questions (closure under known entailment, the nature of evidence, basing relations, internalism vs. externalism, contextualism, virtue and reliabilism, sceptical hypotheses). The format makes the live dialectic visible in a way that monographs cannot. Limitation: the chapters are short and presuppose familiarity with the literature; not a first text. | Graduate students orienting in the post-2000 epistemology landscape. Read after Audi. |

### 6.2 Epistemology — Scepticism & Its Responses

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Research]` Scepticism — major monograph `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Significance of Philosophical Scepticism*, 1984 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Barry Stroud** | The major late-20th-century study of philosophical scepticism: a sustained argument that the standard responses (Moorean, Austinian, Carnapian, Quinean) all fail to engage with the philosophical sceptic on her own terms and therefore fail as responses to the philosophical problem, even where they are decisive against ordinary doubt. Stroud reads the sceptical argument as raising a question about the very intelligibility of our concept of knowledge of the external world — a question that may not have a satisfying answer. The book has shaped the field by making clear that "responding to scepticism" cannot mean merely showing that we have many particular pieces of knowledge. Limitation: Stroud is sympathetic to but does not endorse scepticism; readers expecting either a refutation or a defence of scepticism will find the more aporetic conclusion frustrating, which is itself part of the philosophical point. | Graduate students in epistemology. The book against which subsequent treatments of scepticism (Williams, Pritchard, DeRose) define themselves. See also SEP: "Skepticism." |
| `[Primary]` Sensitivity-based response to scepticism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Philosophical Explanations*, Part III, 1981 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Robert Nozick** | The founding statement of the *sensitivity* (or *tracking*) account of knowledge: S knows p iff S believes p, p is true, and if p were false S would not believe p (plus a "method" parameter for the closest worlds in which p is false). The account dissolves Cartesian scepticism by denying the closure principle: I can know I have hands without knowing I am not a brain-in-a-vat, since my belief that I have hands is sensitive (would track) while my belief that I am not a brain-in-a-vat is not. Nozick's combination — endorse anti-scepticism, sacrifice closure — has been hugely influential and hugely controversial. Limitation: the denial of closure is widely regarded as too high a price (DeRose, Williamson); subsequent tracking accounts (Roush) refine the framework while preserving closure. | Graduate students in epistemology. Read alongside Stroud and DeRose's contextualism for the major options on scepticism. Part III is roughly 300 pp.; the rest of *Philosophical Explanations* (on personal identity, free will, ethics) is independently valuable. |
| `[Research]` Scepticism — contextual dissolution `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Unnatural Doubts: Epistemological Realism and the Basis of Scepticism*, 1996 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Michael Williams** | The major alternative response to philosophical scepticism: Williams argues that scepticism depends on a particular substantive epistemological doctrine — *epistemological realism*, on which our beliefs about the external world form a privileged epistemic kind that can be uniformly evaluated — and that abandoning this doctrine dissolves the sceptical problem without conceding to it. Williams's diagnostic is the most fully developed contextual response to scepticism. Limitation: the diagnosis is itself a substantive thesis and is rejected by Stroud and others who think the sceptical problem survives the contextualisation. | Graduate students in epistemology. Read alongside Stroud; the two together exhibit the dominant late-20th-century alternatives. |

### 6.3 Foundationalism, Coherentism & Reliabilism

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Research]` Coherentism — major statement `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Structure of Empirical Knowledge*, 1985 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Laurence BonJour** | The major contemporary defence of epistemic coherentism: the thesis that the justification of empirical beliefs derives not from foundational beliefs immune to error, but from the holistic coherence of a system of beliefs. BonJour addresses the standard objections (the isolation objection, the alternative coherent systems objection) and develops a sophisticated version of coherentism with a clear account of how observation enters belief revision. Famously, BonJour later renounced coherentism (in *In Defense of Pure Reason*, 1998) and returned to a moderate foundationalism — the book remains the best presentation of the position he subsequently abandoned. Limitation: the alternative-systems objection has not been definitively answered by any coherentist, including BonJour. | Graduate students in epistemology. Read alongside Goldman below for the major contrast between coherentism and reliabilism. See also SEP: "Coherentist Theories of Epistemic Justification." |
| `[Research]` Reliabilism — founding monograph `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Epistemology and Cognition*, 1986 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alvin I. Goldman** | The founding monograph of process reliabilism: the thesis that a belief is justified just in case it is produced by a reliable cognitive process — one that tends to produce more true beliefs than false ones — under appropriate conditions. The book is also a sustained argument for naturalised epistemology — the integration of normative epistemology with empirical cognitive psychology. Goldman's reliabilism is the dominant externalist position in epistemology and has produced a substantial subsequent literature (the generality problem, the new evil demon, virtue reliabilism). Limitation: the generality problem (how to specify the relevant process type) remains unsolved; the new evil demon problem (intuitively, a brain-in-a-vat with the same belief-forming processes as me has justified beliefs even though the processes are unreliable in its environment) is the principal internalist objection. | Graduate students in epistemology. Pair with BonJour. See also SEP: "Reliabilist Epistemology." |
| `[Research]` Knowledge-first epistemology `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Knowledge and Its Limits*, 2000 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Timothy Williamson** | The major work of late-20th-century epistemology and the founding text of the "knowledge-first" programme: knowledge is treated as the fundamental epistemological concept, not analysable into more basic notions (belief + truth + justification + anti-Gettier conditions), but rather the explanans of justification, evidence, assertion, and the propositional attitudes. The book also contains Williamson's anti-luminosity argument (against the claim that we can know whether we know p whenever we know p), the influential account of evidence as knowledge (E = K), and a sustained case against the post-Gettier industry of analysing knowledge. The combination has reshaped epistemology more than any single book since the 1960s.
