Limitation: technical and demanding; Williamson's positive arguments depend on margin-for-error principles that have been disputed. | Graduate students and researchers in epistemology. Required reading for anyone working in the field post-2000. Pair with Greco's *Achieving Knowledge* for the virtue-theoretic alternative. See also SEP: "Knowledge First." |

### 6.4 Virtue Epistemology & Social Epistemology

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Research]` Virtue epistemology — major monograph `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *A Virtue Epistemology: Apt Belief and Reflective Knowledge*, Vol. 1, 2007 (Vol. 2: *Reflective Knowledge*, 2009) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Ernest Sosa** | The systematic development of Sosa's virtue-theoretic epistemology: belief as a performance evaluable in terms of three Aristotelian dimensions — accurate, adroit (manifesting cognitive virtue), and apt (accurate *because* adroit). Sosa distinguishes *animal knowledge* (apt belief) from *reflective knowledge* (apt belief aptly believed to be apt), accommodating both reliabilist intuitions about animals and young children and internalist intuitions about reflective epistemic agents. The framework is now the dominant virtue-theoretic position in Anglophone epistemology. Limitation: Sosa's framework is sufficiently flexible that critics (Lackey, Pritchard) have argued it accommodates competing intuitions rather than adjudicating between them. | Graduate students in epistemology. Pair with Zagzebski's *Virtues of the Mind* (Cambridge, 1996) for the responsibilist alternative emphasising character-virtues over reliabilist faculty-virtues. See also SEP: "Virtue Epistemology." |
| `[Research]` Social epistemology — major monograph `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *Epistemic Injustice: Power and the Ethics of Knowing*, 2007 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Miranda Fricker** | The founding work of one of the most influential subfields in 21st-century epistemology. Fricker identifies two forms of *epistemic injustice* — *testimonial injustice* (when prejudice causes a hearer to give a speaker less credibility than she deserves) and *hermeneutical injustice* (when gaps in collective interpretive resources prevent someone from making sense of her social experience). The book bridges analytic epistemology with political philosophy and feminist philosophy and has shaped both the analytic and broader public discussions of testimony and credibility. Limitation: short and synoptic — the framework opens questions that subsequent literature (Medina, Dotson, Pohlhaus) has had to develop further. | Graduate students in epistemology, political philosophy, or feminist philosophy. Pair with Lackey's *Learning from Words: Testimony as a Source of Knowledge* (Oxford, 2008) for the standard analytic treatment of testimony. |
| `[Research]` Testimony — major monograph `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Learning from Words: Testimony as a Source of Knowledge*, 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Jennifer Lackey** | The major contemporary monograph on testimony as a source of knowledge. Lackey defends a non-reductionist view on which testimonial knowledge is a basic kind that cannot be reduced to inductive justification from speaker reliability, while also rejecting the strong assurance views (Moran, Hinchman) that ground testimonial uptake in interpersonal trust. The middle position — "statement-based" non-reductionism — has been widely influential. Limitation: focused specifically on testimonial knowledge; for the political and social dimensions, supplement with Fricker. | Graduate students in epistemology. Pair with the *Oxford Handbook of Social Epistemology* (Goldman & Whitcomb, eds., 2011). See also SEP: "Epistemological Problems of Testimony." |

### 6.5 Metaphysics — Introductory Surveys

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Metaphysics — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Metaphysics: A Contemporary Introduction*, 4th ed., 2017 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Michael J. Loux & Thomas M. Crisp** | The standard graduate-level metaphysics textbook in English: chapters on universals, particulars, propositions and modality, causation, necessity, time, identity through time, concrete particulars, and realism vs. anti-realism. Loux is uncommonly clear on the relations between positions and the costs of each. Limitation: a textbook — even-handed coverage at the cost of depth on any single topic; for research engagement consult the monographs in §§6.6–6.10. | Graduate students entering analytic metaphysics. The natural first reading; pair with Sider's *Logic for Philosophy* (§5.1) for the formal toolkit. See also SEP: "Metaphysics." |
| `[Systematic]` Metaphysics — alternative survey `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *A Survey of Metaphysics*, 2002 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **E. J. Lowe** | An alternative single-author survey by a major metaphysician working in a more Aristotelian-essentialist tradition than the dominant Lewisian framework: chapters on identity, necessity, modality, universals, substance, causation, time, persistence, agency, persons. Lowe's own neo-Aristotelian commitments are visible throughout, which makes the book a useful counterweight to the Lewis-dominated mainstream. Limitation: written from a substantive philosophical standpoint, not a neutral textbook; pair with Loux for the more conventional coverage. | Graduate students in metaphysics interested in non-Lewisian alternatives. See also Lowe's *The Possibility of Metaphysics* (Oxford, 1998) for the systematic Aristotelian-essentialist position. |

### 6.6 Existence, Ontological Commitment & Quantification

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Ontological commitment `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "On What There Is", 1948 (in *From a Logical Point of View*, Harvard, 1953) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **W. V. O. Quine** | The founding paper of analytic ontology: Quine's argument that to ask what there is, ask what we are committed to by the truth of our best theories — and that the criterion of ontological commitment is just that the predicate variables of canonical regimentation must have something to range over. "To be is to be the value of a variable." The paper's reductive treatment of "Plato's beard" (how can we say what does not exist?) by paraphrase into existentially quantified form has shaped Anglophone ontology for seventy years. Limitation: Quine's deflationary picture of ontological dispute has been challenged by neo-Carnapians (Thomasson, Hirsch) on one side and neo-Aristotelians (Fine, Schaffer) on the other; the meta-ontological landscape has expanded substantially beyond Quine's framework. | Required reading. See also Quine's "Ontological Relativity" (1968) and the Carnap–Quine exchange. SEP: "Quine on Ontology" and "Logic and Ontology." |
| `[Reference]` Meta-ontology — major anthology `[Analytic]` `[Anthology]` | *Metametaphysics: New Essays on the Foundations of Ontology*, 2009 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **David Chalmers, David Manley & Ryan Wasserman** (eds.) | The volume that reopened meta-ontology as a research subfield: 17 essays on whether ontological disputes are substantive (Hirsch, Sider), whether existence is a univocal notion (Fine, McDaniel), the role of fundamentality and grounding (Schaffer), the relation between metaphysics and natural language (Thomasson), and the place of intuitions in metaphysical theorising. Sider's "Ontological Realism," Hirsch's "Ontology and Alternative Languages," and Fine's "The Question of Ontology" are individual landmarks. Limitation: an anthology — readers must assemble the dialectic across multiple essays; the price restricts it to library access for most readers. | Graduate students in metaphysics. The starting point for engagement with post-2000 meta-ontology. |
| `[Research]` Structure-driven metaphysics `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Writing the Book of the World*, 2011 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Theodore Sider** | The major recent systematic statement of structure-realism in metaphysics: the world has a "joint-carving" structure, and the goal of metaphysics is to produce a description that limns this structure. Sider extends the framework from the Lewisian doctrine of natural properties (cf. §6.10) to logical and quantificational structure (existence quantifier is a "joint-carving" notion), to mereological and temporal structure, and to the meta-ontology of ontology itself. The book is the most ambitious metametaphysical proposal of the post-2000 period. Limitation: Sider's structure-realism faces an iteration problem (what makes some notions joint-carving?) and a methodological challenge from the deflationists who deny that there is a privileged ontological vocabulary at all. | Graduate students and researchers in metaphysics. Read after the Chalmers–Manley–Wasserman anthology. |

### 6.7 Modality — Possible Worlds, Essence & Necessity

**The architecture of Lewis's modal metaphysics.** David Lewis's modal realism is the most ambitious systematic metaphysics in late-20th-century analytic philosophy. The doctrine — that possible worlds are concrete, fully real, spatiotemporally and causally isolated cosmoi numerically distinct from our own — is defended on grounds of theoretical utility: it permits a uniform reduction of modal talk, counterfactuals (Lewis 1973), counterpart-theoretic identity across worlds, propositions and properties as sets of worlds (and world-individual pairs), and the framework for the systematic semantics of §5.5. The modal realism cannot be separated from these consequences: rejecting concrete worlds (in favour of actualist alternatives — Plantinga, Stalnaker, Adams) requires reconstructing each of these reductions on weaker resources. The entries below are best read as a single architectural choice with multiple facets, against the major actualist and neo-Aristotelian alternatives.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Counterfactual analysis `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Counterfactuals*, 1973 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Lewis** | Lewis's analysis of counterfactuals in terms of similarity-ordered possible worlds: "if A had been the case, C would have been the case" is true at world *w* iff some A-and-C world is closer to *w* than any A-and-not-C world. The framework is the foundation of Lewis's later analyses of causation (§6.9), of dispositions, and of much of the rest of the metaphysical edifice. The book also contains the system of conditional logic (VC) that Lewis defends, the treatment of "cotenability" approaches to counterfactuals, and the application to causation in the closing chapter. Limitation: the similarity ordering on worlds is left philosophically unanalysed and is sometimes accused of being trimmed to fit; competing accounts (Stalnaker's earlier, structural-equation accounts in Pearl and Woodward, the difference-making views) have all built on Lewis's framework. | Graduate students in modal metaphysics. Pair with Stalnaker's "A Theory of Conditionals" (1968) for the close ancestor that differs on the limit and uniqueness assumptions. |
| `[Primary]` Modal realism — magnum opus `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *On the Plurality of Worlds*, 1986 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Lewis** | The systematic defence of modal realism: there really are infinitely many concrete possible worlds, exactly as real as our own. The book opens with the long "paradise on the cheap" case for the doctrine — its theoretical utility justifies its ontological extravagance — and then defends it against actualist alternatives (ersatz modal realism), addresses the standard objections (the incredulous stare, the relevance objection, the actuality objection), and develops the framework's applications to properties (as sets of possible individuals), propositions, causation, and counterpart theory. Modal realism's incredulity is real and Lewis is candid about it: he asks the reader to weigh it against the theoretical benefits, not to find it intuitively comfortable. Limitation: the actualist competitors (ersatz modal realism — Plantinga's worlds as maximal states of affairs, Stalnaker's worlds as ways the world could be) avoid the incredulity at some cost to the systematicity of Lewis's reductions; the question of which side of the trade-off is the right one remains live. | Graduate students and researchers in modal metaphysics. The single most ambitious metaphysical book of the past fifty years. See also SEP: "David Lewis," "Modal Realism," and "Possible Worlds." |
| `[Primary]` Actualist alternative `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Nature of Necessity*, 1974 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alvin Plantinga** | The major actualist alternative to Lewis's modal realism: Plantinga treats possible worlds as maximal abstract states of affairs (entities exemplifying particular complete properties), defends essentialism about individuals (each individual has an *individual essence* — a property essentially exemplified by it alone), and uses the framework to develop the free-will defence against the argument from evil and a version of the ontological argument. The book is the foundational actualist programme and the principal alternative framework to Lewis's. Limitation: Plantinga's individual essences (haecceities) are themselves controversial entities, and the actualist's appeal to abstract states of affairs faces its own questions of intelligibility. | Graduate students in modal metaphysics. Read against Lewis for the major architectural alternative. See also Stalnaker's *Mere Possibilities* (Princeton, 2012) for the more recent linguistic-actualist version. |
| `[Research]` Modal logic as metaphysics `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Modal Logic as Metaphysics*, 2013 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Timothy Williamson** | The major recent monograph on modal metaphysics: Williamson defends *necessitism* (the thesis that everything that exists exists necessarily — possibilia are not merely possible but actual necessary existents) on the basis of the strongest classical quantified modal logic. The book combines technical work in higher-order modal logic with a sustained metaphysical argument that the natural quantified modal logic for metaphysical necessity is essentially Williamson's. Limitation: necessitism is intuitively startling (Socrates exists necessarily, though not as a human being or as concrete); the dialectical case rests on the strength of the formal arguments against weaker logics. | Researchers in modal metaphysics. Technical; presupposes substantial modal logic background (Sider §5.1 plus more). |

### 6.8 Time, Persistence & Personal Identity

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` McTaggart on the A and B series `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "The Unreality of Time", 1908 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. M. E. McTaggart** | The founding paper of the analytic metaphysics of time: McTaggart distinguishes the *A-series* (past, present, future) from the *B-series* (earlier-than, simultaneous-with, later-than), argues that the A-series is essential to time, and that the A-series is contradictory (every event is past, present, and future, on pain of presupposing temporal locations to make the distinction non-contradictory). The argument is widely regarded as containing fallacies, but the A/B distinction has structured every subsequent debate. Limitation: the argument *itself* is generally rejected; the *framework* is indispensable. The paper is in McTaggart's *Mind* 1908 and in many anthologies. | Required reading in metaphysics of time. Pair with Mellor's *Real Time II* (Routledge, 1998) for the major B-theoretic response and Markosian's "A Defense of Presentism" for the A-theoretic alternative. See also SEP: "Time." |
| `[Research]` Persistence — four-dimensionalism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Four-Dimensionalism: An Ontology of Persistence and Time*, 2001 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Theodore Sider** | The major contemporary defence of four-dimensionalism (perdurance theory): material objects persist through time by having distinct temporal parts at distinct times, the way they extend through space by having distinct spatial parts at distinct places. Sider defends the doctrine against three-dimensionalist alternatives (endurance theory) and uses it to address the classical puzzles of persistence (the problem of temporary intrinsics, the statue and the lump, the problem of vague identity). The book is the standard reference for four-dimensionalism and has reshaped the debate. Limitation: Sider's argument for four-dimensionalism from vagueness has been contested (Markosian, Lowe); the eternalist B-theoretic framework on which four-dimensionalism most naturally rides is itself contestable. | Graduate students in metaphysics. The single most useful book for understanding why four-dimensionalism is the dominant view in current analytic metaphysics. |
| `[Primary]` Personal identity — major work `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Reasons and Persons*, 1984 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Derek Parfit** | One of the most influential books in 20th-century analytic philosophy: Part III develops the *reductionist* view of personal identity — what matters in survival is psychological continuity and connectedness (the Lockean tradition radicalised), and *identity* is not what matters because identity can fail to hold determinately in puzzle cases (fission, fusion, gradual replacement) while what matters in survival can hold in degree. Parts I, II, and IV apply the framework to the structure of practical reasoning, time and discounting, future generations, and population ethics (including the founding statement of the Repugnant Conclusion). Limitation: the reductionist conclusion has been contested by animalists (Olson — below) and by neo-Lockeans who reject the fission cases as decisive; Parfit himself revised significant elements of his view in later work (*On What Matters*). | Required reading in personal identity, practical reason, and population ethics. The book is enormous (~550 pp.) and rewards selective reading; Part III is the personal-identity core. See also SEP: "Personal Identity." |
| `[Research]` Personal identity — animalism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *The Human Animal: Personal Identity Without Psychological Continuity*, 1997 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Eric T. Olson** | The major contemporary statement of animalism: you and I are human animals, identity over time is animal-biological identity, and the psychological-continuity tradition (Locke through Parfit) is mistaken because it falsifies our nature as biological organisms. Olson presses the *thinking animal problem* against the neo-Lockean: if persons and animals are distinct but coincident, there are two thinkers for every one of "us." The book has reshaped the personal identity debate by making animalism a serious competitor to the dominant neo-Lockean framework. Limitation: animalism faces difficulties with cases (fetuses, vegetative-state patients, the transplantation puzzles) where the psychological criteria seem decisive. | Graduate students in personal identity or philosophy of mind. Read against Parfit; the contrast is one of the major contemporary metaphysical disputes. |

### 6.9 Causation & Laws of Nature

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Causation — INUS conditions `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Cement of the Universe: A Study of Causation*, 1974 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. L. Mackie** | The major mid-20th-century Humean treatment of causation: Mackie develops the *INUS condition* analysis — A is a cause of B iff A is an Insufficient but Necessary part of an Unnecessary but Sufficient condition for B — and applies it to causal claims, causal explanation, the directionality of causation, and the relation between causation and laws of nature. The book is a more sophisticated regularity theory than Hume's, with explicit attention to the difficulties (overdetermination, pre-emption, transitivity) that subsequent counterfactual and probabilistic theories have addressed differently. Limitation: regularity theories generally have difficulty with pre-emption cases; Mackie's responses, though ingenious, have not been universally accepted. The Lewis counterfactual analysis (next entry) has been dominant since 1973. | Graduate students in metaphysics of causation. Read alongside Lewis on causation. See also SEP: "The Metaphysics of Causation." |
| `[Primary]` Causation — Lewis's counterfactual analysis `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | "Causation" (1973) and the postscripts (1986), in *Philosophical Papers Vol. II* (Oxford, 1986) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Lewis** | Lewis's counterfactual analysis: c causes e iff (roughly) had c not occurred, e would not have occurred, where counterfactuals are analysed by similarity of possible worlds (cf. §6.7). The original 1973 paper plus the 1986 postscripts handle pre-emption through stepwise causal chains and addresses overdetermination through a more nuanced account of the causal counterfactuals. Lewis's later "Causation as Influence" (2000) substantially revised the framework. The counterfactual analysis is the dominant theory in metaphysics, and contemporary work (Hall, Paul, Hitchcock, Woodward — though Woodward's interventionism is partly independent) builds on it. Limitation: Lewis's analysis has had persistent trouble with late pre-emption and double-prevention cases; the literature is now substantial enough to fill a substantial Oxford Handbook (Beebee, Hitchcock, Menzies, eds., 2009). | Graduate students in metaphysics of causation. The papers are short; read with the Beebee–Hitchcock–Menzies *Oxford Handbook of Causation* for the current state of the field. |
| `[Research]` Laws of nature — DTA theory `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *What Is a Law of Nature?*, 1983 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **D. M. Armstrong** | The major statement of the *Dretske–Tooley–Armstrong* (DTA) nomic-necessitation view of laws: a law of nature is a relation of contingent necessitation between universals (F-ness *necessitates* G-ness), not a Humean regularity. The view explains why laws support counterfactuals, why generalisations from samples are projectible, and why "all the coins in my pocket are copper" is not a law while "all electrons have charge -e" is. The principal alternative is the Humean best-system analysis (Lewis 1973, Loewer): laws are the contingent generalisations in the deductively closed system that best balances strength and simplicity over the totality of particular matters of fact. Limitation: the DTA theory's nomic-necessitation relation is itself philosophically primitive and has been criticised as obscure; the Humean alternative avoids the obscure relation at the cost of giving up the explanatory connection between laws and counterfactuals. | Graduate students in metaphysics or philosophy of science. Read alongside Lewis's "Humean Supervenience Debugged" (1994) and Maudlin's *The Metaphysics Within Physics* (Oxford, 2007) for the major alternatives. See also SEP: "Laws of Nature." |

### 6.10 Properties, Universals & Abstract Objects

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Research]` Universals — immanent realism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Universals: An Opinionated Introduction*, 1989 (longer treatment: *Universals and Scientific Realism*, 2 vols., 1978) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **D. M. Armstrong** | The major contemporary defence of *immanent realism* about universals: universals are real properties shared by their instances, but they exist only in their instances (against Platonism) and they are sparsely distributed (only those properties figuring in the laws of nature qualify as universals, against abundant theories). The shorter *Universals: An Opinionated Introduction* (Westview, 1989) is the accessible version of the position developed at length in *Universals and Scientific Realism* (Cambridge, 1978). Armstrong's framework is the standard sparse-realist alternative to nominalism and to Lewisian abundant property realism. Limitation: Armstrong's commitment to a *combinatorial* theory of possibility and to a sparse ontology has been contested by Lewis (who needs abundant properties for his semantics) and by neo-Aristotelian essentialists (who want richer essences than Armstrong's universals deliver). | Graduate students in metaphysics. Read with Lewis's "New Work for a Theory of Universals" (below) for the major contrast. See also SEP: "Properties." |
| `[Primary]` Properties — Lewisian natural properties `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | "New Work for a Theory of Universals", 1983 (in *Philosophical Papers Vol. I*, Oxford, 1983) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Lewis** | The paper in which Lewis grants what he had previously resisted: there must be a privileged class of *natural* properties to do the metaphysical work of grounding objective similarity, the analysis of intrinsic properties, the constitution of laws of nature (the best-system analysis works only if simplicity is measured in a language of natural predicates), the analysis of intrinsic duplication, and much else. Lewis remains a nominalist about properties — for him, properties are still sets of (possible) individuals — but he accepts a sparse-elite subset of natural properties as metaphysically privileged. The paper is the founding document of contemporary work on naturalness (Sider, Schaffer, Dorr) and is the centre of one of the most productive metaphysical research programmes of the past forty years. Limitation: the metaphysical status of the natural/non-natural distinction is itself disputed — Lewis treats naturalness as a primitive, others as derivable from more fundamental structure. | Graduate students and researchers in metaphysics. Read alongside Armstrong. See also SEP: "Natural Properties." |
| `[Research]` Nominalism about mathematical objects `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Science Without Numbers: A Defense of Nominalism*, 1980 (2nd ed. 2016) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hartry Field** | The most influential contemporary defence of nominalism about mathematical objects: Field argues that the apparent indispensability of mathematics to natural science (the Quine–Putnam argument) can be defeated by showing that natural science can in principle be reformulated without reference to numbers, sets, or other abstract entities — and he carries out the reformulation for Newtonian gravitational physics in the second half of the book. The 2nd edition (2016) adds substantial new material responding to critics. Field's case is the major contemporary alternative to Platonism about mathematical entities. Limitation: the program has had difficulty extending beyond the specific physical theory Field treats — applications to quantum mechanics, statistical mechanics, and general relativity remain contested or incomplete. Burgess and Rosen's *A Subject With No Object* (Oxford, 1997) is the systematic critical assessment of nominalist strategies. | Graduate students in philosophy of mathematics or metaphysics of abstract objects. Pair with Linnebo's *Philosophy of Mathematics* (Princeton, 2017) for the broader landscape. See also SEP: "Nominalism in the Philosophy of Mathematics" and the Mathematics Atlas. |

### Connections to other tables

The formal toolkit for this table — quantification, modal logic, possible-worlds semantics — is the subject of Table 5; Sider's *Logic for Philosophy* and Heim–Kratzer's *Semantics in Generative Grammar* are presupposed by most monographs cited here. Kripke's *Naming and Necessity* (§5.4) is the immediate background to §6.7 (modality) and §6.8 (personal identity — Kripke's argument for the necessity of origin). Williamson's *Knowledge and Its Limits* (§6.3) is the key crossover with §6.7 (Williamson's necessitism is the modal-metaphysical face of the knowledge-first programme). The Parfit/Olson debate on personal identity (§6.8) connects directly to Table 7 (philosophy of mind) and to §8.6 (population ethics, Parfit's later work). Field's nominalism (§6.10) connects to the philosophy-of-mathematics sections of the Mathematics Atlas. The laws-of-nature debates (§6.9) connect to the Physics Atlas treatments of physical laws and to the philosophical interpretation of quantum mechanics (Maudlin's *The Metaphysics Within Physics*). The Lewis architecture mapped in §6.7 underlies Lewis's work in §6.9 (causation), §6.10 (properties), §5.4 (compositional semantics), and §7.2 (functionalism in philosophy of mind) — a single systematic vision threading through five of these nine tables.

# Table 7 · Philosophy of Mind, Consciousness & Cognitive Science

*Scope:* The mind-body problem, consciousness and the hard problem, intentionality and mental content, action and free will, the philosophical foundations of cognitive science, embodied and enactive cognition (the principal contemporary bridge between phenomenology and analytic philosophy of mind), and philosophy of psychology. Predominantly analytic, with the §7.7 bridge texts as a major exception that should not be marginalised.

### 7.1 Philosophy of Mind — Introductory Surveys

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Philosophy of mind — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Philosophy of Mind*, 4th ed., 2019 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Jaegwon Kim** (rev. by **Brian P. McLaughlin** in 4th ed.) | The standard graduate-level textbook in English: chapters on Cartesian dualism, behaviourism, the identity theory, functionalism (machine and causal-theoretic), the computational theory of mind, mental causation, consciousness and qualia, and the mental content problem. Kim's own commitments (against non-reductive physicalism, in favour of either reductionism or limited eliminativism) inflect the exposition but are made explicit. The 4th edition (McLaughlin's revision after Kim's death) substantially updates the consciousness chapter. Limitation: like most textbooks, even-handed coverage at the cost of depth; for research engagement consult the monographs below. | The first text in any graduate-level philosophy of mind sequence. See also SEP: "Philosophy of Mind." |
| `[Primary]` Foundational analytic essay `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | "Empiricism and the Philosophy of Mind" (in *Science, Perception and Reality*, 1963; standalone Harvard, 1997) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Wilfrid Sellars** | The single most influential essay in 20th-century analytic philosophy of mind: Sellars's attack on the *Myth of the Given* — the doctrine that empirical knowledge rests on a foundation of cognitive states that are at once non-conceptual and epistemically authoritative. Sellars argues that the ostensibly cognitive episodes of foundationalist epistemology are themselves conceptual achievements operating in the "space of reasons," and develops an inferentialist conception of meaning and a functional-role account of mental states ("Jonesian" theory) that founded much of subsequent analytic philosophy of mind and language. The essay is the founding text of the Pittsburgh school (McDowell, Brandom — see §9.10). Limitation: notoriously difficult; Sellars's dense and sometimes oblique prose has shaped a substantial commentary literature (Rosenberg, deVries, Brandom). The Harvard 1997 standalone edition with Rorty's introduction and Brandom's commentary is the recommended format. | Required reading for graduate students in epistemology or philosophy of mind. Read with Brandom's *Study Guide* in the Harvard edition. See also SEP: "Wilfrid Sellars." |
| `[Reference]` Philosophy of mind — research orientation `[Anthology]` `[Reference]` | *The Oxford Handbook of Philosophy of Mind*, 2009 (rev. ed. 2018) `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **Brian P. McLaughlin, Ansgar Beckermann & Sven Walter** (eds.) | The current standard research-level reference: 45 chapters covering the mind-body problem in its current state, consciousness, content, perception, action, mental causation, the unity of mind, animal minds, cognitive science, and the historical background. Particularly strong chapters by Block (consciousness), Burge (content), Burge and Yablo (mental causation), and Bermúdez (animal minds). Limitation: as with all such handbooks, uneven across chapters; consult by topic rather than reading linearly. | Graduate students entering specific subfields. Consult before the monographs in the relevant subsection. |

### 7.2 The Mind-Body Problem — Dualism, Physicalism & Functionalism

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Mind-brain identity theory `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Sensations and Brain Processes", 1959 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. J. C. Smart** | The founding statement of the type-identity theory of mind: mental states (sensations, pains, experiences) are *type-identical* with brain states, not merely correlated with them; the relation is the kind of contingent identity exhibited by "the morning star is the evening star" or "water is H₂O." Smart's argument from Occam's razor — that the postulation of non-physical mental states adds nothing to the explanatory work the physical states are already doing — has shaped subsequent physicalist argument. Together with U. T. Place's "Is Consciousness a Brain Process?" (1956), this paper founded contemporary physicalism. Limitation: type-identity is now widely held to founder on the multiple-realisability argument (Putnam 1967 — see below); the *token-identity* descendants and functionalism replaced it. The paper is in many anthologies (Chalmers's *Philosophy of Mind: Classical and Contemporary Readings* is the standard). | Read for the historical baseline against which functionalism and contemporary physicalism define themselves. See also SEP: "The Identity Theory of Mind." |
| `[Primary]` Functionalism — founding paper `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "The Nature of Mental States", 1967 (originally "Psychological Predicates") `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hilary Putnam** | The founding statement of functionalism: mental state types are *functional* types defined by their causal-functional role (typical input → mental state → typical output), not by their physical realisers. Putnam's *multiple-realisability* argument — that the same functional role (e.g. being in pain) can be realised in carbon-based brains, silicon computers, hypothetical Martian hydraulics — became the standard objection to type-identity theory. Functionalism in various forms (machine functionalism, causal-role functionalism, teleo-functionalism) has remained the dominant non-eliminativist physicalist option ever since. Limitation: functionalism faces the absent-qualia and inverted-spectrum objections (Block 1978) — functionally equivalent systems may differ in their phenomenal character; this is now the central objection. Putnam himself later abandoned functionalism (*Representation and Reality*, 1988). | Read with Block's "Troubles with Functionalism" (1978) and Lewis's "Mad Pain and Martian Pain" (1980) for the principal refinement. See also SEP: "Functionalism." |
| `[Primary]` Anomalous monism `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Mental Events", 1970 (in *Essays on Actions and Events*, Oxford, 1980) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Donald Davidson** | Davidson's distinctive contribution to the mind-body debate: *anomalous monism* — there are no strict psychophysical laws connecting mental and physical descriptions (anomaly of the mental), but every event with a mental description also has a physical description and the same events thus belong to both categories (token-identity monism). The argument depends on three premises: principle of causal interaction (some mental events cause physical events), principle of nomological character of causation (causation requires laws), and anomalism of the mental (no strict laws connect mental and physical predicates). Davidson's view has been criticised as making mental properties causally inert (Kim's exclusion argument — below); the question of mental causation under non-reductive physicalism has been Davidson's principal legacy. | Read alongside Kim's "Mental Causation" or *Mind in a Physical World* (next entry) for the exclusion problem. See also SEP: "Anomalous Monism." |
| `[Research]` Mental causation and the exclusion problem `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Mind in a Physical World: An Essay on the Mind-Body Problem and Mental Causation*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jaegwon Kim** | The major late-20th-century argument against non-reductive physicalism. Kim's *causal exclusion argument*: if every physical effect has a complete physical cause (the causal closure of the physical) and mental properties are not identical to physical properties (non-reductionism), then mental properties are either causally redundant (epiphenomenal) or systematically overdetermine their effects — neither acceptable. The argument forces a choice between reductive physicalism and giving up genuine mental causation. The book is the most influential statement of the case and has shaped the dialectic in philosophy of mind ever since. Limitation: the argument depends on a specific (Kim-style) conception of properties and of the supervenience relation; non-reductive physicalists (Yablo, Pereboom, Bennett) have offered responses by reconceiving the property-individuation or the realisation relation. | Required reading on mental causation. Pair with Block's "Do Causal Powers Drain Away?" for the major critical response. See also SEP: "Mental Causation." |

### 7.3 Consciousness — The Hard Problem & Phenomenal Experience

**The hard problem of consciousness, schematically.** David Chalmers's 1995 paper "Facing Up to the Problem of Consciousness" and his 1996 monograph *The Conscious Mind* distinguish the *easy problems* (explaining how the brain performs cognitive functions — discrimination, integration, reportability, attention) from the *hard problem* (explaining why there is phenomenal experience accompanying these functions at all). The easy problems are tractable by ordinary methods of cognitive neuroscience; the hard problem, Chalmers argues, is not, because no functional account explains *why* the functional processes are accompanied by experience. The contemporary landscape divides over how to respond: **Type-A physicalism** (Dennett, Frankish's illusionism) denies that there is anything more to consciousness than the functional facts; **Type-B physicalism** (Block, Loar) accepts the explanatory gap but holds that phenomenal and physical concepts pick out the same properties; **Type-C physicalism** holds that the gap is closable in principle but not yet; **Type-D dualism** (Chalmers's own naturalistic dualism) treats phenomenal properties as fundamental but lawfully linked to physical properties; **panpsychism** (Strawson, Goff) makes phenomenal properties pervasive at fundamental physical levels; **mysterianism** (McGinn) holds that the answer exists but is cognitively closed to us.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Phenomenal consciousness — founding `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "What Is It Like to Be a Bat?", 1974 `[Introductory]` `[Evergreen]` | **Thomas Nagel** | The single most influential short paper on consciousness: Nagel argues that an organism is conscious if and only if there is *something it is like* to be that organism, and that this subjective character of experience is what physicalist reductions fail to capture. The bat thought experiment makes vivid the gap between objective neural-functional descriptions and subjective phenomenal character. The paper does not argue that physicalism is false — Nagel is explicit that it might be true but that we lack the conceptual resources to see how. The framework is the immediate background to Jackson's knowledge argument and Chalmers's hard problem. Limitation: short and suggestive rather than argumentative-systematic; the case for the irreducibility of phenomenal character is sharpened in Jackson and Chalmers below. The paper is in Nagel's *Mortal Questions* (Cambridge, 1979) and in most anthologies. | Required reading. See also SEP: "Consciousness." |
| `[Primary]` Hard problem — magnum opus `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Conscious Mind: In Search of a Fundamental Theory*, 1996 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **David J. Chalmers** | The major systematic statement of the hard problem and the principal philosophical case against physicalism about consciousness. Three arguments anchor the book: the *conceivability argument* (zombies — beings physically identical to us but lacking phenomenal experience — are conceivable, and conceivability is a guide to possibility, so physicalism is false); the *knowledge argument* (Mary the colour scientist, after Jackson); and the *explanatory argument* (no functional account explains why functions are accompanied by experience). Chalmers's positive proposal is *naturalistic dualism*: phenomenal properties are fundamental but lawfully linked to physical properties through psychophysical laws. The book has structured almost all subsequent work on consciousness in analytic philosophy. Limitation: the conceivability-to-possibility inference (the *modal* step) is the most contested part of the argument and has generated a substantial Type-B physicalist response (Loar, Hill, Block, Papineau) on which phenomenal and physical concepts pick out the same properties despite the conceptual gap. | Required reading on consciousness. Read with Frankish's *Illusionism* (next entry) for the principal contemporary anti-realist alternative. See also SEP: "The Hard Problem of Consciousness." |
| `[Primary]` Hard problem — functionalist dissolution `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Consciousness Explained*, 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Daniel C. Dennett** | The major Type-A physicalist treatment: Dennett argues that the apparent residue of phenomenal experience left over after the cognitive-functional facts are explained is itself an illusion — there is no further fact about "what it is like" beyond the functional-representational facts. Dennett develops his *multiple drafts* and (later) *fame in the brain* models of consciousness, sustained criticisms of the "Cartesian theater" picture, and a detailed treatment of cases (filling-in, change blindness, attentional phenomena) that he argues do not require phenomenal-property posits. The 2017 follow-up *From Bacteria to Bach and Back* integrates the position into an evolutionary account. Limitation: critics (Searle, Chalmers, Block) argue that Dennett's framework either tacitly presupposes the phenomenal facts it claims to eliminate or simply changes the subject. The book is also polemically titled — the title was a serial joke in the philosophical community on the grounds that consciousness is precisely what the book does not explain. Frankish's *Illusionism as a Theory of Consciousness* (Imprint Academic, 2017) is the sharper recent statement of the deflationary position. | Read alongside Chalmers; the two together exhibit the principal options. See also SEP: "Eliminative Materialism" and "Consciousness." |
| `[Primary]` Knowledge argument `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Epiphenomenal Qualia", 1982, and "What Mary Didn't Know", 1986 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Frank Jackson** | The knowledge argument: Mary is a brilliant colour scientist who has lived her entire life in a black-and-white room with black-and-white monitors; she knows every physical fact about colour vision. When she leaves the room and sees red for the first time, she learns something new — what it is like to see red. Therefore, there are facts about phenomenal experience that are not physical facts; physicalism is false. The argument has produced an enormous literature; Jackson himself later (1998, 2003) substantially withdrew, accepting that some version of representationalist physicalism can answer it. Limitation: the most influential physicalist replies (Loar's phenomenal-concept strategy, the ability hypothesis of Lewis and Nemirow, the acquaintance response of Conee) accept Mary learns something while denying she learns a new *fact*; whether these responses succeed remains contested. The papers are in *There's Something About Mary* (Ludlow, Nagasawa, Stoljar, eds., MIT, 2004), with replies and Jackson's retraction. | Required reading. See also SEP: "Qualia: The Knowledge Argument." |
| `[Primary]` Panpsychism — contemporary defence `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Consciousness and Fundamental Reality*, 2017 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Philip Goff** | The most rigorous contemporary defence of constitutive panpsychism: phenomenal properties exist at the fundamental physical level (microexperiences associated with fundamental physical entities), and macroexperience (yours, mine) is constituted by appropriate combinations of microexperience. The book systematically develops the case for panpsychism as the only position that combines acceptance of the hard problem (against physicalism) with acceptance of mind's natural place (against substance dualism). The principal challenge — the *combination problem* (how do microexperiences combine into the unified experiences we have?) — receives extended treatment. Limitation: the combination problem is genuinely hard, and not all panpsychists agree on the form a solution must take. Goff's accessible *Galileo's Error* (Pantheon, 2019) is the popular companion. | Graduate students in philosophy of mind. Pair with Strawson's "Realistic Monism: Why Physicalism Entails Panpsychism" (2006) for the founding contemporary case. See also SEP: "Panpsychism." |

### 7.4 Intentionality & Mental Content

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Language of thought hypothesis `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Psychosemantics: The Problem of Meaning in the Philosophy of Mind*, 1987 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jerry A. Fodor** | The systematic statement of Fodor's representational theory of mind: mental processes operate on internal representations belonging to a *language of thought* (LOT, "Mentalese") whose syntactic structure mirrors the logical structure of the propositions the agent thinks. Fodor develops the case for LOT from the productivity, systematicity, and compositionality of thought, defends a causal-historical semantics for the primitive symbols of Mentalese, and addresses the disjunction problem. The book is the major statement of strong representationalism and an essential reference for any subsequent treatment of mental content. Limitation: the asymmetric-dependence theory of content has been criticised as too clever by half; alternative externalist semantics (Dretske's informational semantics, Millikan's teleosemantics) avoid the disjunction problem in different ways. Fodor's earlier *The Language of Thought* (Crowell, 1975) is the founding longer statement. | Required reading on mental content. See also SEP: "The Language of Thought Hypothesis." |
| `[Primary]` Teleological semantics `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Language, Thought, and Other Biological Categories: New Foundations for Realism*, 1984 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Ruth Garrett Millikan** | The founding work of teleosemantics: mental content is fixed by the *proper functions* of the cognitive systems producing the representations, where proper functions are defined by evolutionary history. The framework treats mental representations as the cognitive analogues of biological adaptations — beaver tail-slaps, vervet alarm calls, hormone signals — and gives a uniform naturalistic account of representation across the biological domain. Millikan's framework is the principal alternative to Fodor's causal semantics and Dretske's informational semantics. Limitation: the appeal to evolutionary history raises sceptical questions (what is the proper function of a one-time mutation? of cognitive systems whose evolutionary history is contested or unknown?); these have driven the substantial subsequent teleosemantic literature (Neander, Papineau, Shea). | Graduate students in philosophy of mind, biology, or cognitive science. Read alongside Dretske's *Knowledge and the Flow of Information* (MIT, 1981) for the informational alternative. See also SEP: "Teleological Theories of Mental Content." |
| `[Primary]` Externalism about content `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Individualism and the Mental", 1979 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Tyler Burge** | The major statement of social externalism about mental content: the content of an individual's thoughts depends partly on the linguistic and conceptual practices of the surrounding community, not solely on the individual's intrinsic states. Burge's *arthritis* thought experiment — an individual with incomplete grasp of the concept *arthritis* who believes he has arthritis in his thigh — shows that contents depend on community-wide conceptual norms. The argument is the principal social-externalist counterpart to Putnam's natural-kind externalism (§5.4). Limitation: the externalism Burge defends has consequences for self-knowledge (do we know our own thought contents authoritatively if those contents depend on social facts external to us?) that remain a productive area of research. The paper is in *Foundations of Mind* (Oxford, 2007) and in many anthologies. | Read alongside Putnam's "Meaning of 'Meaning'" (§5.4). See also SEP: "Externalism About the Mind." |
| `[Primary]` Intentionality — Searle's framework `[Analytic]` | *Intentionality: An Essay in the Philosophy of Mind*, 1983 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **John R. Searle** | Searle's systematic account of intentionality as a biological phenomenon: mental states have *content* and *direction of fit* with the world (mind-to-world for belief, world-to-mind for desire and intention); intentionality is a biological feature of brains, not reducible to functional organisation or representational structure. The book is the major statement of Searle's anti-functionalism (continued from his 1980 Chinese Room argument) and his "biological naturalism" about mind. Limitation: Searle's "intrinsic" vs. "derived" intentionality distinction is contested by Dennett and others; his case against the computational theory of mind has been criticised as begging the question against functionalism. | Graduate students in philosophy of mind. Read after Fodor for the major alternative framework. See also SEP: "Consciousness and Intentionality." |

### 7.5 Action Theory & Free Will

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Action theory — founding monograph `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Intention*, 1957 (2nd ed. 1963) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. E. M. Anscombe** | The founding work of modern analytic action theory: Anscombe analyses *intentional action* through the question "Why?" — an action is intentional under a description if the agent has a non-causal answer to the why-question for that description. The book develops the doctrine of practical knowledge (the agent's knowledge of what she is doing is not based on observation), the distinction between action under different descriptions, and the conception of practical reasoning as inferential structure. *Intention* is the source of much subsequent action theory (Davidson, Velleman, Bratman, Setiya) and of contemporary virtue ethics's account of practical reason. Limitation: notoriously compressed; Anscombe presents a position without always fully arguing for it, and the dialectical engagements with rival views are scattered. | Required reading in action theory and Anscombean ethics. Pair with Anscombe's "Modern Moral Philosophy" (1958) for the ethical side. See also SEP: "Action." |
| `[Primary]` Action — causal theory `[Analytic]` `[Anthology]` `[Evergreen]` | *Essays on Actions and Events*, 1980 (2nd ed. 2001) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Donald Davidson** | The collection containing Davidson's major papers on action and event ontology: "Actions, Reasons, and Causes" (1963 — the founding paper of the causal theory of action, on which reasons are causes of the actions they rationalise), "Mental Events" (cf. §7.2), "The Logical Form of Action Sentences" (the event-quantification analysis of adverbial modification), and "Agency." The causal theory of action is now the standard analytic framework and Davidson's logical form analysis founded event semantics. Limitation: the *deviant causal chain* problem (an agent's reasons cause an action through the wrong route, e.g. via fortuitous nervousness rather than the intended deliberative path) remains unsolved by purely causal theories; Davidson never produced a satisfactory response. | Required reading. The 2nd edition adds two later papers. See also SEP: "Donald Davidson." |
| `[Primary]` Free will — reactive attitudes `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Freedom and Resentment", 1962 (in *Studies in the Philosophy of Thought and Action*, Oxford, 1968) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **P. F. Strawson** | The most influential paper in the modern free-will debate. Strawson reframes the question: instead of asking whether determinism threatens responsibility, ask what responsibility *consists in*. He answers: the *reactive attitudes* — resentment, gratitude, moral indignation, love, hurt feelings — that we hold towards each other as participants in interpersonal relationships. These attitudes are constitutive of the moral community, not derived from any antecedent metaphysical fact about freedom; they would not be (because they could not be) abandoned even if determinism were established. The paper has been the principal source of contemporary compatibilism, the founding text of the *attitude*-theoretic approach to responsibility (Wallace, McKenna, Shoemaker), and a major influence on Frankfurt's hierarchical theory of free will. Limitation: short and programmatic; subsequent work has had to clarify what kind of justification (if any) the reactive attitudes admit. | Required reading. The paper is in Watson's *Free Will* anthology (Oxford, 2nd ed. 2003), the standard collection. See also SEP: "Compatibilism." |
| `[Research]` Free will — incompatibilism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *An Essay on Free Will*, 1983 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Peter van Inwagen** | The major modern statement of incompatibilism, containing the *Consequence Argument*: if determinism is true, then our acts are the consequences of laws of nature and events in the remote past; we are not responsible for the laws or the past; we cannot now make them otherwise; therefore we cannot now make our acts otherwise — and so free will is incompatible with determinism. Van Inwagen is a libertarian about free will (we have it; determinism is false), though he is candid that libertarian agency is "mysterious." The Consequence Argument has been the focus of compatibilist responses for forty years (Lewis's "Are We Free to Break the Laws?", various local-power responses). Limitation: van Inwagen's libertarian positive view depends on a mysterious doctrine of agent-causation that he himself acknowledges is incompletely defensible. | Required reading in the free-will literature. See also SEP: "Arguments for Incompatibilism" and "Free Will." |

### 7.6 Cognitive Science — Philosophical Foundations

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Three-level analysis of cognition `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Vision: A Computational Investigation into the Human Representation and Processing of Visual Information*, 1982 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **David Marr** | The single most philosophically influential book of late-20th-century cognitive science: Marr's framework distinguishes three levels of analysis at which any information-processing system must be understood — the *computational* level (what problem is being solved? what are the inputs and outputs?), the *algorithmic* level (what representations and procedures are used?), and the *implementational* level (how is the algorithm physically realised?). Applied by Marr to vision (with substantial empirical results), the framework has structured almost all subsequent philosophical reflection on cognitive science. Limitation: Marr's tripartite distinction is now sometimes accused of being too clean — actual cognitive systems exhibit constraints flowing across the levels; the dynamical-systems and embodied-cognition traditions (cf. §7.7) have substantially complicated Marr's picture. | Read by anyone working at the philosophy–cognitive-science boundary. The first chapter ("The Philosophy and the Approach") is the philosophically essential section. See also SEP: "David Marr." |
| `[Primary]` Modularity of mind `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Modularity of Mind*, 1983 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jerry A. Fodor** | The founding statement of the modularity thesis: certain cognitive systems (especially input systems — vision, hearing, language parsing) are *modular* — domain-specific, mandatory, fast, informationally encapsulated, hardwired, and developmentally fixed; central cognition (belief revision, theory choice, "isotropic" processes generally) is *non-modular* and resists computational tractability. The thesis has shaped cognitive science and the philosophy of mind, and Fodor's pessimism about computational accounts of central cognition has been important for the limits-of-AI literature. Limitation: the *massive modularity* movement in evolutionary psychology (Cosmides, Tooby, Sperber) has argued that central cognition is also modular, against Fodor; the dispute is ongoing. | Read by anyone working in cognitive science or philosophy of mind. Short and accessible. See also SEP: "Modularity of Mind." |
| `[Research]` Predictive processing `[Bridge]` `[Research]` | *Surfing Uncertainty: Prediction, Action, and the Embodied Mind*, 2016 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Andy Clark** | The major philosophical synthesis of the predictive processing framework in cognitive science: the brain is fundamentally a prediction-generating engine, minimising prediction error through hierarchical generative models; perception, action, attention, and even emotion fall under a single unified framework. Clark develops both the empirical and philosophical case, including connections to embodied cognition and to Bayesian formal epistemology (§5.7). The book is the bridge between mainstream computational cognitive science and the embodied/enactive tradition of §7.7. Limitation: the predictive processing framework is sufficiently flexible that it has been criticised as unfalsifiable in its broadest formulations; specific predictive-processing models (Friston's free energy principle, in particular) face substantive empirical and conceptual challenges. | Graduate students in philosophy of cognitive science. Read after Marr; pair with Hohwy's *The Predictive Mind* (Oxford, 2013) and with Clark's earlier *Being There* (MIT, 1997) on extended cognition. |

### 7.7 Embodied & Enactive Cognition — The Analytic-Phenomenological Bridge

This subsection contains the most successful contemporary bridge texts in the atlas. The embodied, embedded, and enactive cognition movements explicitly engage both analytic philosophy of mind/cognitive science and phenomenology (Husserl, Heidegger, Merleau-Ponty — see Table 9), and the resulting literature has reshaped both traditions. The texts below should be read together as a coherent intellectual project.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Founding text of enactivism `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Embodied Mind: Cognitive Science and Human Experience*, 1991 (rev. ed. 2017) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Francisco J. Varela, Evan Thompson & Eleanor Rosch** | The founding work of enactivism in cognitive science: cognition is best understood not as the manipulation of internal representations of a pre-given external world, but as the *enactment* of a world through the structurally coupled activity of an embodied agent. The book systematically connects the cognitive science of the early 1990s (especially the Chilean autopoiesis tradition of Maturana and Varela) with Merleau-Pontian phenomenology and with Buddhist analyses of mind and self. The revised edition (MIT, 2017) by Thompson updates the cognitive science but preserves the original argument. Limitation: deliberately programmatic — the enactivist framework Varela and his collaborators sketch has been developed (and disputed) in detail by subsequent work (Thompson, Di Paolo, Hutto, Myin). | Required reading for the analytic-phenomenological bridge. See also SEP: "Embodied Cognition." |
| `[Primary]` Phenomenological critique of AI and cognitivism `[Bridge]` `[Continental]` `[Evergreen]` | *What Computers Still Can't Do: A Critique of Artificial Reason*, 1992 (orig. *What Computers Can't Do*, 1972; rev. 1979) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hubert L. Dreyfus** | The Heideggerian-Merleau-Pontian critique of classical (GOFAI) artificial intelligence and of the representational-computational theory of mind generally. Dreyfus's argument: human intelligence is grounded in skilled, embodied, contextual coping with the world — Heideggerian *Zuhandenheit* — that resists formalisation as explicit rule-following on context-free symbols. The classical cognitivist programme's failures (the frame problem, the relevance problem, the brittleness of expert systems) are predicted symptoms of trying to capture skilled coping in a representational framework. Dreyfus's critique was dismissed by mainstream AI for decades and has been substantially vindicated by the subsequent shift to embodied robotics and connectionist/deep-learning approaches. Limitation: the polemical edge can obscure the positive philosophical claims; *Being-in-the-World* (MIT, 1991), Dreyfus's commentary on Heidegger's *Sein und Zeit*, is the systematic philosophical companion. | Required reading for anyone working at the AI/philosophy or cognitive science/phenomenology boundary. See also SEP: "Hubert Dreyfus." |
| `[Research]` Perceptual experience as activity `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Action in Perception*, 2004 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alva Noë** | The major analytic-philosophical statement of *sensorimotor enactivism*: perceptual experience consists not in the production of internal representations but in the exercise of sensorimotor knowledge — practical mastery of how sensory stimulation varies with bodily movement. Visual experience of (e.g.) a tomato's hidden far side is constituted by the perceiver's implicit grasp of how the appearances would change with motion. The book draws systematically on Merleau-Pontian phenomenology while engaging the analytic philosophy of perception (Burge, Tye, Block). Limitation: the sensorimotor account has been criticised as either too strong (denying that experience could be present without motor capacities) or too weak (when sufficiently qualified, no longer distinctive). Noë's later *Out of Our Heads* (Hill and Wang, 2009) extends the framework but more popularly. | Graduate students in philosophy of perception or cognitive science. Read after the Varela–Thompson–Rosch and Dreyfus. |
| `[Research]` Phenomenology and cognitive science `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Mind in Life: Biology, Phenomenology, and the Sciences of Mind*, 2007 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Evan Thompson** | The most ambitious recent synthesis of enactivist cognitive science with phenomenology: Thompson works through autopoietic biology (Maturana–Varela), the cognitive science of perception, action, and emotion, and the Husserlian-Merleau-Pontian phenomenology of consciousness, building a sustained argument that life and mind are continuous — the same self-organising principles that constitute living organisms constitute minds. The book is the best single contemporary resource for understanding what serious engagement between the analytic and phenomenological traditions in philosophy of mind looks like in practice. Limitation: long (~500 pp.) and demanding; presupposes familiarity with both traditions to some degree, though Thompson writes accessibly. | Required reading for the analytic-phenomenological bridge tradition. The contemporary masterwork in this corner of the field. See also Thompson's *Waking, Dreaming, Being* (Columbia, 2014) for the continuation into consciousness studies. |

### 7.8 Philosophy of Psychology — Explanation, Reduction & Folk Psychology

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Intentional stance `[Analytic]` `[Anthology]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Intentional Stance*, 1987 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Daniel C. Dennett** | Dennett's systematic statement of his instrumentalist-realist account of intentional explanation: we can predict the behaviour of any sufficiently complex system by taking the *intentional stance* — treating it as a rational agent with beliefs and desires — without thereby committing ourselves to the literal presence of internal representations in the system. Beliefs are not internal sentences but patterns visible from the intentional stance, real in the same way Dennett-style "real patterns" generally are. The book combines essays on intentional system theory, the relation between intentional and design stances, evolutionary explanation, and the place of folk psychology in scientific cognition. Limitation: Dennett's view is awkwardly placed between realism and instrumentalism about mental states, and critics have argued that the position is unstable (either collapses to eliminativism or commits to more realism than Dennett wants). | Read alongside Fodor's *Psychosemantics* (§7.4) for the realist alternative. See also SEP: "Daniel Dennett." |
| `[Primary]` Eliminative materialism `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Eliminative Materialism and the Propositional Attitudes", 1981 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Paul M. Churchland** | The founding paper of eliminative materialism about folk psychology: belief, desire, and the other propositional attitudes are theoretical posits of a folk-psychological theory ("folk psychology" as a proto-scientific theory of mind), and like other historically discarded theoretical posits (caloric, phlogiston, the celestial spheres), they will likely be eliminated from our scientific ontology as a mature neuroscience develops. Churchland's case rests on the failures of folk psychology to explain mental phenomena (sleep, dreams, mental illness, perceptual illusions) and on the principled possibility of a successor cognitive science whose categories cut nature differently. Limitation: eliminativism faces a self-refutation objection (claiming that no one believes anything is itself a belief) and the explanatory-success objection (folk psychology is remarkably effective at prediction and explanation). The dialectical situation post-1990 has been broadly unfavourable to eliminativism but the position survives. | Required reading. Read alongside Stich's *From Folk Psychology to Cognitive Science* (MIT, 1983) for the major contemporary book-length defence. See also SEP: "Eliminative Materialism." |
| `[Systematic]` Philosophy of psychology — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Philosophy of Psychology: A Contemporary Introduction*, 2005 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **José Luis Bermúdez** | The standard textbook on philosophy of psychology specifically (distinct from philosophy of mind): chapters on the autonomy of psychology, the architecture of cognition, the role of representations, the relation between psychology and neuroscience, animal cognition, infant cognition, and the developmental and evolutionary explanation of mental capacities. Bermúdez integrates philosophical analysis with substantial discussion of the empirical psychology and cognitive science. Limitation: like all textbooks, even-handed; for engagement with specific controversies consult the monographs in §7.6 and the relevant SEP entries. | The natural follow-up to Kim's *Philosophy of Mind* (§7.1) for readers interested in the philosophy of empirical psychology. |

### Connections to other tables

This table connects upward to Table 6 (Kim's exclusion argument in §7.2 is a paradigm case of analytic metaphysics; mental content externalism in §7.4 connects to §6.6 ontological commitment and to Kripke and Putnam in §5.4) and to Table 5 (the philosophy of language background to mental content). The §7.3 hard-problem literature engages directly with the modal arguments developed in §5.4 and §6.7 — the zombie conceivability argument is a modal-metaphysical argument applied to consciousness, and the responses turn on the same conceivability-to-possibility issues as in §5.4. Anscombe's *Intention* (§7.5) is the founding text of contemporary virtue ethics in §8.4. The §7.7 bridge texts connect this table forward to Table 9: Dreyfus's commentary on Heidegger is part of §9.2, Thompson and Varela are in dialogue with Husserlian phenomenology in §9.1, and the entire embodied/enactive tradition presupposes the phenomenological framework developed in §9.4 (Merleau-Ponty). Aristotle's *De Anima* (§2.4) is the deep historical background to contemporary hylomorphic and Aristotelian-functionalist treatments of mind (Wedin, Nussbaum, Shields). The free-will literature (§7.5) connects to §8.6 (moral responsibility) and to the determinism/indeterminism debate in the Physics Atlas (philosophical interpretations of quantum mechanics).

# Table 8 · Ethics, Metaethics & Political Philosophy

*Scope:* Normative ethical theory (consequentialism, deontology, virtue ethics, contractualism), metaethics (the metaphysics, epistemology, and semantics of moral discourse), and political philosophy (justice, liberty, equality, democracy, legitimacy, and the critical and feminist traditions). Applied ethics is treated only where methodologically important. Aristotle's *Nicomachean Ethics* (§2.4) and Kant's *Groundwork* / *CPrR* / *Metaphysics of Morals* (§4.3) are the primary texts for virtue ethics and Kantian ethics respectively; this table presupposes them.

### 8.1 Normative Ethics — Introductory Surveys

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Normative ethics — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Normative Ethics*, 1998 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Shelly Kagan** | The best graduate-level survey of normative ethical theory in English: chapters on the structure of normative theories (the role of consequences, constraints, options, and special obligations) followed by systematic treatments of consequentialism and deontology. Kagan is uncommonly clear on the dialectical relations between positions and on what moves the theories are forced to make to handle each other's objections. Limitation: organised around the consequentialism/deontology axis; virtue ethics and contractualism receive less central attention (consult §§8.4–8.5). | The natural first text in a graduate normative ethics sequence. Pair with Williams below for a sharper critical edge. |
| `[Primary]` Ethics — critical introduction `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Morality: An Introduction to Ethics*, 1972 (rev. ed. 1993) `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Bernard Williams** | A short (~100 pp.) introduction that is also a sustained argument: Williams takes the reader through the central problems of ethics (subjectivism, relativism, utilitarianism, God and morality, justice) while developing his own dissident view of what moral philosophy can and cannot accomplish. The brevity belies the depth — many of Williams's later positions (the critique of system in ethics, the priority of practical reasons over moral reasons) are visible in seed form. Limitation: an introduction by an iconoclast — readers should know that Williams's framing is itself a substantive position, not the neutral overview Kagan attempts. | Read alongside or after Kagan. Williams's later *Ethics and the Limits of Philosophy* (Harvard, 1985) is the mature systematic statement; see §8.6. |

### 8.2 Consequentialism — Bentham, Mill, Sidgwick & Contemporary Utilitarianism

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Classical utilitarianism — popular statement `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Utilitarianism*, 1861 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Stuart Mill**; ed. **Roger Crisp** (Oxford, 1998) | Mill's classic short presentation of utilitarianism for an educated general readership: the greatest-happiness principle, the qualitative distinction between higher and lower pleasures (Mill's revision of Benthamite hedonism), the "proof" of utility, the relation between justice and utility, and the treatment of secondary moral rules. The text contains the rhetorical core of the utilitarian tradition and remains the natural first reading. Limitation: Mill's "proof" of utility (Chapter IV) has been notoriously controversial — the argument from each person desiring her own happiness to happiness being the general good has been criticised as either fallacious or charitably reconstructable along non-standard lines; the matter is unresolved. The qualitative pleasure distinction is also widely held to be in tension with the underlying hedonist framework. | Required reading in ethics. The Crisp Oxford edition is the standard student version. See also SEP: "John Stuart Mill" and "Consequentialism." |
| `[Primary]` Utilitarianism — systematic philosophical statement `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Methods of Ethics*, 7th ed., 1907 (1st ed. 1874) `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Henry Sidgwick** | The most philosophically rigorous classical statement of utilitarianism and the work that Parfit and Rawls both regarded as the high-water mark of pre-20th-century moral philosophy. Sidgwick distinguishes three methods of ethics — egoism, intuitionism (deontology), and utilitarianism — and works through their relations methodically. His treatment of the *dualism of practical reason* (egoism and utilitarianism each look defensible from their own standpoint, but together they cannot both be vindicated by reason alone) has remained productive. Limitation: enormous (~500 pp.) and demanding; the Victorian intellectual setting can obscure the contemporary force of the arguments. The 7th edition (1907, published posthumously) is the canonical text. | Graduate students in ethics. Read selectively — Books I and III–IV are the philosophical core. Parfit explicitly modelled *On What Matters* on the *Methods*. |
| `[Anthology]` Utilitarianism — debate landmark `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Utilitarianism: For and Against*, 1973 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. J. C. Smart & Bernard Williams** | A two-essay volume in which Smart defends and Williams attacks act-utilitarianism. Smart's "An Outline of a System of Utilitarian Ethics" is the clearest short statement of contemporary act-utilitarianism. Williams's "A Critique of Utilitarianism" contains the landmark cases — the famous Jim-and-the-Indians and George-the-chemist — that crystallised the *integrity objection* (utilitarian agents lose their integrity by becoming mere conduits for the maximisation of utility) and the case for agent-relative restrictions. The book is the most efficient single text for understanding the late-20th-century critique of utilitarianism. Limitation: a relatively short volume; the literature on each side has grown enormously since 1973, but the central considerations are still those Williams identifies. | Required reading in normative ethics. The single book to read on whether utilitarianism is plausible as a complete moral theory. |
| `[Research]` Rule consequentialism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Ideal Code, Real World: A Rule-Consequentialist Theory of Morality*, 2000 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Brad Hooker** | The major contemporary statement of rule consequentialism: actions are morally permissible iff they are permitted by the set of rules whose general internalisation by everyone would maximise expected wellbeing. Hooker addresses the standard objections to rule-consequentialism (collapse into act-consequentialism; the partial-compliance problem; the rule-fetishism objection) more systematically than any previous treatment. Limitation: the framework's reliance on a specific notion of "internalisation" and on counterfactual reasoning about general compliance is contested, and the relation between rule-consequentialism and standard intuitive deontology remains delicate. | Graduate students in normative ethics. See also Mulgan's *The Demands of Consequentialism* (Oxford, 2001) for the major treatment of the demandingness objection. |

### 8.3 Deontology — Contemporary Kantian and Pluralist Ethics

Kant's primary practical texts (*Groundwork*, *CPrR*, *Metaphysics of Morals*) and the major contemporary Kantian secondary literature (Korsgaard's *Creating the Kingdom of Ends*, Wood's *Kant's Ethical Thought*, O'Neill's *Constructions of Reason*) are in §4.3 and should be read as the foundation of contemporary deontology. The entries below cover the major non-Kantian deontology (Ross) and a major recent Kantian-systematic statement not in §4.3 (Herman).

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
