| `[Primary]` Pluralist deontology — *prima facie* duties `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Right and the Good*, 1930 (rev. ed. ed. **Philip Stratton-Lake**, Oxford, 2002) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **W. D. Ross** | The major non-Kantian deontological text of the 20th century: Ross develops a pluralist intuitionism on which there are several irreducible *prima facie* (more accurately *pro tanto*) duties — fidelity, reparation, gratitude, justice, beneficence, self-improvement, non-maleficence — that the moral agent must weigh in particular situations. Ross's framework avoids both the over-systematicity of Kantian deontology and the consequentialist reduction of all duties to one. The work is the founding text of contemporary moral particularism (Dancy) and pluralist intuitionism (Audi, Stratton-Lake). Limitation: Ross's epistemology of moral perception — that the *prima facie* duties are self-evident to the mature moral agent — is the most contested part of the framework. The Stratton-Lake edition (Oxford, 2002) is the scholarly edition with the major recent introduction. | Required reading in normative ethics. See also SEP: "W. D. Ross." |
| `[Research]` Contemporary Kantian moral judgment `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *The Practice of Moral Judgment*, 1993 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Barbara Herman** | A major collection of Herman's essays developing a contemporary Kantianism centred on the practical judgment of the moral agent rather than on the mechanical application of the categorical imperative. The essays "On the Value of Acting from the Motive of Duty," "The Practice of Moral Judgment," and "Could It Be Worth Thinking About Kant on Sex and Marriage?" are individually influential. Herman shows how Kantian ethics can address objections (Schiller's "duty-grimly" objection; the action-from-inclination problem) by attending to the moral psychology Kant actually develops. Limitation: a collection rather than a treatise; the cumulative position must be assembled across essays. Herman's later *Moral Literacy* (Harvard, 2007) is the more systematic restatement. | Graduate students in Kantian ethics. Read after Korsgaard's *Creating the Kingdom of Ends* and Wood's *Kant's Ethical Thought* (§4.3). |

### 8.4 Virtue Ethics — Contemporary Virtue Theory

Aristotle's *Nicomachean Ethics* (§2.4) is the founding text. The contemporary revival begins with Anscombe's 1958 paper.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Virtue ethics — founding modern paper `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Modern Moral Philosophy", 1958 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. E. M. Anscombe** | The single most influential short paper in 20th-century ethics: Anscombe argues that the modern concepts of moral *obligation* and *moral law* survive from a (now-vanished) divine-command framework that gave them sense, and that without it they are conceptual ruins; consequentialism is also dismissed (Anscombe coins the term *consequentialism* here as a term of art for the position she rejects). The constructive proposal: return to virtue-theoretic moral psychology — the analysis of human flourishing, of the virtues, of practical wisdom — until we have a better account, the modern moral concepts should be suspended. The paper founded contemporary virtue ethics and was the proximate cause of Foot's, MacIntyre's, and Hursthouse's projects. Limitation: short and polemical; the negative case against modern moral philosophy is sharper than the positive case for the Aristotelian alternative. Available in many anthologies; standard text is in *The Collected Philosophical Papers of G. E. M. Anscombe* Vol. 3. | Required reading in ethics. See also SEP: "Virtue Ethics." |
| `[Primary]` Virtue ethics — historical and systematic critique `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *After Virtue: A Study in Moral Theory*, 3rd ed., 2007 (1st ed. 1981) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Alasdair MacIntyre** | MacIntyre's diagnosis and proposed remedy for the condition of contemporary moral discourse: modern moral debate is interminable because the participants invoke fragments of incompatible traditions (Kantian, utilitarian, Christian, contractualist) that have lost their original systematic settings. MacIntyre proposes a return to Aristotelian-Thomistic virtue ethics rooted in social practices, narrative unity of life, and tradition. The book is one of the most influential works of moral philosophy of the late 20th century, with substantial influence on theology, political philosophy (communitarianism), and the social sciences. Limitation: the historical narrative is selectively constructed (especially on the Enlightenment); the constructive Aristotelian position depends on substantive theological and metaphysical commitments that MacIntyre develops more fully in *Whose Justice? Which Rationality?* (1988) and *Three Rival Versions of Moral Enquiry* (1990 — see §1.5). | Required reading in virtue ethics and contemporary political philosophy. Pair with Hursthouse below for the systematic Aristotelian alternative. |
| `[Research]` Virtue ethics — systematic statement `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *On Virtue Ethics*, 1999 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Rosalind Hursthouse** | The major systematic defence of neo-Aristotelian virtue ethics as a complete normative theory able to give action guidance in particular cases: Hursthouse defends the formulation "an action is right iff it is what a virtuous agent would characteristically do in the circumstances," addresses the standard objections (the action-guidance objection, the situationist objection, the relativism objection), and develops a naturalistic account of the virtues based on Foot's framework. The chapters on abortion and on the unity of the virtues are individually influential. Limitation: the naturalistic grounding of the virtues in human nature has been criticised (the *natural good* approach, developed at length in Foot's *Natural Goodness* — below — and in McDowell's *Mind, Value, and Reality*, is the major systematic alternative). | Required reading in contemporary virtue ethics. Pair with Foot below. |
| `[Research]` Virtue ethics — natural goodness `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Natural Goodness*, 2001 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Philippa Foot** | A short, dense book that grounds virtue ethics in a biological-teleological conception of natural goodness: just as we evaluate plants and animals by reference to what is good for things of their kind, we evaluate human beings by reference to what is good for humans as the rational social animals they are. The framework draws on Aristotelian natural teleology and on Anscombe's earlier work. The book is Foot's mature statement after her earlier *Virtues and Vices* (Blackwell, 1978) and represents her revision of her earlier neo-Humean position. Limitation: short — only 130 pp. — and presupposes substantial prior engagement with Foot's earlier essays and with Anscombe; the biological-naturalistic grounding has been criticised as either too thin or too committed. | Graduate students in virtue ethics. Read after Hursthouse and Foot's earlier *Virtues and Vices*. |

### 8.5 Contractualism — Hobbes, Rousseau, Rawls, Scanlon

The Rawls problem. *A Theory of Justice* (1971; rev. ed. 1999) is the single most influential work in 20th-century political philosophy. *Political Liberalism* (1993) is substantially a revision in response to the communitarian critique of TJ — Rawls came to see that TJ's *Kantian* metaphysical foundations would not be acceptable as the political basis of a pluralistic society and that justice as fairness must be reconceived as a *political*, not a *comprehensive*, moral doctrine. The two books together constitute Rawls's mature position; treating TJ as self-sufficient (a common error) misses the substantial revision *PL* represents.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Contractarian foundations `[Evergreen]` | *Leviathan*, 1651 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Thomas Hobbes**; ed. **Edwin Curley** (Hackett, 1994) or **Richard Tuck** (Cambridge, rev. ed. 1996) | The founding work of modern contractarianism: the derivation of political authority from the rational consent of self-interested agents in the state of nature, the absolute sovereignty thesis, the natural-law foundations of obligation, and the famous characterisation of life in the state of nature as "solitary, poor, nasty, brutish, and short." *Leviathan* is also a major work of materialist philosophy of mind and of philosophy of language. The book has been read in incompatible directions — as cynical-realist political theory, as proto-liberal contractarian, as natural-law theory in modern dress — and the literature reflects all these readings. Limitation: Hobbes's argument depends on substantive moral psychology (the universal desire for self-preservation, the inevitability of war in the state of nature) and on a controversial conception of practical rationality that the contemporary literature contests. **Recommended translation/edition:** Curley (Hackett, 1994) for student use; Tuck (Cambridge, rev. ed. 1996) for the Cambridge Texts scholarly edition. | Required reading in political philosophy. See also SEP: "Hobbes's Moral and Political Philosophy." |
| `[Primary]` Contractarian foundations — democratic `[Evergreen]` | *On the Social Contract*, 1762 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jean-Jacques Rousseau**; trans. **Donald A. Cress** (Hackett, 2nd ed. 2019) or **Victor Gourevitch** (Cambridge, 1997) | Rousseau's argument for the legitimacy of political authority through the general will: a free people legitimately governs itself by laws that all citizens would consent to under conditions of equality, and freedom in society consists in being subject only to such laws. The doctrine of the general will and the sharp distinction between general will and the mere aggregate of particular wills is the philosophical core, and the work is the principal source of subsequent democratic-republican thought (Kant, Hegel, the French Revolution, contemporary deliberative democracy). Limitation: the general-will doctrine has been read as either democratically liberatory or totalitarian; both readings have substantial textual support and Rousseau's prose admits both. **Recommended translation:** Gourevitch (Cambridge, 1997) for the most scholarly edition with Rousseau's other major political writings; Cress (Hackett) for accessible student use. | Required reading in political philosophy. Pair with the *Second Discourse* (on the origins of inequality), available in the same Gourevitch edition. See also SEP: "Jean-Jacques Rousseau." |
| `[Primary]` Justice as fairness `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *A Theory of Justice*, 1971 (revised edition 1999) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Rawls** | The most influential work of political philosophy in the second half of the 20th century. Rawls's central thesis: the principles of justice are those that free and equal rational agents would choose from behind a *veil of ignorance* — a hypothetical position in which they do not know their natural endowments, social position, or comprehensive conceptions of the good. The agents choose two principles: (i) each person has equal claim to a fully adequate scheme of basic liberties (the *liberty principle*); (ii) social and economic inequalities are arranged so they (a) attach to positions open to all under fair equality of opportunity and (b) are to the greatest benefit of the least advantaged (the *difference principle*). The book reshaped political philosophy by making systematic normative theorising central to the field again after decades of behaviouralist and analytic suspicion. Limitation: the original *Theory* was vulnerable to communitarian critiques (Sandel, MacIntyre, Walzer) that it presupposed a *comprehensive* Kantian liberal moral doctrine that pluralistic societies could not be expected to share. Rawls's response — *Political Liberalism* (next entry) — must be read as the partner volume, not as an addendum. The revised 1999 edition makes various clarifications and corrections, including to the difference principle and the priority of liberty; cite from the revised edition. | Required reading. The book to start with in political philosophy. See also SEP: "John Rawls" and "Original Position." |
| `[Primary]` Justice as fairness — political conception `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Political Liberalism*, 1993 (expanded edition 2005) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Rawls** | Rawls's reformulation of justice as fairness in response to the communitarian critique of TJ. The central thesis: in a pluralistic society characterised by *reasonable disagreement* about comprehensive moral, religious, and philosophical doctrines, the principles of justice must be defensible as a *political* conception that can be the subject of an *overlapping consensus* among reasonable comprehensive doctrines — not as a freestanding Kantian moral position. Rawls develops the doctrine of *public reason* (the form of reasoning appropriate to fundamental constitutional questions), the conception of citizens as *free and equal*, and the framework of the *reasonable* (distinct from the rational). The 2005 expanded edition adds "The Idea of Public Reason Revisited," Rawls's most accessible synthesis. Limitation: critics (Habermas, Cohen, Forst) have argued that the move to a political conception either concedes too much to the actual fact of pluralism or fails to deliver substantive normative guidance; the dialectic continues. | Required reading. Cannot be skipped if one reads TJ. Pair with Habermas's reply (in his *Justification and Application*, 1993) for the major dialogue with continental democratic theory. |
| `[Research]` Contractualism — Scanlonian `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *What We Owe to Each Other*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **T. M. Scanlon** | The major contemporary statement of contractualism as a moral (not merely political) theory: an act is wrong iff its performance under the circumstances would be disallowed by any system of rules for the general regulation of behaviour which no one could *reasonably reject* as a basis for informed, unforced general agreement. Scanlon's reasonable-rejection framework gives content to the contractualist intuition without depending on the rationality assumptions of Hobbesian contractarianism or the veil-of-ignorance machinery of Rawls. The book also contains Scanlon's important earlier work on reasons, on responsibility, on promising, and on well-being (the *aim of well-being* chapter is foundational for the contemporary debate on prudential value). Limitation: Scanlon's framework has been criticised for its difficulties with the inclusion of non-human animals (who cannot reasonably reject anything), with aggregation cases (saving one life or many?), and with the priority of the right over the good. | Required reading in contemporary normative ethics. See also Parfit's substantial engagement in *On What Matters* Vol. 2 (§8.7). |

### 8.6 Moral Epistemology & Applied Ethics Methodology

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Williams's mature ethics `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Ethics and the Limits of Philosophy*, 1985 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Bernard Williams** | Williams's major systematic work in ethics: a sustained argument that the modern philosophical attempt to ground ethics in theory — the *morality system* of obligations, blame, and voluntariness — has been radically overconfident, that ethical reflection is essentially historically situated rather than theoretically systematic, and that ancient ethics (especially in its Aristotelian and Sophoclean forms) offers a better model than modern moral philosophy. The book contains the influential treatments of *thick ethical concepts* (concepts like *brutal*, *kind*, *cowardly* whose application is both descriptive and evaluative), the distinction between *ethics* and *morality*, and the critique of utilitarian and Kantian *theories*. Limitation: dialectically dense; the book responds to a literature it assumes the reader knows. Williams's *Shame and Necessity* (California, 1993) is the historical-philological companion. | Graduate students in ethics. The book that does for ethics what Williams's *Descartes* did for epistemology — substantive philosophy through serious history. |
| `[Research]` Moral particularism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Ethics Without Principles*, 2004 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Jonathan Dancy** | The major contemporary statement of moral particularism: there are no defensible moral principles — the moral relevance of a consideration in one case may be different from its relevance in another, and moral judgment is irreducibly a matter of judging particular cases rather than applying general principles. Dancy develops the *holism of reasons* — what counts in favour in one context may count against in another — and uses it to argue against the generalist assumption common to consequentialism, deontology, and most contemporary virtue ethics. Limitation: particularism's critics (Hooker, McKeever and Ridge) have argued that the holism of reasons does not entail particularism about principles, and that some level of principled moral reasoning survives Dancy's arguments. | Graduate students in ethics or metaethics. See also SEP: "Moral Particularism." |

### 8.7 Metaethics — Realism, Anti-Realism & Expressivism

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Error theory `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Ethics: Inventing Right and Wrong*, 1977 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **J. L. Mackie** | The founding statement of moral error theory: ordinary moral discourse purports to refer to objective moral properties (Mackie accepts the cognitivist semantics of moral statements), but there are no such properties; therefore all positive moral assertions are systematically false. Mackie's two principal arguments are the *argument from relativity* (the actual variation in moral codes is best explained by the absence of objective moral facts) and the *argument from queerness* (objective moral properties, if they existed, would be metaphysically and epistemologically unlike anything else we accept). The book is also a constructive statement of Mackie's positive proposal: morality as a useful human invention, defensible on broadly Hobbesian grounds. Limitation: the argument from queerness depends on substantive metaphysical and epistemological premises that contemporary moral realists (Shafer-Landau, Enoch, Parfit) have contested. The companion debate is now substantial. | Required reading in metaethics. See also SEP: "Moral Anti-Realism" and "Moral Skepticism." |
| `[Research]` Quasi-realism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Ruling Passions: A Theory of Practical Reasoning*, 1998 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Simon Blackburn** | The systematic statement of Blackburn's quasi-realism: moral judgments express non-cognitive attitudes (a broadly Humean-expressivist starting point), but the *practice* of moral judgment earns the right to all the realist-sounding features (truth, error, knowledge, mind-independence) through the construction of moral discourse from those attitudes. Quasi-realism is the principal contemporary expressivist programme and is the alternative to error theory for those persuaded by Mackie's metaphysics but not by his nihilism. The book builds on Blackburn's earlier *Spreading the Word* (Oxford, 1984). Limitation: the *Frege-Geach problem* (how can the same content figure unembedded in assertion and embedded in conditionals if its meaning is non-cognitive?) is the major technical challenge; Blackburn's solution (the *higher-order attitudes* approach) is contested. Schroeder's *Slaves of the Passions* (Oxford, 2007) and *Being For* (Oxford, 2008) are the major recent attempts to solve the Frege-Geach problem within a Humean expressivist framework. | Required reading in metaethics. See also SEP: "Moral Expressivism." |
| `[Research]` Constructivism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Sources of Normativity*, 1996 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Christine M. Korsgaard** (with replies by G. A. Cohen, Raymond Geuss, Thomas Nagel, Bernard Williams; ed. **Onora O'Neill**) | Korsgaard's Tanner Lectures plus four substantial replies. Korsgaard defends a Kantian constructivism on which the source of moral normativity is the rational agent's reflective endorsement of her own *practical identity* — the necessity of valuing humanity as such follows from the necessity of valuing anything at all. The book is the major contemporary statement of Kantian constructivism and the principal alternative to both moral realism and expressivism. The replies (especially Williams's and Cohen's) form a sustained critical dialogue and are integral to the volume. Limitation: Korsgaard's transcendental argument from the reflective structure of agency to the value of humanity has been contested at multiple points by both critics (Cohen, FitzPatrick, Enoch) and friends (Bagnoli, Schroeder); whether the argument succeeds is genuinely open. | Required reading in metaethics. See also SEP: "Constructivism in Metaethics." |
| `[Research]` Non-naturalist realism — magnum opus `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *On What Matters*, 2 vols., 2011 (Vol. 3, 2017) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Derek Parfit** | Parfit's massive (~1,500 pp.) systematic treatment of normative ethics and metaethics. Vol. 1 reconstructs Kantian, contractualist, and consequentialist ethics and argues for a "Triple Theory" on which the best versions of all three converge — *they are climbing the same mountain on different sides*. Vol. 2 develops Parfit's non-naturalist moral realism, defending the existence of irreducibly normative truths that hold non-causally and are knowable a priori. Includes substantial replies by Susan Wolf, Allen Wood, Barbara Herman, and T. M. Scanlon (in Vol. 2). Vol. 3 (2017, posthumously completed) extends the project to objections from Williams, Wolf, and others. Limitation: enormous and uneven; readers should consult selectively — the Triple Theory chapters in Vol. 1 and the non-naturalism chapters in Vol. 2 are the core. Parfit's argument for the convergence has been contested by critics who hold that the three traditions disagree about substantive issues (aggregation, integrity, partiality) that the Triple Theory papers over. | Graduate students in ethics and metaethics. Read selectively rather than linearly. The single most ambitious work of analytic ethics since Sidgwick. |
| `[Reference]` Metaethics — comprehensive reference `[Anthology]` `[Reference]` | *The Oxford Handbook of Metaethics*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` | **David Copp** (ed.) | The current standard research-level reference on metaethics: 26 chapters covering moral semantics (cognitivism, expressivism, hybrid theories), moral epistemology (intuitionism, reflective equilibrium, evolutionary debunking), the metaphysics of moral properties (naturalism, non-naturalism, response-dependence, fictionalism), and the relations between metaethics and normative ethics. Chapters by Sturgeon (Cornell realism), Boyd (moral naturalism), Shafer-Landau (non-naturalism), Gibbard (expressivism), and Sayre-McCord (moral epistemology) are particular contributions. Limitation: 2006; substantial post-2006 work (Schroeder, Street, Enoch, the evolutionary debunking literature, the new emergence of relaxed realism) is later. The companion anthology *Foundations of Ethics* (Shafer-Landau & Cuneo, eds., Blackwell, 2007) collects the founding papers and is the standard graduate course text. | Graduate students entering metaethics; researchers consulting on specific subtopics. See also SEP: "Metaethics." |

### 8.8 Political Philosophy — Introductory Surveys

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Systematic]` Political philosophy — textbook `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Contemporary Political Philosophy: An Introduction*, 2nd ed., 2002 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Will Kymlicka** | The standard graduate-level textbook on contemporary Anglophone political philosophy: utilitarianism, liberal egalitarianism (Rawls, Dworkin), libertarianism (Nozick, Hayek), Marxism, communitarianism, citizenship theory, multiculturalism, feminism. Kymlicka writes as a partisan liberal egalitarian — the book's framing is liberal, the alternative views are presented with respect and sympathetically but from the liberal vantage point. Limitation: as the title indicates, *contemporary* and *Anglophone*; the historical figures (Hobbes, Locke, Rousseau, Hegel, Marx) are background rather than primary subjects, and continental political philosophy (Habermas, Foucault, Schmitt) is largely absent. | The first text in a graduate political philosophy sequence. Pair with Swift below for the more recent introductory level. |
| `[Systematic]` Political philosophy — accessible introduction `[Analytic]` `[Self-Study]` | *Political Philosophy: A Beginners' Guide for Students and Politicians*, 4th ed., 2019 (1st ed. 2001) `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Adam Swift** | The best brief introduction to contemporary political philosophy: organised around the central political values (social justice, liberty, equality, community, democracy), with each chapter exhibiting the major positions and their relations. Swift writes for undergraduate readers and for non-specialists (including, on his account, politicians) with serious engagement. The 4th edition substantially updates the social-justice chapter to address contemporary issues. Limitation: brief — chapter-length rather than book-length treatments; for depth move to Kymlicka and to the monographs in §§8.9–8.10. | First reading on political philosophy for general readers and undergraduates. |

### 8.9 Political Philosophy — Justice, Liberty & Equality

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Libertarianism `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | *Anarchy, State, and Utopia*, 1974 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Robert Nozick** | The major libertarian response to Rawls's *Theory of Justice*: Nozick defends a minimal night-watchman state on the grounds that any more extensive state would violate individual rights (especially property rights), and his *entitlement theory of justice* — holdings are just if they were acquired through just initial acquisition or voluntary transfer — is the principal alternative framework to the patterned theories of justice that Rawls's difference principle exemplifies. Nozick's *Wilt Chamberlain* argument (that any pattern of distribution can be disrupted by voluntary transactions) is the most famous critical case against patterned theories. The book is also philosophically rich on the experience machine, the meaning of life, anarchism, and utopia. Limitation: the appeal to self-ownership and to Lockean acquisition has been criticised as morally unfounded (G. A. Cohen's *Self-Ownership, Freedom, and Equality*, 1995, is the major egalitarian reply); Nozick himself partly retreated from the strongest libertarianism in later work. | Required reading. The principal alternative to Rawlsian liberal egalitarianism within liberal political philosophy. See also SEP: "Robert Nozick." |
| `[Research]` Egalitarian critique of Rawls `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Rescuing Justice and Equality*, 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **G. A. Cohen** | Cohen's major critique of the Rawlsian difference principle from the egalitarian left: he argues that the difference principle, as Rawls applies it, illicitly accommodates a *basic structure restriction* (justice applies only to the basic institutions of society, not to individual choices within them) that allows the talented to demand inequality-generating incentives without injustice. Cohen contends this is incompatible with a thoroughgoing egalitarian ethos. The book is the most sustained critique of Rawls from within broadly liberal egalitarianism. Limitation: Cohen's "egalitarian ethos" demand has been criticised as either utopian (it requires moral motivations the basic structure cannot guarantee) or as confused (some Rawlsians argue Rawls already accommodates the ethos at the cultural level). | Graduate students in political philosophy. Read after Rawls's TJ (§8.5). Pair with Cohen's earlier *Self-Ownership, Freedom, and Equality* (Cambridge, 1995) for the systematic egalitarian critique of libertarianism. |
| `[Research]` Capability approach `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Bridge]` | *The Idea of Justice*, 2009 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Amartya Sen** | The Nobel laureate Sen's major statement of his alternative to Rawlsian "transcendental institutionalist" theories of justice. Sen argues that the focus should be on *realised* injustices and their comparative reduction, not on the construction of an ideal just society; the *capability approach* (individuals' substantive freedoms to do and be what they have reason to value, developed jointly with Martha Nussbaum) supplies the framework. The book is also a sustained critical engagement with Rawls, Nozick, and the Anglo-American political-philosophy mainstream from an Indian and broadly cosmopolitan perspective. Limitation: more critical-corrective than constructively systematic; Nussbaum's *Women and Human Development* (Cambridge, 2000) and *Creating Capabilities* (Harvard, 2011) are the more systematic statements of the capability approach as normative theory. | Graduate students in political philosophy or development studies. See also SEP: "The Capability Approach." |
| `[Primary]` Liberty — conceptual analysis `[Analytic]` `[Evergreen]` | "Two Concepts of Liberty", 1958 (in *Four Essays on Liberty*, Oxford, 1969; and *Liberty*, ed. Henry Hardy, Oxford, 2002) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Isaiah Berlin** | The founding modern paper on the analysis of political liberty: Berlin's distinction between *negative liberty* (the absence of obstacles to action) and *positive liberty* (self-mastery, the realisation of one's "true" self). Berlin defends negative liberty as the form most appropriate to a pluralist liberal democracy and is sceptical of positive liberty as historically liable to misuse (the "true self" being whatever a paternalistic authority claims it to be). The framework has structured every subsequent debate on political liberty. Limitation: Berlin's case against positive liberty has been criticised as making it too easy for negative liberty (MacCallum's "Negative and Positive Freedom" presents a unifying account; Skinner and Pettit's republican-freedom revival is the major contemporary alternative — see §8.10). | Required reading in political philosophy. The paper is in *Liberty* (Hardy ed., Oxford, 2002), the most comprehensive single volume of Berlin's political essays. |

### 8.10 Democracy, Legitimacy & Authority

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Liberalism — classical statement `[Evergreen]` | *On Liberty*, 1859 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **John Stuart Mill**; ed. **John Gray** (Oxford World's Classics, 2008) | Mill's classic defence of individual liberty against state and social tyranny: the *harm principle* (the only legitimate ground for restricting individual liberty is the prevention of harm to others), the defence of freedom of thought and discussion (Chapter II), the case for "experiments in living" (Chapter III), and the limits of social authority over the individual. The book is the founding text of modern liberalism in the Anglo-American tradition and remains the most influential statement of the case for freedom of expression. Limitation: Mill's harm principle has been contested at every level (what counts as harm? what counts as self-regarding?), and the apparent tension between Mill's utilitarianism and his liberalism is a long-standing problem in the secondary literature. **Recommended edition:** Gray (Oxford World's Classics) for the standard student edition with Gray's substantial introduction. | Required reading. Pair with the chapter on Mill in *Three Essays on Religion* and *Considerations on Representative Government* for the broader political theory. See also SEP: "John Stuart Mill" and "Mill on Liberty." |
| `[Research]` Discourse ethics and democratic theory `[Continental]` `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Between Facts and Norms: Contributions to a Discourse Theory of Law and Democracy*, 1992 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jürgen Habermas**; trans. **William Rehg** (MIT, 1996) | Habermas's mature political philosophy: the systematic argument that legitimate law and political authority can be grounded only in discourse — in the form of communication oriented to consensus among free and equal participants. The book integrates Habermas's earlier *Theory of Communicative Action* with substantial analysis of constitutional law, the public sphere, and the relation between popular sovereignty and individual rights. *Between Facts and Norms* is the major recent continental work in political philosophy and a principal bridge between the German critical-theory tradition and Anglo-American political philosophy. Limitation: long (~600 pp.) and dense, with substantial technical apparatus from sociology of law and political theory. **Recommended translation:** Rehg (MIT, 1996) — competent and the only English version. | Graduate students in political philosophy. Pair with Rawls's *Political Liberalism* (§8.5) — the Habermas–Rawls exchange (in their respective journals, 1995) is essential dialogue. See also Table 9 §9.7 on the Frankfurt School. |
| `[Research]` Democratic authority `[Analytic]` `[Research]` | *Democratic Authority: A Philosophical Framework*, 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **David Estlund** | The major contemporary analytic monograph on democratic legitimacy. Estlund defends a procedural-epistemic theory: democratic decisions are legitimate because the democratic procedure has *epistemic value* — it tends to track substantive correctness better than the available alternatives — without thereby requiring that those who lose any particular decision were *epistemically wrong*. The framework is the principal alternative to purely procedural and to purely epistemic theories. Limitation: the epistemic claims for actual democratic procedures are themselves empirically contested, and critics (Caplan, Brennan) have argued that real democratic publics fail to clear the epistemic bar Estlund sets. | Graduate students in political philosophy. Pair with Christiano's *The Constitution of Equality* (Oxford, 2008) for the major egalitarian alternative grounding of democracy. |
| `[Research]` Republicanism `[Analytic]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Republicanism: A Theory of Freedom and Government*, 1997 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Philip Pettit** | The major contemporary statement of the neo-republican tradition: freedom is best understood as *non-domination* — not merely non-interference (Berlin's negative liberty) but the absence of being subject to anyone's arbitrary power, regardless of whether that power is actually exercised. The framework recovers the civic-republican tradition of Cicero, Machiavelli, the English commonwealth-men, and Rousseau, and gives it a contemporary systematic statement. Republicanism has become the major alternative to mainstream liberalism in political philosophy. Limitation: critics (Larmore, Carter) have argued that the non-domination conception of freedom either collapses into a sophisticated version of negative liberty or smuggles in egalitarian commitments it does not openly defend. Skinner's *Liberty Before Liberalism* (Cambridge, 1998) is the major historical-recovery companion. | Graduate students in political philosophy. See also SEP: "Republicanism." |

### 8.11 Feminist, Critical & Non-Western Perspectives

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Feminist political philosophy `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Justice, Gender, and the Family*, 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Susan Moller Okin** | The major feminist engagement with the Rawlsian and broader liberal egalitarian tradition. Okin argues that mainstream theories of justice (Rawls's, Nozick's, Walzer's) treat the family as a private institution outside the scope of justice, and that this exclusion systematically disadvantages women — the gendered division of labour in the family is itself unjust, and a just society must extend principles of justice to family life. The book reshaped both feminist political philosophy and the engagement of mainstream political philosophy with gender. Limitation: the framework is broadly liberal-individualist; subsequent feminist political philosophy (Pateman, Young, Fraser — below) has criticised the liberal framework itself from within feminism. | Required reading in feminist political philosophy. See also SEP: "Feminist Political Philosophy." |
| `[Primary]` Feminist critique of contractarianism `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Sexual Contract*, 1988 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Carole Pateman** | The major feminist critique of the social-contract tradition: Pateman argues that the social contract has always been simultaneously a *sexual contract* establishing male sex-right over women, and that the apparent gender-neutrality of liberal contract theory (Hobbes, Locke, Rousseau, and their contemporary heirs) is a structural feature of a patriarchal framework that has merely been bracketed in the contemporary literature. The book is the principal feminist counter-tradition to mainstream contractarianism and shaped subsequent feminist political philosophy (Mills's *Racial Contract* — below — explicitly models its argument on Pateman's). Limitation: the historical-textual case against the canonical contract theorists has been disputed by liberal-feminist readers (notably Okin) who hold that the contract tradition contains resources for feminist critique that Pateman underplays. | Graduate students in political philosophy. The principal alternative tradition to mainstream contractarianism (§8.5) within feminist political philosophy. |
| `[Research]` Justice and the politics of difference `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Justice and the Politics of Difference*, 1990 (2nd ed. 2011) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Iris Marion Young** | Young's systematic critique of distributive-paradigm theories of justice (Rawls, Dworkin, Walzer) from the standpoint of social group oppression. Young identifies *five faces of oppression* — exploitation, marginalisation, powerlessness, cultural imperialism, and violence — that cannot be reduced to maldistribution and that mainstream liberal theories systematically fail to address. The framework has been central to the development of recognition-based justice theories (Honneth, Fraser) and to the integration of identity-politics analyses with mainstream political philosophy. Limitation: Young's framework is sometimes accused of giving up on substantive principles of justice in favour of structural-political analysis; the response (in *Inclusion and Democracy*, Oxford, 2000) is to develop a procedural-deliberative alternative. | Graduate students in political philosophy. Pair with Fraser's *Justice Interruptus* (Routledge, 1997) for the redistribution/recognition debate. |
| `[Research]` Racial contract `[Analytic]` `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Racial Contract*, 1997 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Charles W. Mills** | The major work of contemporary critical philosophy of race. Mills argues that the social-contract tradition presupposes and conceals a *racial contract* — an actual historical agreement among Europeans to constitute themselves as the political community and to dominate non-European peoples on the basis of racial hierarchy. The mainstream contract tradition treats this as outside its scope; Mills argues that this exclusion is itself a feature of the racial contract, not a limit of philosophical interest. Modelled on Pateman's *Sexual Contract* (above) and similarly an immanent critique using the contract framework to expose its own exclusions. Limitation: short (~150 pp.) and polemical — Mills's full constructive philosophical programme is developed across later works (especially *Black Rights / White Wrongs*, Oxford, 2017). | Required reading in critical philosophy of race. See also SEP: "Race." |

### Connections to other tables

This table presupposes Aristotle's *Nicomachean Ethics* (§2.4) and Kant's practical philosophy (§4.3) as primary texts for §8.4 (virtue ethics) and §8.3 (deontology) respectively. Mill, Sidgwick, and Bentham connect to the empiricist tradition of Table 3. Hume's *Treatise* Book III and *Enquiry concerning the Principles of Morals* are the historical background to Blackburn's expressivism (§8.7) and to neo-Humean ethics generally. The Williams texts cited here (§8.1, §8.6) build on his *Descartes* (§3.1) and connect to §1.4 (philosophy as a humanistic discipline). The metaethics section (§8.7) draws systematically on the philosophy of language tools developed in Table 5 (Frege-Geach, truth and minimalism). Parfit's *On What Matters* extends *Reasons and Persons* (§6.8) into normative ethics. Rawls's *Political Liberalism* and the Rawls–Habermas exchange connect this table to Table 9 §9.7 (Frankfurt School). The §8.11 critical-feminist tradition connects forward to Table 9 §9.9 (Beauvoir, Butler, Irigaray) and presupposes Mill's *Subjection of Women* and the broader 19th-century background. MacIntyre's *After Virtue* uses Aristotle (§2.4) and Aquinas (§2.8) and connects to §1.5 historiography. The communitarian critique of Rawls connects directly to Hegel's *Philosophy of Right* (§4.7).

# Table 9 · Phenomenology, Hermeneutics & Continental Philosophy

*Scope:* Husserlian phenomenology, Heidegger's early and late thought, Merleau-Pontian embodied phenomenology, Sartrean existentialism, philosophical hermeneutics (Gadamer, Ricoeur), the Frankfurt School (Horkheimer, Adorno, Habermas), French structuralism and poststructuralism (Foucault, Derrida, Deleuze), continental feminist philosophy, and the major bridge texts between analytic and continental traditions. The translation question is consequential at every stage; the difficulty of the primary literature is real and not to be evaded.

### 9.1 Husserl — Primary Texts & Commentary

**Translation orientation.** Husserl wrote in German across half a century (1887–1938) and his published corpus is large; the unpublished *Nachlass* (the *Husserliana* critical edition) is larger still. English translations have been uneven: the *Logical Investigations* and *Ideas I* exist in multiple translations of varying quality, and the late *Crisis* in only one. The current best practice is to use the Husserliana volume numbers (e.g. *Hua* III/1 for *Ideas I*) and to consult the German alongside the English where the philosophical stakes are high.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Phenomenology — founding work | *Logical Investigations*, 2 vols., 1900–1901 (2nd ed. 1913–1921) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Edmund Husserl**; trans. **J. N. Findlay** (Routledge, 1970; 2001 ed. with new introduction by **Dermot Moran**) | Husserl's first major work and the founding text of the phenomenological movement. Volume 1 is the *Prolegomena to Pure Logic* — a sustained critique of psychologism in logic and an argument for the objectivity of logical and mathematical truths. Volume 2 contains six logical investigations on expression and meaning, ideal unities and species, parts and wholes, the difference between independent and non-independent meanings, intentional experiences, and the phenomenology of knowledge. The Sixth Investigation — on the structure of categorial intuition and the conditions of knowledge — was Heidegger's principal route into philosophy and the immediate background to *Being and Time*. Limitation: the work pre-dates Husserl's transcendental turn (announced in *Ideas I*, below); the Husserl of the *Logical Investigations* is a realist about logical objects and a descriptive psychologist of intentional experience, not yet the transcendental phenomenologist of the later writings. **Recommended translation:** Findlay (Routledge, 1970; Moran's 2001 revised edition with new introduction) is the standard; the German is in Husserliana XVIII–XIX. | Required reading in phenomenology. Read the *Prolegomena* and the Sixth Investigation; the middle investigations more selectively. See also SEP: "Edmund Husserl." |
| `[Primary]` Transcendental phenomenology | *Ideas Pertaining to a Pure Phenomenology and to a Phenomenological Philosophy, First Book*, 1913 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Edmund Husserl**; trans. **F. Kersten** (Husserliana edition, Kluwer, 1982); alternative: **Daniel O. Dahlstrom** (Hackett, 2014) | The systematic presentation of Husserl's transcendental phenomenology after the so-called *transcendental turn*: the doctrine of the *phenomenological reduction* (the methodological suspension — *epoché* — of the natural attitude in favour of attention to the structures of consciousness), the analysis of intentionality and the noesis/noema distinction, the doctrine of essences and eidetic intuition, and the programme of transcendental phenomenology as the foundational science. *Ideas I* is the text from which the phenomenological movement organised itself and against which Heidegger, Sartre, and Merleau-Ponty defined their projects. Limitation: difficult and methodologically dense; the relation between the *Ideas I* phenomenology and the earlier *Logical Investigations* is itself a major scholarly question. **Recommended translation:** Kersten (1982) for the standard scholarly version; Dahlstrom (Hackett, 2014) for a more readable recent translation with helpful glossary. The Boyce Gibson translation (1931) is now superseded. | Required for engagement with classical phenomenology. Read with Zahavi below. |
| `[Primary]` Late Husserl — the crisis of the sciences `[Bridge]` | *The Crisis of European Sciences and Transcendental Phenomenology*, 1936 (partial), 1954 (full, posthumous) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Edmund Husserl**; trans. **David Carr** (Northwestern, 1970) | Husserl's final major work and his most accessible. Three central themes: the *crisis* of European rationality, in which the mathematisation of nature begun by Galileo has produced a science abstracted from the *lifeworld* (*Lebenswelt*) of concrete human experience; the recovery of the lifeworld as the indispensable ground of all theoretical activity; and a renewed argument for transcendental phenomenology as the discipline appropriate to that ground. The doctrine of the lifeworld is among Husserl's most influential late contributions and the principal Husserlian background for Habermas (§9.7), Schutz, and the social-theoretic appropriation of phenomenology. Limitation: incomplete at Husserl's death — only the first two of three projected parts and substantial supplementary material survive; the systematic argument for the lifeworld's grounding role must be partly reconstructed from the materials. **Recommended translation:** Carr (Northwestern, 1970) — the only English version, competent and integrated with Carr's own substantial Husserl scholarship. | The most accessible entry point to Husserl. Read with Zahavi's *Husserl's Phenomenology* (below). |
| `[Secondary]` Husserl — single-author overview `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *Husserl's Phenomenology*, 2003 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Dan Zahavi** | The best single-volume introduction to Husserl's phenomenology in English: concise (~150 pp.), philosophically engaged, and integrated with both the continental phenomenological tradition and analytic philosophy of mind. Zahavi treats the development of Husserl's thought, the transcendental reduction, intentionality, time-consciousness, intersubjectivity, and the lifeworld with uncommon clarity. Limitation: introductory; for research depth on specific topics consult the relevant Cambridge Companion essays (Smith & Smith eds., 1995) or Zahavi's larger *Self-Awareness and Alterity* (Northwestern, 1999). | The first secondary text to read on Husserl. Pair with Sokolowski's *Introduction to Phenomenology* (Cambridge, 2000) for the broader phenomenological orientation. |

### 9.2 Heidegger — *Being and Time*: Primary Text & Commentary

**The Heidegger translation problem.** The Macquarrie–Robinson translation of *Sein und Zeit* (Harper & Row, 1962) was the only English version for over thirty years and remains widely cited, but its handling of central terms is interpretively coloured: *Dasein* is left untranslated but *Zuhandenheit* becomes "readiness-to-hand," *Vorhandenheit* "presence-at-hand," *Zeitlichkeit* "temporality" (losing the resonance with *Zeit*), *Sein-können* "potentiality-for-Being," and the existential structures are often given a Sartrean-existentialist gloss that misrepresents Heidegger's intentions. The Stambaugh translation (SUNY, 1996), substantially revised by **Dennis J. Schmidt** (SUNY, 2010), is now preferred by most scholars: it is more literal, handles Heidegger's hyphenated coinages more consistently, and integrates with the broader Stambaugh-translated late Heidegger corpus. For serious work, both translations should be consulted along with the German.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Fundamental ontology | *Being and Time*, 1927 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Martin Heidegger**; trans. **Joan Stambaugh**, rev. **Dennis J. Schmidt** (SUNY, 2010); alternative: **John Macquarrie & Edward Robinson** (Harper & Row, 1962) | The most influential work of 20th-century continental philosophy: Heidegger's attempt to renew the question of the meaning of Being by way of an analytic of *Dasein* (the being that we ourselves are, for whom Being is at issue). The book is divided into a published Division I (the existential analytic of being-in-the-world, the structures of equipment-use, anxiety, falling, care) and a Division II (death, conscience, authenticity, temporality as the meaning of care), with the projected Divisions III and onward never appearing. The framework reshaped phenomenology, hermeneutics, theology, philosophy of technology, existentialism (Sartre, Beauvoir), and indirectly post-structuralism. Limitation: Heidegger's prose is wilfully obscure, the architectonic of the book breaks off unfinished, and the existential analytic of authenticity contains material whose relation to Heidegger's subsequent National Socialist commitments is a serious scholarly and ethical question (cf. the Black Notebooks debate, 2014–). **Recommended translation:** Stambaugh-Schmidt (SUNY, 2010) for current scholarship; Macquarrie–Robinson (1962) for the historical reference still cited in the older literature. Use the German Gesamtausgabe pagination for citation. | Always read with substantial secondary support (Mulhall or Dreyfus). Read Division I before Division II. See also SEP: "Martin Heidegger." |
| `[Secondary]` *Being and Time* — pragmatist commentary `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Being-in-the-World: A Commentary on Heidegger's Being and Time, Division I*, 1991 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hubert L. Dreyfus** | Dreyfus's section-by-section commentary on Division I, developed over decades of teaching: Heidegger is read as a kind of philosophical pragmatist whose analysis of skilled comportment with equipment (*Zuhandenheit*) precedes and grounds the theoretical-representational stance (*Vorhandenheit*). The reading is bracingly analytic in style, and Dreyfus's connections with Merleau-Ponty, Wittgenstein, and the cognitive sciences make this the principal bridge text for English-speaking readers from analytic philosophy and AI into Heidegger. Limitation: covers only Division I — the analyses of authenticity, conscience, death, and the temporality structures of Division II receive no commentary. Dreyfus's pragmatist reading is also contested (especially by Carman, Wrathall, and Sheehan) as too analytic and as marginalising the religious-historical dimensions of Heidegger's project. | Required reading. The principal route into Heidegger for analytic-trained readers. Pair with Mulhall (below) and with §7.7 for the broader Dreyfusian project. |
| `[Secondary]` *Being and Time* — introduction `[Self-Study]` | *Heidegger and Being and Time* (Routledge Philosophy Guidebooks), 3rd ed., 2013 (1st ed. 1996) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Stephen Mulhall** | The best section-by-section introduction to the whole of *Being and Time*, including the Division II material that Dreyfus does not cover. Mulhall's commentary is methodically organised and presupposes no prior Heidegger background; the treatment of authenticity, death, and temporality is the most accessible in English. The 3rd edition is substantially revised. Limitation: introductory in depth; for engagement with the major interpretive debates consult Dreyfus, Sheehan, or Carman's *Heidegger's Analytic* (Cambridge, 2003). | Read alongside Division II of *Being and Time*. The book to put in the hands of a graduate student starting Heidegger. |
| `[Secondary]` *Being and Time* — alternative interpretation `[Research]` | *Heidegger's Being and Time: A Reader's Guide*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **William Blattner** | A more philosophically argumentative commentary than Mulhall's, developing Blattner's distinctive *temporal idealism* interpretation: Heidegger's central thesis is that temporality is the transcendental condition of the being of beings, and the much-discussed Kehre (turn) in Heidegger's later thought is to be read as a working-out of difficulties in this position. Blattner's earlier *Heidegger's Temporal Idealism* (Cambridge, 1999) is the longer scholarly version. Limitation: temporal idealism is one interpretation among several; readers should weigh it against Dreyfus's pragmatism, Sheehan's "clearing"-centred reading, and the more religious-historical interpretations. | Graduate students with prior Heidegger exposure who want the major contemporary interpretive alternative. |

### 9.3 Heidegger — Later Work & Influence

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Late Heidegger — selection `[Anthology]` `[Reference]` | *Basic Writings*, 2nd rev. ed., 2008 (1st ed. 1977) `[Advanced]` `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | **Martin Heidegger**; ed. **David Farrell Krell**; multiple translators | The standard English-language selection from Heidegger's late writings: "The Origin of the Work of Art," "Letter on Humanism," "The Question Concerning Technology," "The Way to Language," "What Calls for Thinking?," and the post-*Being and Time* recovery essay "The End of Philosophy and the Task of Thinking." Krell's introductions situate each essay; the translations are by various hands but consistently competent. The volume is the principal way most readers will encounter the late Heidegger before (or instead of) the daunting *Contributions to Philosophy* (below). Limitation: a selection — the *Nietzsche* lectures (4 vols.), the Hölderlin readings, the *Parmenides* and *Heraclitus* lecture courses, and much else are not included; consult the Indiana University Press *Studies in Continental Thought* series for the broader corpus. | The natural entry to the late Heidegger. Read after *Being and Time* and Mulhall. |
| `[Primary]` Late Heidegger — magnum opus | *Contributions to Philosophy (Of the Event)*, written 1936–1938, published 1989 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Martin Heidegger**; trans. **Richard Rojcewicz & Daniela Vallega-Neu** (Indiana, 2012); alternative: **Parvis Emad & Kenneth Maly** (Indiana, 1999, as *Beiträge zur Philosophie*) | Heidegger's most ambitious unpublished work and the central text of his "second beginning" (*der andere Anfang*) of philosophy. The book is organised in six "joinings" (*Fügungen*) — Echo, Playing-Forth, Leap, Grounding, The Ones to Come, The Last God — and develops the late thought of *Ereignis* (the event of appropriation), the history of being, and the overcoming of metaphysics. The text is notoriously difficult; Heidegger himself did not publish it, and its eventual posthumous publication in 1989 began the major scholarly engagement with the late thought. Limitation: among the most opaque texts in 20th-century philosophy; without prior orientation in Heidegger's published late writings (*Basic Writings*, *On the Way to Language*, *What Is Called Thinking?*) the book is essentially unreadable. **Recommended translation:** Rojcewicz–Vallega-Neu (Indiana, 2012) — the second, more carefully revised translation; the earlier Emad–Maly is now superseded. | Researchers in late Heidegger. Not a first or second reading of Heidegger; the destination, not the starting point. See also Sheehan's *Making Sense of Heidegger* (below). |
| `[Secondary]` Heidegger — interpretive map `[Research]` | *Making Sense of Heidegger: A Paradigm Shift*, 2015 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Thomas Sheehan** | A polemical and influential recent re-reading of Heidegger's entire corpus around what Sheehan calls *die Lichtung* (the clearing) rather than around the "question of the meaning of Being" as conventionally translated. Sheehan argues that Heidegger's project from beginning to end concerns the ontological clearing — the space of intelligibility — that makes meaningful encounters with beings possible, and that the standard "Being-with-a-capital-B" rendering systematically obscures this. The book has reshaped the Anglophone Heidegger scholarship of the past decade. Limitation: Sheehan's reading is polemical and many Heideggerians dispute the proposed paradigm shift; readers should weigh it against the alternative interpretive frameworks (Dreyfus, Wrathall, Polt, Capobianco). | Researchers in Heidegger. Pair with the late Heidegger primary texts above. |

### 9.4 Merleau-Ponty — Phenomenology of Perception & Embodiment

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Embodied phenomenology — magnum opus `[Bridge]` | *Phenomenology of Perception*, 1945 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Maurice Merleau-Ponty**; trans. **Donald A. Landes** (Routledge, 2012); alternative: **Colin Smith** (Routledge, 1962) | Merleau-Ponty's major work and the founding text of philosophical accounts of embodied perception. The book develops a phenomenology of perception in which the body is neither object (as in Cartesian dualism) nor representational vehicle (as in classical cognitivism) but the *subject* of perception — the *body schema* through which the world is given to us as meaningful. The framework draws on Husserl and Heidegger but also engages substantively with the gestalt psychology of the 1930s, with the neurological literature on the case of Schneider (the brain-injured patient with severe motor and perceptual disturbance), and with classical psychophysics. The work is the principal source for the contemporary embodied-cognition movement (§7.7) and has been central to the analytic-phenomenological bridge. Limitation: long (~500 pp.) and methodologically heterogeneous; Merleau-Ponty's engagement with the empirical psychology of his time has dated parts of the argument, though the philosophical framework remains live. **Recommended translation:** Landes (Routledge, 2012) — the new scholarly standard, with substantially better handling of Merleau-Ponty's technical vocabulary; Smith (1962) is the older translation and remains in wide citation. | Read with Carman below. Central for §7.7. See also SEP: "Maurice Merleau-Ponty." |
| `[Primary]` Late Merleau-Ponty — ontology of the flesh | *The Visible and the Invisible*, 1964 (posthumous, incomplete) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Maurice Merleau-Ponty**; trans. **Alphonso Lingis** (Northwestern, 1968) | Merleau-Ponty's unfinished late work, on which he was working at his death in 1961. The book develops an ontology of the *flesh* (*la chair*) — a fundamental element of being (more basic than the subject/object distinction) of which both perceiving body and perceived world are modifications. The text is incomplete and the published version supplements the working manuscript with working notes; readers must work to assemble the systematic position. The flesh ontology has influenced subsequent French philosophy (Henry, Marion, Nancy) and continues to be productive in environmental philosophy and philosophy of art. Limitation: incompletely worked out and presentationally fragmentary. **Recommended translation:** Lingis (Northwestern, 1968) — the only English translation. | Graduate students in continental ontology or environmental philosophy. Read after *Phenomenology of Perception*. |
| `[Secondary]` Merleau-Ponty — single-author overview `[Bridge]` `[Self-Study]` | *Merleau-Ponty* (Routledge Philosophers), 2008 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Taylor Carman** | The best single-volume study of Merleau-Ponty in English: chapters on the body, perception, intersubjectivity, language, and the ontology of the late work, integrated with substantial engagement with the analytic philosophy of mind (Dreyfus, McDowell, contemporary embodied cognition). Carman is uncommonly clear about which Merleau-Pontian arguments survive in contemporary debates and which depend on now-superseded empirical assumptions. Limitation: focused on Merleau-Ponty's philosophy of mind and ontology; the political and aesthetic dimensions receive less attention. | Read after a first pass through *Phenomenology of Perception*. The principal companion in English. |

### 9.5 Sartre & Existentialism

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Existentialist phenomenology | *Being and Nothingness: An Essay on Phenomenological Ontology*, 1943 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jean-Paul Sartre**; trans. **Sarah Richmond** (Routledge, 2018); alternative: **Hazel Barnes** (Philosophical Library, 1956) | Sartre's principal philosophical work and the founding text of French existentialism: the analysis of consciousness as *for-itself* (*pour-soi*) against the in-itself (*en-soi*) of brute being, the doctrine of nothingness as the structure that consciousness introduces into the world, the analyses of bad faith, of the look of the other, of concrete relations with others (love, masochism, sadism, hate), and of freedom and responsibility. Sartre's framework — radical freedom, the absence of any pre-given human nature ("existence precedes essence"), the negative-ontological structures of consciousness — defined existentialism for a generation and remains the most ambitious phenomenological-ontological synthesis after Heidegger. Limitation: long (~800 pp.) and uneven; the early chapters on bad faith and the look are widely read, the long Part Three on concrete relations less so. Sartre's psychology of the emotions and his account of sadism/masochism are partly dated by his pre-WWII frame. **Recommended translation:** Richmond (Routledge, 2018) — the new scholarly translation, substantially more accurate than Barnes; the Barnes (1956) translation has been the standard for sixty years but has known imprecisions. | Required reading in 20th-century continental philosophy. See also SEP: "Jean-Paul Sartre." |
| `[Primary]` Existentialism — popular statement | *Existentialism Is a Humanism*, 1946 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jean-Paul Sartre**; trans. **Carol Macomber** (Yale, 2007); ed. **John Kulka** | Sartre's famous 1945 public lecture, posthumously the most-read text of mid-century French philosophy. The lecture compresses the central existentialist doctrines for a general audience: "existence precedes essence," radical freedom and the resulting anguish, "bad faith," the relation between individual existential freedom and political solidarity, and the engagement with both communism and Christianity. The Yale 2007 edition includes Sartre's own 1964 explanation of his dissatisfaction with the lecture, plus a 1945 discussion with Pierre Naville. Limitation: simplified — Sartre himself later regarded the lecture as a popular oversimplification of *Being and Nothingness*, and many critics (notably Beauvoir) thought the lecture had misrepresented the substance of existentialist philosophy. The lecture is therefore best read as orientation, not as a substitute for *Being and Nothingness*. | Useful for first contact; do not stop here. Read with Beauvoir's *The Ethics of Ambiguity* (§9.9 — the philosophical companion that *Being and Nothingness* did not produce). |
| `[Secondary]` Existentialism — historical context `[Bridge]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | *From Hegel to Existentialism*, 1987 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Robert C. Solomon** | A collection of essays placing existentialism in its German-philosophical context — Hegel, Schopenhauer, Kierkegaard, Nietzsche — and exhibiting the conceptual roots of the existentialist project of the 1930s and 1940s. Solomon is particularly strong on the relation between Sartre's project and Hegel's *Phenomenology* (the chapter on Sartre's reading of Hegel through Kojève is excellent) and on the moral philosophy of existentialism. Limitation: a collection of essays rather than a continuous treatise; readers must assemble the cumulative case. Walter Kaufmann's older *From Shakespeare to Existentialism* and *Existentialism from Dostoevsky to Sartre* are useful complements. | The first secondary text on existentialism. See also SEP: "Existentialism." |

### 9.6 Hermeneutics — Gadamer, Ricoeur

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Philosophical hermeneutics — magnum opus `[Evergreen]` | *Truth and Method*, 1960 (revised editions through 1990) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hans-Georg Gadamer**; trans. **Joel Weinsheimer & Donald G. Marshall** (Continuum, 2nd rev. ed. 1989) | Gadamer's major work and the founding text of philosophical hermeneutics: a sustained argument that understanding is not a method to be applied to texts and historical artifacts but the basic mode of human being in the world. The book is divided into three parts: aesthetics (the model of the work of art as the entry to the hermeneutic problem), the historical sciences (the dialectic of question and answer; the fusion of horizons; prejudice as enabling rather than obstacle to understanding), and the universal hermeneutic dimension of language. The framework draws on Heidegger (Gadamer's teacher) while substantially correcting and extending him in the direction of dialogue and tradition. Limitation: long (~600 pp.) and dense in its German-philosophical-historical references; the engagement with Schleiermacher, Dilthey, and Hegel can be unfamiliar to readers without that background. **Recommended translation:** Weinsheimer–Marshall (Continuum, 1989) — the second revised translation, substantially better than the older Barden–Cumming. | Required reading in continental philosophy. See also SEP: "Hans-Georg Gadamer." |
| `[Primary]` Hermeneutics and selfhood `[Bridge]` `[Evergreen]` | *Oneself as Another*, 1990 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Paul Ricoeur**; trans. **Kathleen Blamey** (Chicago, 1992) | Ricoeur's Gifford Lectures and the systematic statement of his late hermeneutic philosophy of the self. Ten studies move from a semantics and pragmatics of the self (drawing on analytic philosophy of language and action — Anscombe, Davidson, Strawson) through narrative identity to an ethics of mutual recognition. The book is one of the most successful bridge texts in 20th-century philosophy — explicitly engaging analytic action theory, phenomenology, and the hermeneutic tradition in a single sustained argument. Limitation: dense and ambitious; the analytic engagement is sometimes selectively conducted (Ricoeur engages Davidson but not, e.g., Bratman or Velleman). **Recommended translation:** Blamey (Chicago, 1992) — careful and the standard. | Graduate students in continental philosophy, action theory, or ethics. The principal bridge from hermeneutics to analytic action theory and ethics. See also SEP: "Paul Ricoeur." |
| `[Anthology]` Hermeneutics — bridge to structuralism `[Bridge]` | *Hermeneutics and the Human Sciences*, 1981 `[Advanced]` `[Anthology]` | **Paul Ricoeur**; ed. & trans. **John B. Thompson** (Cambridge, 1981) | A collection of Ricoeur's essays presenting his mature hermeneutic position in dialogue with structuralism (Lévi-Strauss, the early Foucault), with analytic philosophy of action, and with the social sciences. The essays "The Hermeneutical Function of Distanciation," "What Is a Text?," "The Model of the Text" (on meaningful action understood as text), and "Hermeneutics and the Critique of Ideology" (on the Habermas–Gadamer debate) are individually classic. The volume is the most efficient introduction to Ricoeur's middle-period hermeneutics and to his engagement with both continental and analytic interlocutors. Limitation: a collection rather than a treatise; the systematic position is to be assembled from the essays. | Graduate students. The best single-volume introduction to Ricoeur and a model of the hermeneutic-bridge methodology. |

### 9.7 The Frankfurt School — Critical Theory

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Founding work of critical theory | *Dialectic of Enlightenment: Philosophical Fragments*, 1947 (drafted 1944) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Max Horkheimer & Theodor W. Adorno**; ed. **Gunzelin Schmid Noerr**; trans. **Edmund Jephcott** (Stanford, 2002) | The founding text of the first-generation Frankfurt School: an extended argument that the Enlightenment project of demystifying nature through rational mastery turns against itself, producing a totally administered society in which reason has become instrumental and humans have become objects of their own technologies. The book is organised in five essays — the main "Concept of Enlightenment," two excursuses (on Odysseus as proto-bourgeois subject; on Juliette and the dialectic of morality), and chapters on the culture industry and on antisemitism. Written in California during the Second World War, the book is haunted by both the European catastrophe and the early American mass-culture experience the authors found there. Limitation: prophetic and aphoristic in style rather than argumentatively systematic; the totalising critique has been criticised (by Habermas in particular) as leaving no resources for its own grounding. **Recommended translation:** Jephcott (Stanford, 2002) — the new authoritative translation; the older Cumming (1972) is now superseded. | Required reading in critical theory. See also SEP: "Theodor W. Adorno." |
| `[Primary]` Adorno — late negative dialectics | *Negative Dialectics*, 1966 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Theodor W. Adorno**; trans. **E. B. Ashton** (Continuum, 1973); alternative: new **Dennis Redmond** (online, 2001) | Adorno's most systematic late philosophical work: a sustained meditation on the inadequacy of identity-thinking (the philosophical drive to subsume the particular under universal concepts) and an argument for *negative dialectics* — a thinking that resists conceptual closure and remains in continuous self-correcting motion. The book is also a sustained critique of Hegel (whose dialectic was identity-driven) and of Heidegger (whose ontology Adorno regards as covertly affirmative jargon). Limitation: notoriously difficult prose, even by continental standards; the negative-dialectical method resists summary or paraphrase by its nature. **Recommended translation:** Ashton (Continuum, 1973) — the standard, though with acknowledged imperfections. *Minima Moralia* (Adorno's aphoristic *Reflections from Damaged Life*, 1951) is the much more accessible companion volume and a recommended first entry to Adorno. | Graduate students with substantial prior Hegelian background. Pair with *Minima Moralia* for the accessible entry. See also SEP: "Theodor W. Adorno." |
| `[Primary]` Habermas — discourse theory of communicative action `[Bridge]` | *The Theory of Communicative Action*, 2 vols., 1981 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jürgen Habermas**; trans. **Thomas McCarthy** (Beacon, 1984, 1987) | Habermas's systematic mature work and the principal continental alternative to first-generation Frankfurt School pessimism. Vol. 1 develops a theory of *communicative action* — action coordinated through speech acts oriented to mutual understanding rather than to strategic success — in dialogue with Weber, Mead, and (importantly) Austin's speech-act theory (cf. §5.6); Vol. 2 develops the systematic social theory and the diagnosis of the *colonisation of the lifeworld* by economic and administrative imperatives. The work is one of the major successful bridges between continental social philosophy and analytic philosophy of language. Limitation: enormous (~1,000 pp.) and integrates substantial empirical sociology with the philosophical core; readers selecting are advised to consult Vol. 1 chapters 1, 3, and 5 and Vol. 2 chapter 6. **Recommended translation:** McCarthy (Beacon, 1984/1987) — the standard, with substantial introductions integrated with McCarthy's *The Critical Theory of Jürgen Habermas* (MIT, 1978). | Graduate students in critical theory or philosophy of language. The principal contemporary Frankfurt School text and a major bridge to analytic philosophy. Pair with *Between Facts and Norms* (§8.10) for the political-legal extension. See also SEP: "Jürgen Habermas." |
| `[Secondary]` Frankfurt School — history `[Reference]` `[Evergreen]` | *The Dialectical Imagination: A History of the Frankfurt School and the Institute of Social Research, 1923–1950*, 1973 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Martin Jay** | The standard historical study of the Frankfurt School's first generation: Horkheimer, Adorno, Marcuse, Pollock, Lowenthal, Benjamin, and the institutional history of the Institut für Sozialforschung from Frankfurt to New York and Los Angeles. Jay's narrative is the entry point for English-speaking readers to the broader intellectual and political context of *Dialectic of Enlightenment* and the Frankfurt project. Limitation: covers the first generation through 1950; for the second-generation Habermas-Honneth tradition, consult Honneth's *The Critique of Power* (MIT, 1991) or the *Cambridge Companion to Critical Theory* (Rush, ed., 2004). | The first secondary text on the Frankfurt School. |

### 9.8 French Structuralism & Poststructuralism

**The Foucault problem.** Foucault's work is conventionally divided into three periods: the *archaeological* (1961–1969; *History of Madness*, *The Order of Things*, *The Archaeology of Knowledge*) — concerned with the historical *a priori* conditions of discursive formations; the *genealogical* (1971–1976; *Discipline and Punish*, *History of Sexuality* Vol. 1) — adding Nietzschean genealogy and the analysis of power/knowledge; and the *ethics of the self* (1976–1984; *History of Sexuality* Vols. 2–3) — focusing on practices of self-formation through which subjects constitute themselves. These periods address different problems by partly different methods and should not be treated as a unified system. **The Derrida problem.** Derrida's notorious difficulty is real, not affected. Reading Derrida without secondary support produces predictable misreadings; Culler's *On Deconstruction* (Cornell, 1982) and Norris's *Derrida* (Harvard, 1987) are the recommended entry points.

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Foucault — archaeological period | *The Order of Things: An Archaeology of the Human Sciences*, 1966 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Michel Foucault**; trans. anonymous (Pantheon, 1970; Vintage, 1994) | Foucault's first major theoretical work and the founding text of the archaeological period: an analysis of the *epistemes* (historical *a priori* structures of knowledge) underlying the human sciences in Western thought from the Renaissance through the modern period. The famous opening on the Borges Chinese-encyclopedia taxonomy and the closing prediction that "man" — the figure of modern epistemic discourse — is a recent invention soon to disappear "like a face drawn in sand at the edge of the sea" are individual classics. The book's argument that the human sciences arose in a particular historical configuration around the figure of *empirico-transcendental Man* shaped both subsequent French philosophy and the history of the human sciences. Limitation: long, dense, and methodologically opaque; Foucault's later *Archaeology of Knowledge* (1969) is the methodological explanation. **Recommended translation:** the anonymous Pantheon (1970) translation has had widely-discussed imperfections but remains the only complete English version. | Graduate students in continental philosophy or history of the human sciences. Read with Dreyfus and Rabinow (below). See also SEP: "Michel Foucault." |
| `[Primary]` Foucault — genealogical and ethics-of-self periods | *Discipline and Punish: The Birth of the Prison*, 1975, and *The History of Sexuality*, 4 vols., 1976–1984 (Vol. 1 published 1976; Vols. 2 & 3 1984; Vol. 4 posthumously 2018) `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Michel Foucault**; trans. **Alan Sheridan** (*Discipline and Punish*, Vintage, 1977); **Robert Hurley** (*HoS* Vols. 1–3, Random House, 1978–1986); **Frédéric Gros** ed. for Vol. 4 (*Confessions of the Flesh*, trans.
