Hurley, Pantheon, 2021) | *Discipline and Punish* is Foucault's genealogy of modern penal practices and the founding analysis of *disciplinary power* — power that operates through surveillance, normalisation, and the production of "docile bodies" rather than through sovereign violence. The book contains the famous analyses of the Panopticon and of the transition from public execution to the carceral system. *History of Sexuality* Vol. 1 (*The Will to Knowledge*) develops the related concept of *biopower* — power over life — and the *repressive hypothesis* critique. Vols. 2 and 3 (*The Use of Pleasure*, *The Care of the Self*) mark the shift to the ethics-of-the-self period, with extended analyses of classical Greek and Hellenistic-Roman practices of self-formation. The posthumous Vol. 4 (*Confessions of the Flesh*) continues into the early Christian period. Limitation: the genealogical method's historiographical accuracy has been contested at every point by historians; whether the philosophical project survives the historical contests is a major question in the Foucault literature. **Recommended translations:** Sheridan and Hurley are the standards; the new Vol. 4 (Hurley, 2021) is essential for any complete picture of the late Foucault. | Required reading in contemporary continental philosophy. See also SEP: "Michel Foucault." |
| `[Primary]` Derrida — deconstruction founding | *Of Grammatology*, 1967 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Jacques Derrida**; trans. **Gayatri Chakravorty Spivak** (Johns Hopkins, 1976; 40th anniversary ed. 2016) | Derrida's most influential book and the founding text of deconstruction: an extended argument that the privileging of speech over writing in the Western philosophical tradition (from Plato through Saussure) is symptomatic of a broader *logocentrism* — the metaphysical assumption that meaning is fully present to itself in the moment of speaking. Derrida's analyses of Saussurean linguistics and of Rousseau's *Essay on the Origin of Languages* exhibit the deconstructive method: not the destruction of texts but the demonstration that the conceptual hierarchies they appear to establish are unstable and self-subverting. *Of Grammatology* is published alongside *Writing and Difference* (1967) and *Speech and Phenomena* (1967) — the three constitute Derrida's mature debut. Limitation: Derrida's prose is wilfully unstable and rejects standard argumentative form; many readings — both sympathetic and hostile — proceed by misunderstanding. Culler's *On Deconstruction* (Cornell, 1982) and Norris's *Derrida* (Harvard, 1987) are essential as guides. **Recommended translation:** Spivak (Johns Hopkins, 1976; 40th anniversary ed. 2016) — substantial introduction, has become the canonical English text. | Graduate students in continental philosophy or literary theory. Always read with secondary support (Culler, Norris). See also SEP: "Jacques Derrida." |
| `[Primary]` Deleuze — metaphysics of difference | *Difference and Repetition*, 1968, and (with Félix Guattari) *A Thousand Plateaus*, 1980 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Gilles Deleuze**; trans. **Paul Patton** (*Difference and Repetition*, Columbia, 1994); **Gilles Deleuze & Félix Guattari**; trans. **Brian Massumi** (*A Thousand Plateaus*, Minnesota, 1987) | *Difference and Repetition* is Deleuze's principal philosophical work: a metaphysical statement of difference-in-itself (against the subordination of difference to identity that Deleuze diagnoses across the Western tradition from Aristotle through Hegel) and a reconception of repetition not as the recurrence of the same but as the production of difference. The book draws systematically on Bergson, Nietzsche, Spinoza, and Hume while criticising the dialectical Hegelian tradition Deleuze regards as the principal obstacle to a philosophy of difference. *A Thousand Plateaus* (with Guattari) is the major political-philosophical extension — the schizoanalytic critique of psychoanalysis and capitalism continued from *Anti-Oedipus* (1972), the analysis of social formations through concepts like *assemblages*, *rhizome*, *territorialisation*, and *deterritorialisation*. Limitation: Deleuze's vocabulary is highly idiosyncratic and resistant to standard philosophical exposition; both books require substantial work to extract systematic positions. **Recommended translations:** Patton and Massumi — both standards, both with substantial translators' introductions. | Graduate students. Daniel Smith's *Essays on Deleuze* (Edinburgh, 2012) is the indispensable English-language guide. See also SEP: "Gilles Deleuze." |
| `[Secondary]` Foucault — major commentary `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Michel Foucault: Beyond Structuralism and Hermeneutics*, 2nd ed., 1983 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **Hubert L. Dreyfus & Paul Rabinow** | The most influential English-language commentary on Foucault and a model of cross-tradition philosophical engagement: Dreyfus (analytic-trained Heideggerian) and Rabinow (Foucault's Berkeley colleague and intellectual collaborator) work systematically through the archaeological and genealogical periods, situating Foucault between structuralism (Lévi-Strauss, Althusser) and hermeneutics (Heidegger, Gadamer) and arguing that he transcends both. The 2nd edition adds a substantial afterword by Foucault himself ("The Subject and Power") and an interview that became central to Foucault's late self-understanding. Limitation: pre-dates the late ethics-of-the-self period and so requires supplementation by the later literature (Davidson, McGushin, Gros) on the third Foucault. The book remains the principal bridge between continental Foucault scholarship and Anglo-American philosophy. | Required reading on Foucault. The book that put Foucault on the analytic philosophical map. |

### 9.9 Feminist Philosophy & Continental Tradition

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Founding work of modern feminist philosophy | *The Second Sex*, 1949 `[Advanced]` `[Evergreen]` | **Simone de Beauvoir**; trans. **Constance Borde & Sheila Malovany-Chevallier** (Knopf, 2009); alternative: **H. M. Parshley** (Knopf, 1953) | The founding work of 20th-century feminist philosophy: a sustained existential-phenomenological analysis of women's situation as the *Other* in a patriarchal society — the structures of biology, psychoanalysis, history, and myth that have constructed "woman" as the second sex, and the lived experience (childhood, sexual initiation, marriage, motherhood, ageing) through which women come to be what they are. The book's argument that "one is not born, but rather becomes, a woman" (Vol. 2 opening) is the founding statement of the social-construction analysis of gender. *The Ethics of Ambiguity* (1947) is the companion existentialist ethics that *Being and Nothingness* never produced. Limitation: the long-standard Parshley translation (1953) was a substantially abridged and philosophically distorting version — Parshley was a zoologist who cut roughly 15% of the text and altered Beauvoir's philosophical vocabulary throughout. The complaint went uncorrected for over fifty years. **Recommended translation:** Borde & Malovany-Chevallier (Knopf, 2009) — the first complete English translation, philosophically scrupulous; cite this edition for any serious work. Parshley remains the historical reference for older scholarship. | Required reading in feminist philosophy, existentialism, and 20th-century continental philosophy. See also SEP: "Simone de Beauvoir." |
| `[Primary]` Sexual difference philosophy | *This Sex Which Is Not One*, 1977 `[Advanced]` `[Research]` | **Luce Irigaray**; trans. **Catherine Porter & Carolyn Burke** (Cornell, 1985) | Irigaray's major collection of essays developing her philosophy of sexual difference against the Lacanian psychoanalytic tradition out of which she emerges and the broader phallocentric tradition of Western philosophy she diagnoses. The title essay's analysis of female sexuality as not reducible to the single, phallic standard of male sexuality is the conceptual core; subsequent essays engage Freud, Plato, and the structural-linguistic tradition. Irigaray's earlier *Speculum of the Other Woman* (1974) is the more systematic doctoral-thesis-based work; *This Sex* is the more accessible companion. Limitation: Irigaray's mode is partly performative — her texts enact a difference in philosophical style as well as describing it — and the dialectical engagement with Lacan is dense for readers without psychoanalytic background. **Recommended translation:** Porter–Burke (Cornell, 1985) — the standard. | Graduate students in feminist philosophy or continental philosophy. Read with Whitford's *Luce Irigaray: Philosophy in the Feminine* (Routledge, 1991). See also SEP: "Luce Irigaray." |
| `[Primary]` Performativity and gender | *Gender Trouble: Feminism and the Subversion of Identity*, 1990 (anniversary ed. 1999, 2007 with new prefaces) `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` `[Evergreen]` | **Judith Butler** | The single most influential work in feminist philosophy of the past forty years: Butler's argument that gender is not the cultural expression of biological sex but the *performative* effect of repeated stylised acts that produce the appearance of an underlying gender identity. The book draws systematically on Foucault, Derrida, and the early feminist tradition (Beauvoir, Wittig, Irigaray) while reformulating their projects in the framework of performativity. The work reshaped feminist philosophy, queer theory, and the cultural studies of identity. Limitation: Butler's prose has been justly criticised as obscure even by continental standards; the 1999 anniversary edition preface is candid about the book's stylistic difficulty and its reception. *Bodies That Matter* (Routledge, 1993) is the major sequel addressing critiques of *Gender Trouble*'s alleged voluntarism. | Required reading in feminist philosophy. See also SEP: "Judith Butler." |

### 9.10 Continental Philosophy — Surveys & Bridges to Analytic Philosophy

| Type & Subdomain | Title, Edition, Year | Author / Translator | Description & Analysis | Target Audience & Characteristics |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| `[Primary]` Analytic-continental bridge — major work `[Bridge]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | *Mind and World*, 1994 (expanded ed. 1996) `[Advanced]` `[Research]` `[Evergreen]` | **John McDowell** | One of the most successful bridge texts in 20th-century philosophy: McDowell's Locke Lectures (1991) develop a position that uses Kantian and Aristotelian-Hegelian resources to address an analytic problem (the question of how experience can rationally justify belief). The argument draws systematically on Kant's *Critique of Pure Reason*, on Sellars's critique of the Myth of the Given (§7.1), on Gadamer's hermeneutics, and on Wittgenstein, while engaging contemporary analytic philosophy of mind and epistemology throughout. The book is among the few that has been productively received in both traditions, and is one of the founding texts of the Pittsburgh school (with Brandom, Pippin, and Rorty's late work). Limitation: short (~150 pp.) but compressed; McDowell's prose is densely allusive and many of the key moves depend on background the reader is presupposed to share. | Required reading for graduate students interested in the analytic-continental bridge. Pair with Brandom's *Making It Explicit* (§5.6) and Pippin's *Hegel's Idealism* (§4.7). See also SEP: "John McDowell." |
| `[Systematic]` Continental philosophy — short introduction `[Self-Study]` | *Continental Philosophy: A Very Short Introduction*, 2001 `[Introductory]` `[Self-Study]` | **Simon Critchley** | The best brief introduction to the continental tradition in English: Critchley addresses both the *what* (the major figures and movements from Kant through poststructuralism) and the *why* (the analytic/continental divide as itself a substantive philosophical and historical question rather than a stylistic preference). Compact (~140 pp.) and unusually fair to both traditions. Limitation: brief — chapter-length treatments of figures who in many cases deserve full books. The book is the entry point, not the destination. | First reading on the continental tradition for analytic-trained readers; orientation for general readers. |
| `[Systematic]` The analytic/continental divide — meta-question `[Bridge]` `[Self-Study]` | *The Idea of Continental Philosophy: A Philosophical Chronicle*, 2006 `[Advanced]` `[Self-Study]` | **Simon Glendinning** | Glendinning's substantive engagement with the question of what continental philosophy is — or whether the very category is intelligible. He argues that the analytic/continental divide is a construction of analytic philosophy in particular historical moments rather than a description of two genuinely separate traditions, and that "continental philosophy" is best understood through its representative texts rather than through any abstract definition. The book is one of the most philosophically serious recent treatments of the meta-question. Limitation: methodological-historiographical rather than substantive-philosophical; readers wanting to read continental philosophy itself should consult the primary texts in §§9.1–9.9 first. | Graduate students reflecting on the structure of contemporary philosophy. Read alongside Glock's *What Is Analytic Philosophy?* (Cambridge, 2008 — see §1.2) for the symmetrical question. |

### Connections to other tables

This table is in continuous dialogue with most other tables in the atlas. The phenomenological tradition (§§9.1–9.5) presupposes Kant (Table 4) and Hegel (§4.7), and Heidegger's later work engages systematically with Aristotle (§2.4), Plato (§2.2), the Pre-Socratics (§2.1), Descartes (§3.1), and Nietzsche (§4.10). Husserl's *Logical Investigations* engages directly with the same problems in philosophy of logic and mathematics treated by Frege (§5.3). The §7.7 bridge texts (Varela–Thompson–Rosch, Dreyfus, Noë, Thompson) presuppose §9.1 (Husserl), §9.2 (Heidegger), and §9.4 (Merleau-Ponty); the embodied cognition movement *is* the major contemporary analytic-phenomenological bridge. Habermas (§9.7) connects to §5.6 (Austin and speech act theory), §6.3 (epistemology), and §8.10 (democratic theory — *Between Facts and Norms* is in §8.10 and continues the project of *Theory of Communicative Action*). The Frankfurt School genealogy depends on Marx (§4.9) and Hegel (§4.7); contemporary critical theory (Honneth, Forst) connects to Rawls (§8.5). Foucault's three periods (§9.8) connect respectively to the history of the human sciences (the archaeological), to Nietzsche's *Genealogy* (§4.10, the genealogical), and to Hadot's reading of ancient ethics (§§1.5, 2.6, the ethics of the self). Derrida engages systematically with Husserl, Plato, Rousseau, Hegel, and Saussure. Beauvoir's *The Second Sex* (§9.9) presupposes Sartre (§9.5) and Hegel's master-slave dialectic (§4.7); Butler's *Gender Trouble* connects to §8.11 (feminist political philosophy). McDowell's *Mind and World* (§9.10) connects Kant (§4.1), Sellars (§7.1), Gadamer (§9.6), and contemporary analytic epistemology (§6.1).

## Coda — Reading Sequences

A few cross-table reading sequences are worth flagging explicitly. For *Kantianism*: §4.1 (CPR) → §4.3 (practical philosophy) → §8.3 (contemporary Kantian ethics) → §8.7 Korsgaard. For *phenomenology and cognitive science*: §9.1 Husserl → §9.2 Heidegger → §9.4 Merleau-Ponty → §7.7 embodied cognition → §7.3 hard problem. For *the Lewisian metaphysical programme*: §5.4 Lewis on semantics → §6.7 modal realism → §6.9 causation → §6.10 natural properties → §5.5 formal semantics. For *the post-Kantian historical sequence*: §4.1 Kant → §4.7 Hegel → §4.9 Marx → §4.10 Nietzsche → §9.7 Frankfurt School → §9.8 Foucault. For *the contemporary normative-political mainstream*: §8.5 Rawls → §8.7 metaethics → §8.9 the Nozick–Cohen debate → §8.10 Habermas and Estlund → §8.11 Pateman, Mills.

The atlas as a whole is intended to be entered at any point and traversed in any direction; the table-by-table sequence presented here is one route through the material, not the only one. The *Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy* should be open throughout.
